Foreword
The Inquiry’s Terms of Reference required me to consider the extent of <PERSON>Harold Fredrick
Shipman</PERSON>’s unlawful activities but left it to me to decide how this should be done. Within a very
short time, I came to realise that the only way in which to satisfy the reasonable expectations of
the families and friends of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s former patients was to provide, so far as I could, a
decision in each individual case in which suspicion might arise. The work of investigation and
decision writing proved to be far greater than I had anticipated and has taken longer than I had
hoped. I believe that this First Report provides as complete and accurate an account of
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s criminality as it will ever be possible to give.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> breached the trust of his patients and of the communities in which he lived. He has
caused unimaginable grief and distress. No one who reads this Report can fail to be deeply
shocked. I would like to express my profound sympathy to those who have been bereaved by
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s actions and also to those whose trust has been so callously betrayed.
I wish to thank the many witnesses who have enabled the Inquiry to carry out its task. I know
that, for some, the experience of providing evidence, whether orally or in writing, has been a
painful one.
Although the investigation of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s crimes has at times been harrowing, there is one
particular respect in which it has been positively heart-warming. I want to express my
admiration and respect for the way in which the people of <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> and <PERSON>Todmorden</PERSON> care so
affectionately for their relatives and neighbours. I have heard and read of countless families
where a son or daughter, son-in-law or daughter-in-law, niece, nephew or grandchild cared
devotedly for an elderly relation, sometimes visiting several times a day, while looking after his
or her own family and children and often coping with a job. I have heard many accounts of
kindness by neighbours.
The completion of this First Report is an important landmark in the life of the Inquiry. It provides
me with the opportunity to thank the Inquiry staff, who have worked unremittingly hard during
the last 18 months. I am grateful to the administrative team, led by <PERSON>Andrew Griffiths</PERSON> and
strongly supported by <PERSON>Oonagh McIntosh</PERSON> and <PERSON>Helen Owen</PERSON>. I express my admiration for, and
gratitude to, <PERSON>Henry Palin</PERSON>, who, together with <PERSON>Ita Langan</PERSON>, recruited and managed a team of
solicitors and paralegals. I thank the IT experts, in particular <PERSON>Michael Taylor</PERSON>, who have enabled
us to cope with an enormous quantity of information without drowning in a sea of paper. I am
grateful to Dr <PERSON>Aneez Esmail</PERSON>, the Inquiry’s Medical Advisor, whose help with medical issues has
been invaluable. Finally, I must mention the enormous assistance I have received from <PERSON>Caroline
Swift</PERSON> QC, <PERSON>Christopher Melton</PERSON> QC, <PERSON>Anthony Mazzag</PERSON> and <PERSON>Michael Jones</PERSON>, without whose
indefatigable industry and considerable talents my task would have been impossible.
<PERSON>Janet Smith</PERSON>
July 2002

SUMMARY
1. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> entered general practice in early 1974, when he joined the <ORGANIZATION>Abraham Ormerod
Medical Practice</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>. He remained there until September 1975, when his
partners discovered that he had been dishonestly obtaining controlled drugs for his
own use.
2. In February 1976, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> pleaded guilty at the <ORGANIZATION>Halifax Magistrates’ Court</ORGANIZATION> to three
offences of obtaining pethidine by deception, three offences of unlawful possession of
pethidine and two further offences of forging a prescription. He asked for 74 similar
offences to be taken into consideration. He was ordered to pay a fine and
compensation.
3. The fact of his convictions was reported to the <ORGANIZATION>General Medical Council</ORGANIZATION>, which decided
to take no disciplinary action against him. The <ORGANIZATION>Home Office</ORGANIZATION> imposed no prohibition on
his future dealings with controlled drugs. He was, therefore, free to continue practising
as a doctor without limitation or supervision.
4. In October 1977, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> joined the seven doctor <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook</ORGANIZATION> practice in <LOCATION>Hyde</LOCATION>. He
remained there until January 1992, when he began to practice single-handed from
within the same building.
5. In August 1992, he moved to new surgery premises at 21 <LOCATION>Market Street</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>, where he
continued to work as a single-handed practitioner until his arrest in September 1998.
6. Throughout his career as a general practitioner, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> enjoyed a high level of
respect within the communities in which he worked. In <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>, he was extremely popular
with his patients, particularly his elderly patients, and was regarded by many as ‘ the
best doctor in <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>’.
7. In July 1998, the <LOCATION>Greater Manchester</LOCATION> Police began an investigation into the death of one
of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patients, <PERSON>Mrs Kathleen Grundy</PERSON>. That investigation was rapidly widened to
include the deaths of many other patients of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>.
8. On 7th September 1998, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was arrested, interviewed and charged with the
murder of <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> and with other offences associated with the forgery of her will,
under which he was to be the sole beneficiary of her estate. He was subsequently
suspended from practice and charged with 14 further murders.
9. On 31st January 2000, following a lengthy trial, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was convicted of 15 counts of
murder and one of forging <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s will. He was sentenced to 15 terms of life
imprisonment and, for the forgery, a concurrent term of four years’ imprisonment. The
trial judge said that his recommendation to the Home Secretary would be that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
should spend the remainder of his days in prison. Following the criminal trial, the
Director of Public Prosecutions announced that no further criminal proceedings would
be instituted against <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>.
10. Subsequently, the <ORGANIZATION>Professional Conduct Committee</ORGANIZATION> of the <ORGANIZATION>General Medical Council</ORGANIZATION>
erased <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s name from the medical register. 
2
The Shipman Inquiry
11. Before the trial and subsequently, the police investigated a large number of deaths of
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patients where there was evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had been responsible for
the death. Those deaths were reported to the South Manchester Coroner, Mr <PERSON>John
Pollard</PERSON>. Between August 2000 and April 2001, he conducted inquests into 27 deaths of
patients of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, recording verdicts of unlawful killing in 25 cases and open verdicts
in the remaining two. On 18th May 2001, the Coroner opened inquests into a further 232
deaths; those inquests were immediately adjourned on the direction of the Lord
Chancellor, pending publication of the findings of this Inquiry.
12. On 31st January 2001, following resolutions of both Houses of <ORGANIZATION>Parliament</ORGANIZATION>, the Secretary
of <ORGANIZATION>State for Health</ORGANIZATION> issued the instrument of appointment establishing The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman
Inquiry</ORGANIZATION>, giving it the powers conferred by the Tribunals of Inquiry (Evidence) Act 1921
and appointing me as Chairman of the Inquiry.
13. The first of the Inquiry’s Terms of Reference requires it, ‘ after receiving the existing
evidence and hearing such further evidence as necessary, to consider the extent of…
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s unlawful activities’. In this, the Inquiry’s First Report, I set out my findings as
to how many of his patients <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed, the means employed and the period over
which the killings took place.
14. Volumes Two to Six of this Report contain my written decisions in 494 cases – 493
deaths and one incident involving a living person. Those decisions are based on an
enormous volume of evidence, which has been gathered by the Inquiry team.
15. I have found that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed 215 of his patients. The first, Mrs <PERSON>Eva Lyons</PERSON>, was killed
in March 1975, when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was practising in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>, and the last, <PERSON>Mrs Kathleen
Grundy</PERSON>, died in June 1998.
16. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s usual method of killing was by the administration of a lethal dose of an
opiate, most frequently diamorphine. There is some evidence that he may have killed a
few patients by the administration of large doses of a sedative. There is no reliable
evidence that he killed by any means other than the administration of a drug.
17. Of the 215 killings, one took place in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>, 71 during <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s time at the
<ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook</ORGANIZATION> practice and the remaining 143 during his six years at the Market Street
Surgery. While at the Market Street Surgery, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed one patient in 1992,
16 patients in 1993 and 11 in 1994. In each of the years 1995 and 1996, he killed
30 patients, increasing to 37 in 1997. During the first three months of 1998, he killed
15 patients, after which there was an interval of about seven weeks; he went on to kill a
further three patients before his arrest in September 1998.
18. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s oldest victim, Miss <PERSON>Ann Cooper</PERSON>, was 93 years old when she was killed. The
majority of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims were elderly but he did, on occasions, kill younger people.
Mr <PERSON>Peter Lewis</PERSON> died at the age of only 41; he was the youngest of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patients to
die at his hands. Mr <PERSON>Lewis</PERSON> was in the advanced stage of a terminal illness and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
hastened his death. The youngest of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims to suffer an unexpected death
was Mr <PERSON>David Harrison</PERSON>, who was 47 years old when he died.
3
19. Of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s 215 victims, 171 were women and 44 were men. In general, women live
longer than men, so that there are more elderly women than elderly men living alone.
Since <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s typical victim was an elderly person living alone, he found most of his
potential victims among his female patients. However, he also killed men when the
opportunity presented itself.
20. Whilst the majority of the deaths for which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was responsible occurred while he
was working as a single-handed practitioner, it is nevertheless clear that, even
while working in a multi-handed practice, he was able to kill undetected over a period
of many years.
21. I have found that 210 of the deaths investigated by the Inquiry team occurred as the
result of natural causes and not by reason of any action on the part of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. I hope
that the families concerned with these cases will be reassured by my finding that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was not responsible for their relative’s death.
22. There are 45 deaths for which I have found that a real suspicion arises that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
may have been responsible, although the evidence is not sufficiently clear for me to
reach a positive conclusion that he was. In addition, there are a further 38 deaths in
respect of which there was so little evidence, or evidence of such poor quality, that I was
unable to form any view at all. These are mainly deaths dating from the early years,
where little documentary or witness evidence survives. I regret that the families
concerned with these deaths are left in a state of uncertainty, but it was inevitable that
there would be some cases where the evidence would not permit me to reach a positive
conclusion one way or the other. I can only hope that it will be of some comfort to the
relatives at least to know that the circumstances of each death have been investigated
as fully as possible.
23. In all, the Inquiry has examined 888 cases; I have given a written decision in 494
(493 deaths and one incident involving a living person) of those cases. In the remaining
394 cases, there was compelling evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was not responsible for the
death. The Inquiry legal team therefore closed the files in those cases, without the
necessity for a written decision. In all but the most straightforward cases, I examined the
file and confirmed the decision to close it.
24. Professor <PERSON>Richard Baker OBE</PERSON>, Professor of Quality in Health Care at the <ORGANIZATION>University of
Leicester</ORGANIZATION>, conducted a review of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s clinical practice, which was published in
January 2001. He carried out a number of analyses of the estimated excess of deaths
among <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patients during his career as a general practitioner. He estimated that
the true number of excess deaths lay between 198 and 277 and concluded that an
excess of 236 deaths was ‘ most likely to reflect the true number of deaths about which
there should be concern’.
25. Having considered my findings, Professor <PERSON>Baker</PERSON> has concluded that they support the
conclusion that the excess of deaths is in the region of 220 to 240, i.e. very close to his
own figure of 236. Here, Professor <PERSON>Baker</PERSON> is taking into account, not only the 215 deaths
which I have found that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> caused, but also some of the deaths about which I was
unable to reach a positive conclusion but where I found that there was a real suspicion
4
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry</ORGANIZATION>
that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was responsible. It is inevitable that that group of deaths will, in fact,
contain some killings. The striking compatibility between the results of Professor <PERSON>Baker</PERSON>’s
previous review and my own findings strongly suggests that the conclusions of the
Inquiry and of the review are very likely to be correct.
26. All but three of the deaths for which I have found that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was responsible were
entered in the register of deaths in reliance upon Medical Certificates of Cause of Death
completed by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. The majority of those deaths were followed by cremation.
Before a cremation can be authorised, a second doctor must confirm the cause of death
and the cremation documentation must be checked by a third doctor employed at the
crematorium. These procedures are intended to provide a safeguard for the public
against concealment of the fact that a person has been unlawfully killed. Yet, even with
those procedures in place, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was able to kill 215 people without detection. It is
clear therefore that, in reality, the procedures provided no safeguard at all. Why that
was, and what steps should be taken to devise a system which will afford the public a
proper degree of protection in the future, are issues which the Inquiry will consider
during Phase Two.
27. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patients frequently died suddenly at home, without any previous history of
terminal or life-threatening illness. Such deaths should be reported to the coroner. Yet
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> managed to avoid a referral to the coroner in all but a very few cases in which
he had killed. He did this by claiming to be able to diagnose – and, therefore, to certify –
the cause of death and by persuading relatives that there was no need for a postmortem
examination. In Phase Two, the Inquiry will consider measures which can be
taken to ensure that all unexpected or unexplained deaths are reported and their cause
properly investigated.
28. After <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s convictions for drugs offences in 1976, he declared his intention never
to carry controlled drugs again. Accordingly, he was not obliged to have a controlled
drugs register. Yet he was able, by a number of different methods, to obtain large
quantities of controlled drugs; in 1996, he prescribed and obtained in the name of a
dying patient as much as 12,000mg diamorphine on a single occasion. That alone
would have been sufficient to kill about 360 people. Despite the fact that the possession
and supply of such drugs is said to be ‘ controlled’, those controls did not prevent
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> from acquiring large amounts of diamorphine without detection. How that
could happen, and what measures should be taken to strengthen the system of
controlling access to such drugs, are matters which will also be considered by the
Inquiry in Phase Two.
29. Professor <PERSON>Baker</PERSON> has observed that one implication of the high number of patients killed
by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> is that an effective system of monitoring the death rates of patients of
general practitioners would have detected the excess number of deaths. No such
system was in place during <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s time in general practice. In Phase Two, the
Inquiry will seek to identify effective systems for monitoring death rates, and will
consider other possible improvements to the arrangements for monitoring general
practitioners and ways of encouraging those genuinely concerned about possible
5
misconduct on the part of doctors to express their concerns to those in a position
properly to investigate and evaluate them.
30. No one reading this Report can fail to be shocked by the enormity of the crimes
committed by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> and to feel, as I do, the deepest sympathy for his victims and
their families. His activities have brought tragedy upon them and also upon the
communities in which he practised and which gave him their trust.
31. In its first Phase, the Inquiry has determined the extent of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s criminality. We shall
now direct our efforts to attempting to devise improved systems so as to ensure that
such a terrible betrayal of trust by a family doctor can never happen again.
6
The Shipman Inquiry

CHAPTER ONE
Before the Inquiry
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s Professional Career: Training
1.1 In September 1965, <PERSON>Harold Fredrick</PERSON>*
 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> entered the <ORGANIZATION>University of Leeds School of
Medicine</ORGANIZATION> at the age of 19 years. He spent five years there, training for his future medical
career. During that time, he married and the couple’s first child was born. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> left
<ORGANIZATION>Leeds</ORGANIZATION> in 1970, having gained the qualification MB ChB.
1.2 From <LOCATION>Leeds</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> moved to <LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION>, where he was employed for 12 months as a
pre-registration house officer at the <LOCATION>Pontefract General Infirmary</LOCATION>, before being fully
registered with the <ORGANIZATION>General Medical Council</ORGANIZATION> (GMC) in August 1971. Thereafter,
he continued to work at the same hospital as a senior house officer, gaining a
diploma in child health (DCH) in 1972 and a diploma in obstetrics and gynaecology
(DRCOG) in 1974.
The Move to <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>
1.3 In the early part of 1974, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> answered an advertisement in a medical publication
and, after an interview, secured a position in a busy general practice operating from the
<LOCATION>Abraham Ormerod Medical Centre</LOCATION> in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>, a town in the <LOCATION>Pennines</LOCATION> on the
Lancashire/Yorkshire border. After a short probationary period as an assistant general
practitioner, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> became a junior partner in the practice, with a view to becoming
an equal partner in due course.
1.4 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> impressed his partners with his competence, enthusiasm and capacity for
hard work. He was popular with patients. He persuaded his partners to adopt a more
modern system of categorising data contained in patient records and himself undertook
much of the work required to change to the new system. Another task that he undertook
was the disposal of a quantity of out-of-date controlled drugs which were stored in
the surgery’s controlled drugs cabinet. It also seems that he assumed responsibility for
re-stocking the cabinet and, on occasion, for ordering stocks of controlled drugs for use
by members of the practice.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s Abuse of Pethidine
1.5 By February 1975, the <ORGANIZATION>Home Office Drugs Inspectorate</ORGANIZATION> and the <ORGANIZATION>West Yorkshire Police
Drugs Squad</ORGANIZATION> had become aware that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was obtaining abnormally large
quantities of pethidine from local pharmacies. Their discussions with the pharmacists
concerned were reassuring; <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was held in high esteem by them and was
described as ‘ very efficient and confident’. The police report written at the time
concluded:
‘ It would seem from the enquiries made into this matter that there is no
drug abuse by Dr <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>.
* <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> himself uses the spelling ‘ <PERSON>Fredrick</PERSON>’; see, for example, page CO 76 04038 of the scanned documents.
8
The Shipman Inquiry
A watch will be maintained and should anything further come to light
then a further report will be submitted’.
1.6 In early June 1975, it was noticed that a local pharmaceutical company was regularly
supplying to the pharmacy at Boots the Chemists in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION> abnormally large
amounts of pethidine for injection. Those amounts were accounted for by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
written orders on behalf of the practice and by prescriptions for the drug issued by him.
As a consequence of this discovery, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was interviewed by two <ORGANIZATION>Home Office</ORGANIZATION>
drugs inspectors and a detective constable from the <ORGANIZATION>West Yorkshire Police</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
offered ready explanations for the amount of pethidine he had obtained and denied that
he was abusing the drug. However, deficiencies were found in the controlled drugs
documentation held by the practice; in particular, there was no register recording the
supply of pethidine to patients from the surgery stocks, as required by law. It appeared
to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s interviewers that some of the ampoules of pethidine, which he had
obtained on written requisition for the practice, were unaccounted for but, without a
register of supplies, this could not be confirmed.
1.7 Because of the deficiencies in procedures which had been revealed at the interview, a
<ORGANIZATION>Home Office</ORGANIZATION> drugs inspector, Mr <PERSON>Donald McIntosh</PERSON>, who has since died, visited the
practice in early August 1975, saw all the partners, including <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, and advised
them on the institution of a controlled drugs supply register and the correct procedure
for destroying controlled drugs. No further action was taken at that stage, although
Mr <PERSON>McIntosh</PERSON> expressed his intention of keeping the case under review. He requested
from the police a further report in about six months’ time, giving details of all controlled
drugs obtained by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> over that period. In the event, that report was never
prepared, having been overtaken by events.
1.8 Meanwhile, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was experiencing problems with his health. In May 1975, one of
his partners, Dr <PERSON>John Dacre</PERSON>, was called out by <PERSON>Mrs Primrose Shipman</PERSON>, after her
husband had fallen in the bathroom and struck his head. Dr <PERSON>Dacre</PERSON> diagnosed
concussion and referred <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to the casualty department of one of the local
hospitals. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s partners recall other occasions when he suffered ‘ blackouts’ or
‘ seizures’; one occurred in the practice car park and another partner, Dr <PERSON>David Bunn</PERSON>,
remembers assisting him on that occasion. <PERSON>Mrs Shipman</PERSON> gave evidence about an
incident when her husband blacked out beside her while she was driving the family car
with him as a passenger. According to another of his partners, Dr <PERSON>Michael Grieve</PERSON>,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> suffered several blackouts in front of patients in the surgery waiting room.
1.9 Because of his blackouts, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was referred to Dr <PERSON>Philip Humberstone</PERSON>, a consultant
physician at the <ORGANIZATION>Halifax Royal Infirmary</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was seen there on 18th August 1975
and it seems that a diagnosis of idiopathic epilepsy (i.e. epilepsy of unidentified origin)
was made.
1.10 At some time during 1975, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was either advised, or himself decided, to stop
driving and, from that time, he relied on his wife to drive him when he visited patients at
their homes. It is not known precisely when this arrangement began. <PERSON>Mrs Shipman</PERSON>’s
recollection was that the blackouts began ‘ not many months’ before they left <ORGANIZATION>Todmorden</ORGANIZATION>
9
in late 1975. She thought that she had been driving him around for only a matter of
weeks before he ceased to practise in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>.
Discovery
1.11 In late September 1975, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s partners discovered that he was abusing pethidine
and had been obtaining the drug illicitly to feed his habit. He had obtained large
quantities of pethidine on written requisition, ostensibly for practice use; these quantities
could not be accounted for and it was plain that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had taken them for his own
use. His partners confronted <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, who admitted that he was abusing pethidine
and, after unsuccessfully trying to persuade his colleagues to assist him in continuing to
obtain supplies of the drug illegally, tendered his resignation from the practice. Although
he later withdrew that resignation, his partners took legal advice and eventually
succeeded in dismissing him from the practice.
1.12 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was immediately admitted to the <ORGANIZATION>Halifax Royal Infirmary</ORGANIZATION> under the care of
Dr <PERSON>Humberstone</PERSON>, who quickly referred him to a consultant psychiatrist, Dr <PERSON>Hugo Milne</PERSON>.
Dr <PERSON>Milne</PERSON> arranged for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s voluntary admission to The Retreat, a private hospital
in <LOCATION>York</LOCATION>, specialising in the treatment of psychiatric disorders. There, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was
placed under the care of Dr <PERSON>R W Bryson</PERSON>, consultant psychiatrist. Both psychiatrists later
notified the <ORGANIZATION>Home Office</ORGANIZATION> that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> should be registered as a drug addict.
1.13 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was successfully withdrawn from pethidine, following which he was diagnosed
as suffering from a moderately severe depressive or melancholic state. He was treated
with antidepressant medication, which appeared to effect a great improvement in his
condition. He was discharged from The Retreat on 30th December 1975, with advice to
continue under psychiatric supervision for several years.
Criminal Proceedings
1.14 Meanwhile, the latest developments had been immediately notified by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
partners to the <ORGANIZATION>Home Office Drugs Inspectorate</ORGANIZATION> which, in turn, had informed the police.
On 28th November 1975, <PERSON>Mr McIntosh</PERSON>, together with Detective Sergeant <PERSON>George
McKeating</PERSON>, from the <ORGANIZATION>West Yorkshire Police Drugs Squad</ORGANIZATION>, interviewed <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> at The
Retreat. Initially, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> refused to speak to the police officer but quickly changed his
mind and gave what his interviewers took to be a full account of his criminal activities.
He admitted using a variety of deceptions to obtain pethidine for his own consumption
which he claimed had risen, by the time of his discovery, to 600 to 700mg a day. He
said that he had started taking pethidine about 18 months previously (that is in about
May 1974) when he became depressed because he did not get on with his partners.
It should be said that his partners do not agree that there was any friction within the
practice; they were never asked for their response to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s assertion and, indeed,
say that they were unaware that he was to be prosecuted until they read about the court
proceedings in the local newspaper.
1.15 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> then made a detailed written statement, setting out his account of what had
occurred. In the course of that statement, he wrote:
10
The Shipman Inquiry
‘ I have no future intention to return to General Practice or work in a
situation where I could obtain supplies of pethidine’.
1.16 On 13th February 1976, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> appeared at the <ORGANIZATION>Halifax Magistrates’ Court</ORGANIZATION>, where he
pleaded guilty to eight specimen charges: three offences of obtaining ten ampoules of
100mg pethidine by deception, three of unlawfully possessing pethidine and two of
forging a prescription. He asked for 74 further offences to be taken into consideration.
Unfortunately, no list of those further offences survives; the police and court files, which
would have contained such a list, have now been destroyed. However, it is clear from
contemporaneous press reports that 67 of the 74 offences concerned the obtaining of
pethidine by deception. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was fined £75 on each charge, £600 in all, and
ordered to pay compensation of £58.78 to the <ORGANIZATION>NHS Family Practitioners Committee</ORGANIZATION>.
A New Job
1.17 By the time of his conviction, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had already started a new job. On 2nd February
1976, he had commenced employment with the <ORGANIZATION>Durham Area Health Authority</ORGANIZATION> as a
clinical medical officer at the <LOCATION>Newton Aycliffe Health Centre</LOCATION>. He told his prospective
employers of his previous problem with drugs and of the fact that he was facing criminal
proceedings and possible disciplinary action by the GMC. Having discussed his case
with the psychiatrists who had been treating him, the <ORGANIZATION>Health Authority</ORGANIZATION> offered him the
post on condition that he continue to have follow-up care from a psychiatrist. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
had no access to controlled drugs in the course of his new employment.
1.18 Meanwhile, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s health problems had resolved and he had ceased to suffer the
blackouts or seizures which had affected him during his time in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>. With
hindsight, it is clear that those episodes were a product of his pethidine abuse, rather
than a manifestation of epilepsy. His wife believes that he began to drive again in
about March 1976. He was still under the care of Dr <PERSON>Milne</PERSON> in April 1976 but it is not
known how much longer this psychiatric supervision continued, as Dr <PERSON>Milne</PERSON>’s records
have not survived.
Possible Disciplinary Proceedings
1.19 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s convictions at the Magistrates’ Court were reported to the <ORGANIZATION>GMC</ORGANIZATION>, which then
had to decide whether to take disciplinary action against him to remove or restrict his
registration as a doctor. Following the procedure then in force, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s case was
automatically referred to the <ORGANIZATION>Penal Cases Committee</ORGANIZATION>, whose task it was to decide, on
the basis of written evidence and submissions, whether the case should be referred for
inquiry to the <ORGANIZATION>GMC Disciplinary Committee</ORGANIZATION>.
1.20 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s case came before the <ORGANIZATION>Penal Cases Committee</ORGANIZATION>, which had before it reports
from Dr <PERSON>Bryson</PERSON> and Dr <PERSON>Milne</PERSON>, the consultant psychiatrists who had treated <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>.
There was also a letter of support from Dr <PERSON>Michael O’Brien</PERSON>, Area Medical Officer of the
<ORGANIZATION>Durham Area Health Authority</ORGANIZATION>; the letter stated that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> ‘ had settled well into his
new employment’ and was ‘ well received by both patients and professional colleagues
alike’, with no evidence to suggest any recurrence of ‘ his former difficulties’.
11
1.21 On 28th April 1976, the <ORGANIZATION>Penal Cases Committee</ORGANIZATION> of the <ORGANIZATION>GMC</ORGANIZATION> determined that no inquiry
into <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s case should be held by the <ORGANIZATION>Disciplinary Committee</ORGANIZATION> and that the case
could, therefore, be concluded. Subsequently, a letter was sent to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, part of
which reads as follows:
‘ The <ORGANIZATION>Committee</ORGANIZATION> instructed me to inform you that they take a grave view
of offences arising out of an abuse of drugs and of offences involving
dishonesty… You would therefore be wise to assume that, if
information relating to any further conviction of a similar nature should
be received by the <ORGANIZATION>Council</ORGANIZATION>, a charge would then be formulated against
you on the basis of both the earlier and the later convictions and
referred to the <ORGANIZATION>Disciplinary Committee of the Council</ORGANIZATION> for inquiry’.
The <ORGANIZATION>GMC</ORGANIZATION> informed the <ORGANIZATION>Home Office</ORGANIZATION> of its decision by a letter dated 3rd May 1976.
1.22 Following <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s conviction for drugs offences under the Misuse of Drugs Act 1971,
the Home Secretary had power under that Act to make a direction pursuant to section
12, prohibiting <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> from having in his possession, prescribing, administering or
otherwise dealing with such controlled drugs as were specified in the direction. In the
event, the <ORGANIZATION>Home Office</ORGANIZATION> officials who dealt with the case decided that no such direction
should be given. In reaching that decision, they appear to have been influenced by the
view expressed by the police that there was no evidence that any of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patients
had suffered as a result of his obtaining of pethidine and also by the decision of the
<ORGANIZATION>GMC</ORGANIZATION> not to take disciplinary proceedings against <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>.
The Move to <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook</ORGANIZATION>
1.23 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was, therefore, free to pursue his medical career when and where he chose.
In 1977, he responded to an advertisement which had been placed in a medical
publication by a seven doctor practice in <LOCATION>Hyde</LOCATION>, a former mill town which has, over the
years, been subsumed into the <LOCATION>Greater Manchester</LOCATION> conurbation. The <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook</ORGANIZATION>
practice was seeking a new doctor to replace one who was leaving to work in industry.
At interview, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told members of the practice about his previous abuse of
pethidine and his convictions. He referred them to one of the psychiatrists who had
treated him and who would be able to give them details about his condition.
1.24 One of the doctors at the practice spoke to the psychiatrist named by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> (it is not
clear whether this was Dr <PERSON>Milne</PERSON> or Dr <PERSON>Bryson</PERSON>, although it seems likely to have been the
latter) and also to officials at the <ORGANIZATION>GMC</ORGANIZATION> and the <ORGANIZATION>Home Office</ORGANIZATION>. He was assured by the
psychiatrist that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was not, in his opinion, suffering from any mental health
problems which would interfere with his work as a general practitioner and he was
informed (correctly) by the <ORGANIZATION>GMC</ORGANIZATION> and the Home Office that there were no restrictions in
force which would affect <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s use of controlled drugs. Once that information had
been obtained, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was invited to join the practice, starting on 1st October 1977.
1.25 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> stayed at the <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook</ORGANIZATION> practice for over 14 years. He was hard-working,
apparently dedicated and popular with his patients. He was active in introducing new
ideas to the practice and also became involved in organisations outside the practice.
12
The Shipman Inquiry
For several years, he was an area surgeon for the local <ORGANIZATION>St John Ambulance</ORGANIZATION>; he was a
member of the (then) <ORGANIZATION>Family Practitioners Committee</ORGANIZATION> and, later, secretary of the
<ORGANIZATION>Tameside Local Medical Committee</ORGANIZATION>.
1.26 In 1991, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told his colleagues at <LOCATION>Donneybrook</LOCATION> that he was intending to leave the
practice; the ostensible reasons for this were <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s dislike of the computer system,
which had been introduced in 1989 to record patient details, and his disagreement
with the proposed scheme of fundholding. With hindsight, these stated reasons for
his departure make little sense since, once in his own practice, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> embraced
enthusiastically the use of computers and became chairman of the local users’ group for
Micro-Doc, a software system developed especially for doctors. Moreover, in 1995, he
joined the <ORGANIZATION>Tameside Consortium</ORGANIZATION> (South) for the specific purpose of fundholding.
It seems that, even at the time, at least some members of the <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook</ORGANIZATION> practice
believed that he might have had other reasons for leaving. One describes <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> as
tending to be ‘ individualistic’ in his approach and says that he ‘ could become irritated
if confronted by any other of the doctors and other staff members’. According to him,
the assumption at the time was that these features of his personality might have led
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to prefer single-handed practice. At his trial, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that he left
because the other doctors were not as committed to fundholding as he was. It seems
also that, towards the end of his time at the <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook</ORGANIZATION> practice, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
relationships with at least one member of the staff there had deteriorated badly.
The Market Street Surgery
1.27 Whatever the real reasons for his move, from 1st January 1992, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> ran a singlehanded
practice from within <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook House</ORGANIZATION> until his new surgery was ready in
August 1992. He then moved to premises at 21 Market Street, <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>. He took with him
several members of staff from the <LOCATION>Donneybrook</LOCATION> practice and, to the annoyance and
financial detriment of his former partners, his patient list. The parting was acrimonious
and was followed by lengthy negotiations between solicitors to settle the financial
arrangements consequent upon <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s departure.
1.28 For the next six years, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s practice appeared to flourish. He enjoyed a high
reputation in <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> as an attentive, caring doctor. A major reason for his popularity was
his willingness to visit his elderly patients at home. One witness described his mother’s
delight at being accepted onto <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patient list; it was, he said, ‘ as though she
had won the lottery’. Many elderly people were persuaded to join <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s practice by
friends or family members who were impressed by the quality of the care which they
received from him. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not have space on his patient list to accommodate all
those who wished to join it and, by the time of his arrest, he was actively attempting to
recruit a partner to share his workload and enable the practice to take on more patients.
1.29 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> and his staff performed regular medical audits, which impressed the <ORGANIZATION>Health
Authority’s Audit Group</ORGANIZATION>, and the practice was generally regarded as being innovative
and advanced. Some indication of the high esteem in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was held emerges
from a <ORGANIZATION>Health Authority</ORGANIZATION> document dating from late December 1997. An issue had arisen
about access to patient records and the <ORGANIZATION>Health Authority</ORGANIZATION>’s solicitor had advised
13
Ms <PERSON>Andrea Horsfall</PERSON>, the deputy complaints manager, to contact a local general
practitioner and ascertain whether he or she was aware of recent guidance issued by
the <ORGANIZATION>British Medical Association</ORGANIZATION>. Ms <PERSON>Horsfall</PERSON> spoke to Dr <PERSON>Alan Banks</PERSON>, then Assistant
Director of <ORGANIZATION>Primary Care and Medical Adviser</ORGANIZATION> to the <ORGANIZATION>Health Authority</ORGANIZATION>. Her note of that
conversation records:
‘ Asked A. Banks which GP I should ring. He suggested Dr <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> as
he is apparantly (sic) very uptodate (sic) on all the latest information/
advice’.
1.30 In addition, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was active in local medical politics and an enthusiastic member,
latterly treasurer, of the local branch of the <ORGANIZATION>Small Practices Association</ORGANIZATION>. Although there
were people who regarded him as arrogant, sometimes overbearing, the majority of his
patients, his staff and other professionals with whom he came into contact appear to
have held him in high esteem and to have believed that the health and welfare of his
patients were his main priority. When giving evidence to the Inquiry in May 2002,
Mr <PERSON>Nigel Reynolds</PERSON>, widower of the late Dr <PERSON>Linda Reynolds</PERSON>, observed that, in 1998,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was quite simply perceived as ‘ the best doctor in <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>’.
The Police Investigation of March 1998
1.31 By March of that year, however, certain people in <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> had begun to feel concern at the
number of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s elderly patients who were dying in curiously similar
circumstances. After discussion with her colleagues, <PERSON>Dr Reynolds</PERSON>, a partner in the
nearby <LOCATION>Brooke Practice</LOCATION>, alerted the Coroner for the <LOCATION>Greater Manchester South District</LOCATION>
(‘ the <LOCATION>South Manchester</LOCATION> Coroner’), Mr <PERSON>John Pollard</PERSON>, to the concerns felt by herself and
others. Mr <PERSON>Pollard</PERSON> initiated a limited police investigation, during which the police sought
the assistance of the <ORGANIZATION>West Pennine Health Authority</ORGANIZATION>. At the conclusion of that
investigation, the police officer who conducted it, Detective Inspector <PERSON>David Smith</PERSON>,
decided that there was no evidence to substantiate the concerns which had been
expressed by Dr <PERSON>Reynolds</PERSON> to the Coroner. No further action was, therefore, taken.
1.32 The conduct of the March 1998 investigation and its outcome have been fully examined
by the Inquiry in the course of oral hearings held between May and July 2002.
The Inquiry’s findings as to the adequacy of the investigation will be published in
due course.
The Death of Mrs <PERSON>Kathleen Grundy</PERSON>
1.33 Mrs <PERSON>Kathleen Grundy</PERSON> died on 24th June 1998. She was <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patient and he
certified the cause of her death as ‘ old age’. Despite her 81 years, <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> had
enjoyed good health and her death was sudden and unexpected. She was buried at
<LOCATION>Hyde Chapel</LOCATION>.
14
The Shipman Inquiry
1.34 <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s daughter, <PERSON>Mrs Angela Woodruff</PERSON>, was a practising solicitor who, ever since
she had qualified, had conducted any necessary legal work on her mother’s behalf.
In 1986, she had drawn up <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s will, by which <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> had made her
daughter the sole beneficiary to her substantial estate. Following <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s death,
<PERSON>Mrs Woodruff</PERSON> became aware of the existence of what purported to be a new will; this
was dated 9th June 1998 and had been sent, together with a covering letter apparently
signed by <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>, to a firm of <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> solicitors very shortly before <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s
death. Those same solicitors, to whom <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> was not known, subsequently
received a letter from a person signing himself or herself ‘ <PERSON>J. Smith</PERSON>’ or ‘ <PERSON>S. Smith</PERSON>’,
informing them of <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s death. The new will left <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s entire estate
to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. A copy of the will and letters can be seen at the end of this Chapter.
Investigating <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s Death
1.35 <PERSON>Mrs Woodruff</PERSON> was immediately suspicious about the new will and her suspicions
deepened after she had visited and spoken to the two patients of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> whose
signatures appeared on the will as witnesses. On 24th July 1998, she reported her
suspicions to the police in <LOCATION>Warwickshire</LOCATION>, where she lived. The matter was passed to the
<ORGANIZATION>Greater Manchester Police</ORGANIZATION> for investigation and it was quickly realised that the doctor
who was the beneficiary of <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s new will was the same doctor who had been
the subject of a police investigation only a few months earlier.
1.36 A warrant for the exhumation of <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s body was obtained from the <LOCATION>South
Manchester</LOCATION> Coroner and the exhumation took place on 1st August 1998. On the same
day, the police executed warrants to search <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery and home address.
A typewriter and <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s medical records were seized from the surgery. On
3rd August 1998, Detective Chief Superintendent (then Detective Superintendent)
<PERSON>Bernard Postles</PERSON> was appointed Senior Investigating Officer and a major incident
investigation began.
1.37 A post-mortem examination of <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s body failed to establish the cause of her
death and a decision was taken to carry out toxicological tests. On 14th August 1998,
the police were told that initial tests carried out at the <LOCATION>North West Forensic Science
Laboratory</LOCATION> had shown the presence of an opiate, possibly morphine, in <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s
body. Further tests were to be carried out to confirm the type and levels of opiate
present.
1.38 Also on 14th August 1998, an inspector from the <ORGANIZATION>Home Office Drugs Inspectorate</ORGANIZATION>,
together with a chemist inspector from the <ORGANIZATION>Greater Manchester Police</ORGANIZATION>, visited <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
at his surgery and interviewed him in connection with his use of controlled drugs. Prior
to that visit, on 10th August, the <ORGANIZATION>Home Office</ORGANIZATION> inspector informed the police that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
had previous convictions. This was the first time that the <ORGANIZATION>Greater Manchester Police</ORGANIZATION>
became aware that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had a criminal record. Enquiries were then made and the
nature of the previous convictions established.
1.39 Meanwhile, the police had decided to re-examine the 19 deaths certified by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, of
which they had become aware during the March 1998 investigation. They began to
interview family members to ascertain whether they had any concerns about the
15
circumstances of the deaths. Later in August, the investigation was widened to include
a further nine deaths.
1.40 On 26th August 1998, the police were informed of the opinion of Mr <PERSON>Michael Hall</PERSON>, a
forensic document examiner, that the signatures on <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s new will had been
forged and the will itself had probably been typed on the typewriter which had been
seized from <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery.
1.41 On 28th August 1998, <PERSON>Mrs Julie Evans</PERSON>, a forensic scientist, told the police that the levels
of morphine present in Mrs <PERSON>Grundy</PERSON>’s body were consistent with levels which had
previously been known to have caused death by morphine overdose.
1.42 On 7th September 1998, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was arrested on suspicion of the murder of
<PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>, of attempting to obtain property by deception and of forgery. He was
interviewed in connection with those offences and later charged. The following day, he
appeared before Tameside Magistrates’ Court, when he was remanded in custody.
He has been in custody ever since.
Widening the Investigation
1.43 During September 1998, the bodies of Mrs <PERSON>Joan Melia</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Winifred Mellor</PERSON> and
<PERSON>Mrs Bianka Pomfret</PERSON> were exhumed. On 5th October 1998, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was arrested on
suspicion of their murders and was interviewed; however, the interview had to be
discontinued because <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> became distressed and confused. He was charged
with the three murders on 7th October 1998.
1.44 In October 1998, the bodies of Mrs <PERSON>Marie Quinn</PERSON> and Mrs Ivy Lomas were exhumed.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was arrested and interviewed in connection with those deaths on
11th November 1998 but made no comment during the interviews. He was charged with
both murders the same day.
1.45 Exhumations of the bodies of Mrs <PERSON>Jean Lilley</PERSON> and Mrs <PERSON>Irene Turner</PERSON> followed in
November 1998 and, following a further ‘ no comment’ interview, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was charged
with their murders on 3rd December 1998.
1.46 On 22nd February 1999, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was charged with the murder of <PERSON>Mrs Muriel Grimshaw</PERSON>,
whose body had been exhumed in December 1998, together with the murders of
Mrs <PERSON>Norah Nuttall</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Kathleen Wagstaff</PERSON>, Miss <PERSON>Maureen Ward</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Pamela Hillier</PERSON>,
<PERSON>Mrs Maria West</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mrs Lizzie Adams</PERSON>, all of whom had been cremated.
Suspension from Practice
1.47 The police had been attempting for some time to prevent <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> from continuing to
practise. They had informed the <ORGANIZATION>GMC</ORGANIZATION> of the position in August 1998 but were told that
the GMC could do nothing until <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had been convicted of an offence. On
18th August, the <ORGANIZATION>West Pennine Health Authority</ORGANIZATION> contacted the <ORGANIZATION>NHS Tribunal</ORGANIZATION>, which had
power to suspend him, but a hearing by the <ORGANIZATION>Tribunal</ORGANIZATION> could not be arranged before
29th September. After that hearing, the <ORGANIZATION>Tribunal</ORGANIZATION>’s decision to suspend <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> from
practice was not communicated to the <ORGANIZATION>Health Authority</ORGANIZATION> until 15th October. The Health
16
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry
Authority</ORGANIZATION> was able to take control of the practice only after the expiration of the period
for an appeal against that decision, on 29th October 1998.
The Criminal Trial
1.48 At <ORGANIZATION>Shipman</ORGANIZATION>’s trial, which opened on 5th October 1999, he pleaded not guilty to the 15
counts of murder against him and to one count of forging <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s will. On
31st January 2000, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was convicted on all counts. He was sentenced to 15 terms
of life imprisonment and, for the forgery, a concurrent term of four years’ imprisonment.
When sentencing <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, the trial judge, <PERSON>Mr Justice Forbes</PERSON>, stated that his
recommendation to the Home Secretary would be that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> should spend the
remainder of his days in prison.
The End of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s Professional Career
1.49 Following the trial, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was suspended from practice by the <ORGANIZATION>GMC Preliminary
Proceedings Committee</ORGANIZATION> and, on 11th February 2000, his name was erased from the
medical register by the <ORGANIZATION>Professional Conduct Committee</ORGANIZATION> of the GMC.
The Inquests
1.50 In the course of the police investigations, bodies had been exhumed in three cases
which did not form the subject of counts on the indictment at the criminal trial. Inquests
into the deaths of Mrs <PERSON>Sarah Ashworth</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Alice Kitchen</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mrs Elizabeth Mellor</PERSON> had
been opened and adjourned by the <LOCATION>South Manchester</LOCATION> Coroner shortly after the
exhumations; these inquests were concluded in August and September 2000; all three
resulted in verdicts of unlawful killing.
1.51 By the time of the trial, the police had investigated a large number of deaths amongst
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patients, in addition to the 15 deaths which were the subject of counts on the
indictment and the additional three cases where bodies had been exhumed. Some of
these investigations had been initiated by the police themselves; others had started as a
result of communications from concerned relatives. As a result of their investigations,
the police identified 23 further cases in which they believed that the evidence was
strong enough to justify a prosecution for murder.
1.52 On 18th February 2000, the Director of Public Prosecutions announced that no further
criminal proceedings would be instituted against <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> because of the impossibility
of his having a fair trial after the publicity surrounding his convictions in January 2000.
A further factor was that, since it had been recommended that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> should spend
the rest of his life in prison, no additional punishment would be imposed as a result of
any future conviction.
1.53 In early 2001, the <LOCATION>South Manchester</LOCATION> Coroner sought and obtained from the Home
Secretary a direction to open inquests into the 23 deaths identified by the police,
together with a further death, that of Mr <PERSON>Charles Killan</PERSON>. The inquests were held between
January and April 2001 and concerned the deaths of Mrs <PERSON>Dorothy Andrew</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Irene</PERSON>
17
<PERSON>Berry</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Edith Brady</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Edith Brock</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Elsie Cheetham</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Erla Copeland</PERSON>,
<PERSON>Mrs Lilian Cullen</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Valerie Cuthbert</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Elsie Dean</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Joan Dean</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Doris
Earls</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Elsie Hannible</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Irene Heathcote</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Hilda Hibbert</PERSON>, Mr <PERSON>Charles Killan</PERSON>,
<PERSON>Mrs Bertha Moss</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Nellie Mullen</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Gladys Saunders</PERSON>, Miss <PERSON>Mabel Shawcross</PERSON>,
<PERSON>Mrs Marjorie Waller</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Mary Walls</PERSON>, Miss <PERSON>Ada Warburton</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Amy Whitehead</PERSON> and
<PERSON>Mrs Joyce Woodhead</PERSON>. All resulted in verdicts of unlawful killing, save for the inquests
into the deaths of Mrs <PERSON>Joan Dean</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mrs Marjorie Waller</PERSON>, at the conclusion of which the
Coroner returned open verdicts.
Further Police Investigations
1.54 The publicity surrounding <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s trial and convictions caused more people to
contact the police, concerned about <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s possible involvement in the death of a
family member. By the beginning of 2001, the <ORGANIZATION>Greater Manchester Police</ORGANIZATION> had
investigated 192 deaths. Meanwhile, the <ORGANIZATION>West Yorkshire Police</ORGANIZATION> had carried out
investigations into the deaths of Mr <PERSON>Edward Walker</PERSON> and Mrs <PERSON>Margaret Wilmore</PERSON> in
<LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION> and an incident involving Professor <PERSON>Elaine Oswald</PERSON>, who had become
concerned during the criminal trial that she may have been one of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
intended victims.
1.55 On 5th January 2001, Professor <PERSON>Richard Baker</PERSON>’s review of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s practice, which
had been commissioned by the Chief Medical Officer, was published. That review,
which is discussed by Professor <PERSON>Baker</PERSON> in Appendix A to this Report, identified deaths
which he considered suspicious, having examined the cremation forms and/or the
medical records of the deceased. Approximately 60 of those deaths had not previously
been investigated by the police; following publication of the review, the
police proceeded to investigate them, together with others of which they had recently
become aware.
1.56 Professor <PERSON>Baker</PERSON> discovered that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had issued 22 Medical Certificates of Cause
of Death (MCCDs) during his time in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>, including the MCCD relating to the
death of Mr <PERSON>Edward Walker</PERSON>. Following the publication of the review, the <ORGANIZATION>West Yorkshire
Police</ORGANIZATION> investigated the other 21 deaths, as well as nine deaths (including that of
Mrs <PERSON>Margaret Wilmore</PERSON>) which had been certified by the local coroner. They interviewed
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> about the Todmorden deaths at <ORGANIZATION>Halifax Police Station</ORGANIZATION> on 30th April 2001. He
refused to answer their questions.
1.57 All those investigations had been concluded by June 2001, at which time the incident
room was closed and the police investigation scaled down.
The Laming Inquiry
1.58 On 1st February 2000, the day after <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s convictions, the Secretary of <ORGANIZATION>State for
Health</ORGANIZATION>, The Rt. Hon. <PERSON>Alan Milburn</PERSON>, MP, announced in the <ORGANIZATION>House of Commons</ORGANIZATION> the setting
up of an inquiry under the provisions of section 2 of the National Health Service Act
1977, under the chairmanship of Lord <PERSON>Laming</PERSON> of <LOCATION>Tewin</LOCATION>.
18
The Shipman Inquiry
1.59 The Laming Inquiry began its preliminary work but, when it was discovered that it was to
be held in private, that members of the families of the deceased former patients of
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> would not be permitted to hear or read the evidence given to the Inquiry by
statutory and other bodies or individuals, and that families would not be permitted legal
representation, there was widespread dissatisfaction about the form of the Inquiry.
Representations were made to the Secretary of State on behalf of a number of relatives
of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s known or suspected victims, known collectively as the <ORGANIZATION>Tameside Families
Support Group</ORGANIZATION>. The Secretary of <ORGANIZATION>State</ORGANIZATION> considered his decision afresh but declined to
change it. He stated his reasons for maintaining his stance in a letter dated 12th April
2000, addressed to Ms <PERSON>Ann Alexander</PERSON>, the solicitor representing the Support Group. As
a result, proceedings for judicial review were commenced by the <ORGANIZATION>Support Group</ORGANIZATION> and,
subsequently, by nine media organisations.
1.60 On 20th July 2000, the Divisional Court set aside the Secretary of <ORGANIZATION>State</ORGANIZATION>’s decision of
12th April and remitted the matter for re-determination by him.
1.61 On 21st September 2000, the Secretary of <ORGANIZATION>State</ORGANIZATION> announced that a public inquiry would
be held into the issues surrounding the crimes committed by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>.
