CHAPTER TEN
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s Unlawful Activities: The Early Years
10.1 The Inquiry’s Terms of Reference require it to consider the extent of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s unlawful
activities, without restriction as to the period to be considered. The legal team took the
view that this meant that the Inquiry should investigate suspicious or potentially
suspicious deaths which occurred at any time during <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s professional career,
starting with his time at the <LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION> General Infirmary.
<LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION> General Infirmary
10.2 The Inquiry received information about one death which occurred at the <ORGANIZATION>Pontefract
General Infirmary</ORGANIZATION> in 1973, regarding which a relative was expressing concern. On
investigation, the death proved to be completely unconnected with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. There
were no grounds whatsoever for suspicion of his involvement and the case was
subsequently closed. No other suspicious deaths occurring in <LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION> have been
brought to the Inquiry’s attention.
10.3 The Inquiry has had difficulty in obtaining information about <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s time in
<LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION>, as it is over 30 years since he started work there. Most of the consultants
under whom he did his training have now died and few staff who worked with him can
be traced. Few relevant documents have survived. However, the Inquiry has been in
touch with a retired consultant obstetrician and gynaecologist, who recalls <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
time in his department, and with other medical professionals who worked with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>.
None was aware of any sudden or unexplained death for which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> may have
been responsible.
10.4 I conclude that there is no evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed any patient while at the
<LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION> General Infirmary.
Locum Work
10.5 When <PERSON>Mrs Shipman</PERSON> attended to give evidence at the Inquiry, she was asked whether
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had worked as a locum whilst in <LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION>. She said that he had, on
occasions, worked as a locum for a general practitioner there. She could not give any
further details. However, the <ORGANIZATION>Eastern Wakefield NHS</ORGANIZATION> Primary Care Trust informed the
Inquiry that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> occasionally did surgeries for a general practice in <LOCATION>Tanshelf</LOCATION>, which
is in <LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION>. A member of that practice has confirmed that no concern was ever
expressed about <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s practice there and that she herself has no such concerns.
A similar view has been expressed by another doctor who also did locum work for the
same practice. The Inquiry has received no expressions of concern at all about
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s activities whilst working as a locum.
10.6 It has been suggested that, between the end of his employment at <LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION> and his
arrival in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have worked as a locum for a general practitioner
practising in the <LOCATION>Boothtown</LOCATION> area of <LOCATION>Halifax</LOCATION>, who had been injured in a road accident.
Enquiries have been made of the <LOCATION>Calderdale</LOCATION> and <ORGANIZATION>Kirklees Health Authority</ORGANIZATION> and of
126
The Shipman Inquiry
general practitioners who were practising in <LOCATION>Boothtown</LOCATION> at the relevant time. None has
any record or recollection that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> worked there, whether as a locum or otherwise.
It has also been suggested that a number of the 74 pethidine offences which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
had taken into consideration by the Magistrates’ Court at the time of his convictions in
1976 were committed in <LOCATION>Halifax</LOCATION>, before his arrival in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>. That information is
certainly wrong. Although the list of offences taken into consideration no longer survives,
the contemporaneous documents from the <ORGANIZATION>Home Office</ORGANIZATION> and the police, which the
Inquiry has obtained, make no mention of any investigations into <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s activities in
<LOCATION>Halifax</LOCATION> and those documents, together with contemporaneous press reports, make it
clear that the conduct under consideration at the Magistrates’ Court took place in
<LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>. <PERSON>Mrs Shipman</PERSON>’s evidence was that she did not believe that her husband
ever worked in <LOCATION>Boothstown</LOCATION> and indeed all the evidence suggests that he moved straight
from <LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION> to <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>.
10.7 I conclude that there is no evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed any patient while working as a
locum doctor in <LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION>. I think he probably did not work as a locum in <LOCATION>Halifax</LOCATION>.
<ORGANIZATION>General Practice</ORGANIZATION> in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>
1974
10.8 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> took up his position as an Assistant General Practitioner with the <ORGANIZATION>Todmorden
Group Practice</ORGANIZATION> at the <LOCATION>Abraham Ormerod Centre</LOCATION> on 1st March 1974. At that time, he was
married and had two young children. He impressed his colleagues and, within a short
time, he became a junior partner.
10.9 In Chapter Eight, I have described how <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> began to obtain large amounts of
pethidine soon after his arrival in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>. It appears to me that these supplies were
primarily for his own use. I do not believe that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> began obtaining pethidine with
the intention of using it to kill patients. Moreover, I do not even suspect that he killed any
patient during his first nine or ten months in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>.
10.10 The first evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> unlawfully administered a drug to a patient arises in the
case of Mrs (now Professor) <PERSON>Elaine Oswald</PERSON>. On 21st August 1974, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> saw
<PERSON>Mrs Oswald</PERSON> in his surgery. A full account of the ensuing events appears in Volume Two.
<PERSON>Mrs Oswald</PERSON> was complaining of pain in her left side. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> advised her that she
might have a kidney stone. He prescribed Diconal, an opiate analgesic taken orally. He
advised her to take one or two tablets and to go home to bed. He said that he would visit
at the end of surgery to take a blood sample for testing purposes. This was, in my view,
a spurious excuse to visit her, as the appropriate test would have been to analyse a
urine sample. In any event, either type of sample could easily have been taken at the
surgery. <PERSON>Mrs Oswald</PERSON> was in bed and was feeling drowsy when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived at her
home in the late morning. Whether he took a blood sample is not clear. He might have
done. However, I have found that he injected <PERSON>Mrs Oswald</PERSON> with a drug, which I think was
probably pethidine. She rapidly became unconscious. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did his utmost to revive
her. He gave cardiac massage and the ‘ kiss of life’. He caused an ambulance to attend
and she was taken to hospital. Professor <PERSON>Oswald</PERSON> says that she was suspected of having
taken a drug overdose. I accept that she had not.
127
10.11 Professor <PERSON>Oswald</PERSON> now believes that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> may have tried to kill her. I am sure that he
did not. Had he intended to kill her, he would not have gone to such lengths to revive
her. It is possible that she collapsed because she is unusually sensitive to opiate drugs.
I think it more likely that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had miscalculated the dose of pethidine he gave her
or failed to take account of the Diconal she had already taken on his instruction. That
leaves unanswered the question as to why he would have wished to give <PERSON>Mrs Oswald</PERSON>
pethidine in the first place. He was already a regular user, if not an addict. I think it most
likely that he wanted to involve the unwitting <PERSON>Mrs Oswald</PERSON> in taking pethidine, possibly
because he hoped to involve her in some sexual activity. I think it likely that this incident
gave him a very nasty scare. I cannot say that he never embarked on such a venture
again. He might have done. However, there is no evidence that he did. I am satisfied
that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s action in injecting <PERSON>Mrs Oswald</PERSON> was unlawful and amounted to an offence
of assault occasioning actual bodily harm contrary to Section 47 of the Offences Against
the Person Act 1861.
10.12 I have examined the circumstances of nine deaths that occurred in 1974, with which
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had some connection. In five, he certified the cause of death. In the other four,
the cause of death was certified by the coroner and I have found that the deaths were
natural. Of the five for which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified the cause, I have found that four were
natural deaths, although in two I have been unable to say that I am sure of my
conclusion, not because there is any real cause for suspicion but because the evidence
is scanty or vague. In the case of the remaining death, I have been unable to reach any
conclusion, as there is so little information.
1975
10.13 In 1975, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s illegal acquisition of pethidine continued. At a conservative estimate
he obtained 70,000mg pethidine and smaller quantities of other drugs, such as
morphine sulphate and <LOCATION>Pethilorfan</LOCATION>. As I noted in Chapter Eight, in February and March
1975, he acquired sufficient morphine sulphate to kill several people.
10.14 I have examined the circumstances of 21 deaths which occurred in 1975 and with which
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had some association. Only in the case of Mrs <PERSON>Eva Lyons</PERSON> have I concluded
that he was responsible for the death. <PERSON>Mrs Lyons</PERSON> was suffering from terminal cancer and
it appears that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> visited late at night on 17th March 1975 and gave her an
intravenous injection into the back of the hand. He then sat with her husband for a few
minutes and <PERSON>Mrs Lyons</PERSON> died. Mr <PERSON>Lyons</PERSON> told his daughter that he thought <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had
helped his wife ‘ on her way’. While I cannot be sure that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s intention was to kill
rather than to relieve pain, I think it likely (from the evidence that he stayed to talk with
Mr <PERSON>Lyons</PERSON> until the death had occurred) that he intended that the death should occur
while he was there, rather than at some time during the night or on the following day.
I think he probably gave a dose of opiate, which was not assessed in good faith with
the primary intention to relieve pain, but was intended to end life.
10.15 I explained in Chapter Nine that I do not intend to involve myself in a debate about
euthanasia or assisted suicide. It may be that some people would regard as acceptable
what <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did to <PERSON>Mrs Lyons</PERSON>. I shall express no view. The law is clear. If a doctor
gives an overdose of an opiate drug, intending thereby to end the patient’s life, and it
128
The Shipman Inquiry
does end the patient’s life, that is murder, even though the patient might have died
naturally within a very short time. I recognise that it may be very difficult for a doctor to
assess the dose of opiate necessary to relieve pain and that sometimes a doctor will
unintentionally hasten the death of a terminally ill patient by giving pain relief. Provided
that the dose is assessed in good faith, as being that which is necessary for pain relief,
the doctor acts lawfully. In the case of <PERSON>Mrs Lyons</PERSON>, the close temporal association
between the injection and the death persuaded me that there was a causal connection
between the two. I had then to determine <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s intention. That he remained with
Mr <PERSON>Lyons</PERSON> until the death occurred suggests that he knew that the death would occur
within minutes. I infer that he intended that it would occur within minutes and must have
given a lethal dose. The evidence of intent might appear slight but seems to me to justify
my conclusion.
10.16 <PERSON>Mrs Lyons</PERSON> was the only <PERSON>Todmorden</PERSON> case in which I reached a positive decision that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had killed. However, there were six further deaths which aroused suspicion
that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have caused them. In none is the evidence clear enough to enable
me to reach a positive conclusion. All but one of these patients was very unwell and a
natural death would have been entirely explicable. Of those five patients, three were
terminally ill and death was probably imminent. The other two were very unwell,
although not expected to die imminently. Only Mrs <PERSON>Edith Roberts</PERSON> appears to have been
in reasonable health at the time of her death.
10.17 Three of those suspicious deaths occurred on the same day, 21st January 1975.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified the cause of all three deaths. The patients were Mrs <PERSON>Elizabeth Pearce</PERSON>,
Mr <PERSON>Robert Lingard</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mrs Lily Crossley</PERSON>.
10.18 <PERSON>Mrs Pearce</PERSON> was 84 and was probably frail and very short of breath. She was living with
her daughter and a downstairs bedroom had been provided for her. Her surviving
relatives recall, however, that she had been well over Christmas 1974 and had joined in
family celebrations. <PERSON>Mrs Pearce</PERSON> was said to be well at lunchtime on the day of her death,
although the evidence is not entirely clear. She died during the afternoon, probably at
about 4.10pm. Her daughter and her daughter’s partner were both present in the house.
Both have since died and there is no direct evidence of the circumstances of
<PERSON>Mrs Pearce</PERSON>’s death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> attributed the death to a cerebrovascular accident due to
underlying atherosclerosis. On cremation Form B, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he had seen
Mrs Pearce alive on the day of her death. He said he also saw the body 20 minutes after
death. That implies two separate visits but would also be consistent with only one visit,
spanning the death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said on Form B that the mode of death was collapse
lasting 15 minutes. He said that this information was based on his own observations and
statements made by <PERSON>Mrs Pearce</PERSON>’s daughter. These answers give rise to a real suspicion
that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was present at the death. He might have visited earlier in the day and
been called back when <PERSON>Mrs Pearce</PERSON> suddenly collapsed and died in the afternoon. On
the other hand, he might have been called out because <PERSON>Mrs Pearce</PERSON> was unwell and he
might have then given her an injection, ostensibly to help her, but in fact ensuring that
her death took place while he was there. As there is no witness evidence, I cannot reach
a decision. However, I recognise that it is possible that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Pearce</PERSON>. If he
did, his modus operandi would be typical of many later killings. Later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> often
129
killed elderly people who were very ill, possibly facing a real risk of death. He would give
an injection which ensured that the patient died, rather than treating his or her condition
and giving a chance of survival and recovery.
10.19 Mr <PERSON>Lingard</PERSON> was only 62 at the time of his death but he was in very poor respiratory
health and his son had been warned by Dr <PERSON>Grieve</PERSON> (who was Mr <PERSON>Lingard</PERSON>’s general
practitioner) that Mr <PERSON>Lingard</PERSON> had not long to live. Mr <PERSON>Lingard</PERSON> died at about 7.30pm. His
wife had been with him all day. She is now dead and there is no evidence from anyone
with direct knowledge of the circumstances of the death. Mr <PERSON>Lingard</PERSON>’s son and
daughter-in-law remember only being summoned to the house by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> with news of
the death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that the death was due to bronchiectasis with emphysema,
conditions from which Mr <PERSON>Lingard</PERSON> was almost certainly suffering. On cremation Form B,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> stated that he had seen Mr <PERSON>Lingard</PERSON> alive on the day of the death. He also said
that he had seen the body almost immediately after death. This gives rise to a suspicion
that he was present at the death. If he was, the suspicion arises that he might have been
involved in it. I cannot say that he was but it is a real possibility. If <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did anything
which hastened Mr <PERSON>Lingard</PERSON>’s death, it would have been typical of later conduct.
10.20 <PERSON>Mrs Crossley</PERSON> was suffering from terminal cancer. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> visited her at about 7.30pm
and administered some sort of injection for pain relief. It is not known whether this was
given intravenously or intramuscularly. Nor it is known what the drug was. It might have
been pethidine, as <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> later admitted, when being interviewed by a police officer
and a <ORGANIZATION>Home Office</ORGANIZATION> drugs inspector in November 1975, that he had taken for himself
most of the proceeds of a prescription for pethidine made out in <LOCATION>Mrs Crossley</LOCATION>’s name.
However, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might well have used a stronger opiate on Mrs <PERSON>Crossley</PERSON> and kept the
pethidine for himself. <PERSON>Mrs Crossley</PERSON> seems to have died about an hour after the injection
was given. A lethal dose, given intramuscularly, will cause death within about an hour.
The temporal relationship between the injection and the death gives rise to a suspicion
that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have deliberately given a lethal dose. However, the evidence is not
sufficiently strong for me to infer that he did. <PERSON>Mrs Crossley</PERSON> might have died naturally. The
evidence about timings is not wholly reliable.
10.21 The fact that three deaths occurred on the same day, 21st January 1975, gives rise to
additional suspicion. Two of the deaths occurred in the evening. It would appear that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must have been on duty that evening. His partners say that he was always
willing to turn out after normal surgery hours. It seems a coincidence that three patients
should die naturally within so short a time, all while under <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s care. On the other
hand, I recognise that this was January and that old people with respiratory problems
are particularly vulnerable at that time of year.
10.22 Mrs <PERSON>Jane Rowland</PERSON> died on 15th February 1975. She was in the terminal stages of a
respiratory illness and was in considerable distress. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave an injection which
he said would make her more comfortable and help her breathing. It is not known what
the injection was or how it was administered. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> left and returned about two hours
later, by which time <PERSON>Mrs Rowland</PERSON> had died; it is not clear when she died. Her daughters
are said to have thought that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had hastened their mother’s death but had done
her a favour, as he had relieved her suffering. Suspicion arises because of the temporal
130
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry</ORGANIZATION>
association between the injection and the death. The death could have been completely
natural but it might not have been. Such circumstances would not give rise to suspicion
with other doctors, but with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> they do.
10.23 Mrs <PERSON>Edith Roberts</PERSON> was found dead on 21st March 1975. Her surviving relatives say that
she was in good health and that her death was very sudden and wholly unexpected.
However, she was diabetic and had some history of chest pain. She was only 67 and
was living a fully independent life. She spent the evening of 20th March with her two
nieces, who say that she was in normal health. The following day, she was found dead in
bed. She was lying back against the pillows, with the bedside light on and a book in
front of her. It appeared that she had fallen asleep while reading. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was called.
Because the death was so sudden, <PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON>’ niece asked if a post-mortem
examination would be necessary. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that it would not, as <PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON> had
seen a doctor recently. He certified that the death was due to coronary thrombosis, due
to ischaemic heart disease. Concern arises because, in such circumstances, most
doctors would have referred the sudden death to the coroner, even if the patient had
been seen within a few days. When <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> completed cremation Form B, he made a
number of entries that give rise to a suspicion that he might have been involved in
<PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON>’ death. These entries are fully explained in my decision. The evidence of
<PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON>’ position and appearance in death, which was confirmed by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> in
Form B, is not typical of that usually seen following a fatal heart attack. Usually, there is
some sign that the patient has been aware that something dreadful is happening and
has tried to seek help. <PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON>’ peaceful appearance, as if asleep, is the first in time
of countless such descriptions I have received during the Inquiry. Her appearance was
typical of what is seen following death by lethal opiate injection. The suspicious features
of the case are not, however, enough to draw me to the conclusion that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
probably killed <PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON>. However, if he did, it would appear that he must have been
called out late the previous evening and must have given her an intramuscular injection
of a lethal dose of opiate. He could not have given her an intravenous injection, as her
front door was locked from within. If she had had an intravenous lethal injection, she
would not have been able to let him out, lock the door and return to her bed. She would
have been able to do all those things after an intramuscular injection. If <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed
<PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON>, he did so only three or four days after he had killed <PERSON>Mrs Lyons</PERSON>.
10.24 The last suspicious death is that of Mr <PERSON>Albert Redvers Williams</PERSON>, who died on 5th August
1975. He was quite unwell but was not thought to be close to death. If <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed
him, he did so by the oral administration of a drug. This method was not typical of
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s later killings.
10.25 The remaining deaths investigated from this period were either plainly or probably
natural or remain shrouded in uncertainty. The Inquiry’s investigations into the
Todmorden deaths were hampered by the passage of time and the fact that most of the
witnesses with firsthand knowledge of the circumstances are now dead. Where
cremation documents have survived, they provide an insight into <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account of
events and, in some cases, these accounts give rise to real suspicion that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
might have been involved. Where the circumstances do give rise to suspicion, they do
not show the clear patterns of behaviour identifiable in later years. This could be
131
because they were, in fact, natural deaths. Another possible explanation is that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
did kill some or all of these patients but had not yet established a preferred technique. It
may be that he was experimenting with drugs and modes of administration. If any or all
of these suspicious cases were killings, it would tend to suggest that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s earliest
killings were of patients who were in poor health and who were likely to die in the very
near or not too distant future.
10.26 I have already described in Chapter One the circumstances in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> left
<LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION> in September 1975, when it was discovered that he had been stealing
pethidine. I have recorded the circumstances of his conviction in February 1976 and the
way in which he was allowed to continue as a medical practitioner. He did so by taking a
job in <LOCATION>County Durham</LOCATION>.
County <ORGANIZATION>Durham</ORGANIZATION>
10.27 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> began his employment at the <LOCATION>Newton Aycliffe Health Centre</LOCATION> on 2nd February
1976 and continued there until 30th September 1977. He worked in the <ORGANIZATION>Community Child
Health Service</ORGANIZATION>, conducting health clinics and advising on child development. Those
involved with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> at the time have little recollection of the circumstances of his
employment and the Inquiry has received no reports of concern about his conduct
during his time at <LOCATION>Newton Aycliffe</LOCATION>. His work there would not have afforded him access to
controlled drugs. I conclude that there is no evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed anyone while
working in <LOCATION>County Durham</LOCATION>.

The Shipman Inquiry
133
CHAPTER ELEVEN
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s Unlawful Activities: The Donneybrook Years
11.1 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> began to practise at the <LOCATION>Donneybrook Medical Centre</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Clarendon Street</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>
on 1st October 1977. In Chapter Four, I have described the arrangements and working
practices in operation there. In this Chapter, I shall deal with the results of the Inquiry’s
investigations into <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s crimes during the <LOCATION>Donneybrook</LOCATION> years.
11.2 The extent to which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was able to and did kill patients is inextricably connected
with his ability to obtain supplies of the strong opioid drugs which he undoubtedly used.
In Chapter Eight, I mentioned that there was no longer any documentary evidence from
the late 1970s and 1980s from which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s access to controlled drugs could be
established. Such evidence is available from his usual source of supply, the <ORGANIZATION>Norwest
Co-op Pharmacy</ORGANIZATION>, from 1991 onwards. One of his favoured methods of obtaining
pethidine in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION> was to prescribe larger than necessary quantities for patients,
many of whom were terminally ill and had a genuine need for the drug, and to take the
excess for himself. I consider it likely that he adopted a similar technique after his arrival
in <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>. It is possible that he also used other methods during the <LOCATION>Donneybrook</LOCATION> years,
such as are demonstrated by the controlled drugs register for 1993. During that year,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obtained fourteen single 30mg ampoules of diamorphine by prescribing them
in the name of patients who did not need them and, sometimes, in the name of a patient
who had recently died.
11.3 I think, however, that he would probably have been very cautious about the ways in
which he obtained controlled drugs when he first arrived in <LOCATION>Hyde</LOCATION>. He had been caught
out in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION> and must have realised that he had been lucky not to be disciplined
by the <ORGANIZATION>GMC</ORGANIZATION> in 1976. He had had to admit to the members of his new practice that he
had been convicted of drugs offences. He told them that he did not intend to keep a
personal controlled drugs register. That would mean that he was not allowed to carry
controlled drugs in his medical bag. He would not have wished to do anything to arouse
their suspicion, in the early days, until he had established a degree of trust and
confidence. I think it unlikely therefore that he would have risked signing orders for
controlled drugs at the pharmacies or prescribing for patients who were dead. It seems
to me that, when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> wanted illicit supplies of controlled drugs, he would, at first,
choose the method least vulnerable to detection, namely to take possession of the
drugs left over after a cancer death. It would be easy for him to say to the family, and
possibly to the district nurses, that he was taking the drugs away for destruction.
11.4 Once the decisions in the individual cases were complete, I was able to identify the
deaths of patients with terminal cancer who had been cared for by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> and for
whom it was known or might reasonably be supposed that he was prescribing opiates.
I found a remarkably close correlation between opportunities to obtain drugs prescribed
for the terminally ill and deaths that I have attributed to him. The following account will
demonstrate a link between the cancer deaths which could have provided supplies of
opiate and the deaths he caused. This correlation is not always precise but I believe it is
close enough to be significant.
134
The Shipman Inquiry
11.5 I do not propose to give a full account of the circumstances of every death that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
caused during his years at the <LOCATION>Donneybrook Medical Centre</LOCATION>. A chronological list of
decided cases appears at Appendix F and the decision for each case is to be found in
Volumes Three to Six. Instead, I shall seek to describe the pattern of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
criminality and to draw attention to the development of his modus operandi. I described
in Chapter Nine some of the features that became apparent during <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s trial as
typical of his methods. Other features, which had not arisen in the 15 conviction cases,
emerged as typical from my examination of the other cases where I have found that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had killed. Many of these features first appeared in the <LOCATION>Donneybrook</LOCATION> years.
The Period from 1977 to 1983
1977
11.6 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not begin to kill patients as soon as he arrived in <LOCATION>Hyde</LOCATION>. He certified the
causes of only four deaths during the three month period between October and
December 1977. I have found that two of the deaths were probably natural. In the third,
I am unable to reach any decision, as almost nothing is known of the circumstances.
The investigation into the fourth death was closed. In none of the four deaths was the
cause related to cancer. Nor was there any reason to suppose that opiate drugs might
have been prescribed.
1978
11.7 I have found that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed four patients in 1978. He attributed each death to
coronary thrombosis. He probably obtained opiates following the deaths of two patients
who died of cancer in late July. Those drugs were almost certainly used to kill Mrs <PERSON>Sarah
Marsland</PERSON> on 7th August and Mrs <PERSON>Mary Jordan</PERSON> on 30th August. Further cancer deaths
occurred on 25th September and 5th December. In both of those cases, the patient was
receiving opiates for pain relief and I suspect that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have hastened the
death by giving an overdose. In either case, he might well have retained excess drugs.
On 7th December, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mr <PERSON>Harold Bramwell</PERSON>, who was suffering from cancer
and had been prescribed opiates. From some or all of these deaths <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> could
have retained excess drugs and would then have had a supply with which to kill
<PERSON>Mrs Annie Campbell</PERSON> on 20th December.
11.8 <PERSON>Mrs Marsland</PERSON>’s death is an early example of what was to become a typical <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
killing. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was in the habit of making unsolicited visits; his elderly patients
appreciated it. I think this was normal practice for many general practitioners in the
1970s and 1980s, although it no longer is. <PERSON>Mrs Marsland</PERSON> was a widow who lived alone.
She was 86 but was in quite good health for her age and was still mobile and
independent. She had recently suffered a bereavement; one of her daughters, <PERSON>Cicely</PERSON>,
had died and she was rather depressed. On the day of the death, her daughter,
<PERSON>Mrs Irene Chapman</PERSON> (who was herself later to become one of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims) arrived
at her mother’s house to find her mother lying on the bed with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> leaning over her.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he had found <PERSON>Mrs Marsland</PERSON> sitting in her chair; she had told him that
she ‘ got an awful pain’ when she thought of <PERSON>Cicely</PERSON>. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said he had moved her
onto the bed. She had collapsed and died. He said that he had tried unsuccessfully to
135
resuscitate her. That was plainly not true; for one thing he had not moved her onto a
hard surface. However, it was the extraordinary coincidence that <PERSON>Mrs Marsland</PERSON> should
die just at the time of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s unsolicited visit which persuaded me that he had
probably killed her. This case illustrates <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s ability to make up a story as an
explanation for what had occurred and the willingness of shocked and grieving relatives
to accept a most implausible account of the death.
1979–1980
11.9 I have found that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not kill any patients in the early part of 1979. On 18th July,
<PERSON>Mrs Lavinia Wharmby</PERSON> died of cancer. I suspect that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have hastened
her death and I also suspect that he acquired a supply of opioid drugs at the time of
her death. There was another cancer death on 2nd August. On 4th and 5th August,
there were two deaths about which I have strong suspicions but cannot reach
positive conclusions.
11.10 On 9th August 1979, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave <PERSON>Mrs Alice Gorton</PERSON> a large dose of opiate, from which
she died the following day. <PERSON>Mrs Gorton</PERSON> was 76 and suffered from very severe psoriasis.
Her general health was reasonable, although she probably had angina. <PERSON>Mrs Gorton</PERSON>’s
daughter, <PERSON>Mrs O’Neill</PERSON>, lived nearby and saw her every day. She spent the morning of
9th August with her mother, who seemed in normal health. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was due to make a
routine visit to provide supplies of creams and dressings needed for her chronic
condition. When <PERSON>Mrs O’Neill</PERSON> went home for lunch, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had not yet arrived. About
half an hour after she had reached home, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> came to <PERSON>Mrs O’Neill</PERSON>’s house. He told
her that her mother had been taken ill and she must come with him immediately.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> left and <PERSON>Mrs O’Neill</PERSON> followed. When she arrived, a few minutes later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
was in her mother’s front room. He told her that there would be no need for a postmortem
examination. The import of this was just dawning on <PERSON>Mrs O’Neill</PERSON> when she heard
a loud groan coming from the bedroom. She rushed in to find her mother lying
unconscious on the bed, fully clothed. <PERSON>Mrs Gorton</PERSON> lay in a coma until she died the
following afternoon. She was not admitted to hospital. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that the death
was due to a coronary thrombosis. The death was not referred to the coroner.
11.11 On hearing the evidence, I strongly suspected that <PERSON>Mrs Gorton</PERSON>’s sudden collapse into
unconsciousness had occurred in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s presence and had been caused by him.
The coincidence that she should have been taken ill in the short interval between
<PERSON>Mrs O’Neill</PERSON>’s departure and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s arrival was too great to accept. I was puzzled
because, in the other killings that I had by this time considered, the deaths had all
occurred very quickly. I had not come across a lingering death about which there was
suspicion or concern. However, Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> explained that it was possible that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
had given a ‘ sub-lethal’ dose of opiate, which was not quite enough to kill <PERSON>Mrs Gorton</PERSON>
outright. The opiate could have depressed her respiration to such an extent that she had
suffered permanent brain damage due to lack of oxygen. She could have then lingered
until she died, either as the result of the brain damage or from bronchopneumonia (to
which she would be vulnerable while lying unconscious) or possibly from a combination
of the two. I concluded that that is what had happened. I infer that, for some reason,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> underestimated the amount of opiate needed.
136
The Shipman Inquiry
11.12 Three months later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mr <PERSON>Jack Leslie Shelmerdine</PERSON>. Mr <PERSON>Shelmerdine</PERSON> had
chronic bronchitis and, in the few days before his death, was suffering from an episode
of heart failure. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was called out late one evening when Mr <PERSON>Shelmerdine</PERSON> was very
breathless. He gave him an intramuscular injection of opiate. Mr <PERSON>Shelmerdine</PERSON> went into
a deep sleep from which he never awoke. He died from bronchopneumonia about
30 hours after the injection. It is known that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave an injection of opiate
because he said so in a letter written in response to a complaint made by
Mr <PERSON>Shelmerdine</PERSON>’s son to the <ORGANIZATION>Regional Health Authority</ORGANIZATION>. The complaint arose because
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had promised to arrange a domiciliary visit by a geriatrician. The geriatrician
did not attend when expected. As a result, Mr <PERSON>Shelmerdine</PERSON> was moved to hospital and
died not long after arrival. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> admitted that he had given 10mg morphine at his
late night visit, although I suspect that he gave more. Even 10mg would have been
excessive for a patient in Mr <PERSON>Shelmerdine</PERSON>’s condition and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must have known
that the likely effect would be to send the patient into a deep sleep and to depress his
respiration. I think it likely that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> intended that Mr <PERSON>Shelmerdine</PERSON> would die of
respiratory failure during the hour following the injection. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must also have known
that, if Mr <PERSON>Shelmerdine</PERSON> were to survive the night but remain unconscious, he could well
die of bronchopneumonia within the next day or so. I do not think <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was ignorant
of the effects of opiate drugs. I think he knew of the special risk of giving opiates to
patients with impaired respiratory function.
11.13 I strongly suspect that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave Mr <PERSON>Shelmerdine</PERSON> more than 10mg morphine.
I suspect that he intended to kill him outright but, as with <PERSON>Mrs Gorton</PERSON>, he
underestimated the dose. I think <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must have been unnerved by his failure to kill
these two victims as quickly and efficiently as he had intended. He was probably very
worried by the complaint to the <ORGANIZATION>Regional Health Authority</ORGANIZATION>, even though he himself was
not the subject of the complaint. It appears likely that he did not kill again until April
1981, well over a year later. In the intervening period, there is a suspicion that he might
have killed Miss <PERSON>Bethel Evans</PERSON> on 3rd January 1980. There are also several deaths during
this period about which I have been unable to reach any conclusion due to the
insufficiency of evidence.
1981
11.14 On 7th January and 2nd March 1981, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> attended at two cancer deaths, either or
both of which could have provided him with a supply of opiates. On Saturday, 18th April,
he killed <PERSON>Mrs May Slater</PERSON>. This death is an early example of the way in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
involved and made use of other people in the events surrounding the death. He often
involved the wardens of sheltered accommodation. Wardens are in a position of
responsibility towards their residents and their involvement would allay any suspicion
which might otherwise arise. On occasions, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> would leave the warden to deal
with the deceased’s relatives.
11.15 <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON> was 84 and lived in sheltered accommodation at <LOCATION>Bradley Green Old People’s
Centre</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Hough Lane</PERSON>, <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>, which was supervised by a warden, <PERSON>Mrs Doreen Laithwaite</PERSON>.
On the Saturday afternoon, <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON>’s son, Mr <PERSON>James Slater</PERSON>, and his wife received a
telephone call from <PERSON>Mrs Laithwaite</PERSON>, to say that <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON> had been taken ill and the
137
doctor had been called. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> attended. <PERSON>Mrs Laithwaite</PERSON> says that it would be her
usual practice to accompany a doctor who called to see one of her residents and to stay
during the consultation. However, it appears that, on this occasion, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must have
instructed her to meet the family and prevent them from coming to <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON>’s
bungalow. He was then alone with <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON> and had the opportunity to kill her, which
I believe he took. When Mr and <PERSON>Mrs James Slater</PERSON> arrived, they were prevented from
going straight into <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON>’s bungalow. They cannot now recall why this happened
but they recall meeting <PERSON>Mrs Laithwaite</PERSON> and being taken to her flat. A while later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
came out of <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON>’s bungalow and told <PERSON>Mrs Laithwaite</PERSON> that <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON> had died. He
did not speak to the family. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> later certified that the death was due to congestive
heart failure. The medical records have not survived and I cannot tell whether <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON>
had been suffering from that condition. I assume that she had been, at least to some
extent. I also assume that <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON> was quite unwell that afternoon as the doctor was
called out on a Saturday afternoon. It is possible that <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON> died a natural death.
However, as <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obviously contrived to be alone with <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON>, I think it likely that
he killed her. Even if <PERSON>Mrs Slater</PERSON> was suffering from the effects of heart failure, it is typical
that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> would take the opportunity to ensure that she did not survive.
11.16 In August 1981, there is a very close temporal relationship between the natural death of
Mrs <PERSON>Emma Smith</PERSON> from cancer on 25th August and the killing of Mrs <PERSON>Elizabeth Ashworth</PERSON>
on the following day. <PERSON>Mrs Ashworth</PERSON>’s death was in many respects a typical <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
killing. She was living independently at home and was in reasonable health. She had
been gardening on the day of her death. She was taken ill and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was called. He
gave her an injection and she died not long afterwards.
11.17 In September and October 1981, there were three deaths about which I entertain some
suspicion but the evidence is not sufficiently strong for me to reach a positive
conclusion. Two of them raise the possibility that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have killed using
Largactil (the proprietary name for chlorpromazine) rather than an opiate drug. On
8th September 1981, <PERSON>Mrs Ann Coulthard</PERSON> died. She had had a stroke, or possibly two.
She was plainly declining and, during her last few days, she seemed restless and
possibly in pain. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> attended on 7th September and gave an injection in the
buttock, after which <PERSON>Mrs Coulthard</PERSON> was very sleepy. The injection might have been a
sub-lethal dose of an opiate but might well have been Largactil, or some other sedative.
Largactil is not a controlled drug. The following morning, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> informed the family
that <PERSON>Mrs Coulthard</PERSON> would die that day. It is not clear whether or not he gave another
injection during that visit. However, he came again in the early evening and gave an
injection. <PERSON>Mrs Coulthard</PERSON> died about an hour afterwards, although the time estimate is
very uncertain. It is possible that this death was entirely natural but other possibilities do
arise. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have given a lethal dose of opiate on the evening of the death. He
might have given a further dose of a sedative, such as Largactil. In other words, it is
possible that he kept Mrs Coulthard very deeply sedated for something over 24 hours.
11.18 Miss <PERSON>Elsie Scott</PERSON> died on 6th October 1981. There is evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave her a
very large dose of Largactil on the day before her death. The dose appears to have
been 100mg. Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> says that an appropriate dose for Miss <PERSON>Scott</PERSON> would have been
of the order of 25mg. The evidence of Miss <PERSON>Scott</PERSON>’s underlying state of health is unclear.
138
The Shipman Inquiry
Here again, the suspicion arises that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have deliberately over-sedated this
elderly patient in the 18 hour period before her death.
11.19 Elderly patients who are over-sedated are at increased risk of developing
bronchopneumonia, a common mechanism of death in the elderly. It is possible that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> deliberately over-sedated Mrs Coulthard and Miss <PERSON>Scott</PERSON> to ensure that they
would not regain consciousness. There are other cases where a similar suspicion
arises. Even where the factual evidence is clear (and in some cases it is not) I do not
feel able to reach a positive decision. It is quite possible that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave the sedative
for proper therapeutic reasons. He might have given the drug as the best way to keep
the patient comfortable. However, because I know that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed his patients,
I naturally suspect his motives. If <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> wanted to kill, to inject Largactil (or indeed
any other sedative) would be a very uncertain method to choose. More than one
ampoule would be necessary to produce very deep sedation. Patient responses are
very variable and the patient might not die. However, it is possible that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> used
<PERSON>Largactil</PERSON>, intending to kill, despite the disadvantage of uncertainty. <PERSON>Largactil</PERSON> would have
some advantages. It would be less likely to excite the suspicion of the care staff in a
residential home than would an opiate, which, if given in a lethal dose, would cause
death within the hour. Largactil is not a controlled drug and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> would have been
entitled, quite properly, to have a supply in his medical bag. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might at times
have been short of illicit supplies of opiate. In the light of these uncertainties, I cannot
say whether <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> ever deliberately killed a patient by over-sedation. I suspect that
he might have done.
1982–1983
11.20 With the possible exception of the two suspicious deaths mentioned above and four
other suspicious deaths in 1982, I have not made any finding that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed
between August 1981, when he killed <PERSON>Mrs Elizabeth Ashworth</PERSON>, and January 1983, when
he hastened the death of Mr <PERSON>Percy Ward</PERSON>, who was terminally ill. Part of this interval
might be explained by the lack of a ready supply of opiates. However, that does not
seem to have been the only reason, as there were two cancer deaths in March 1982 and
no death which arouses more than suspicion occurred for a further ten months.
11.21 Mr <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> was 90 and was very ill indeed. He had had a burst duodenal ulcer; he was
having great difficulty in breathing and his death was expected. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave him an
injection after which he soon died. I observe that the risk to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> when killing a
patient who was terminally ill was very much less than the risk he ran when killing a
patient who was in reasonable health and living independently and whose death was
therefore sudden and unexpected.
11.22 After killing <PERSON>Mrs Ashworth</PERSON> in August 1981, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not kill a ‘ healthy’ patient until
28th June 1983, when he killed Miss <PERSON>Moira Fox</PERSON>. Not long before Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON>’s death,
Mr <PERSON>Charles MacConnell</PERSON> died of lung cancer on 24th May 1983. I suspect that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
might have hastened his death by giving an overdose of opiate. I think it likely that he
prescribed more opiate than was needed and kept the excess.
139
11.23 <PERSON>Miss Fox</PERSON>’s death was entirely typical of a Shipman killing; it bears his hallmark. Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON>
was 77 and lived in sheltered accommodation at <ORGANIZATION>Chartist House</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>. She was well
known to the caretaker, Mr <PERSON>Ralph Unsworth</PERSON>, and his wife, who was the warden. Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON>
talked a great deal about her ailments but Mr <PERSON>Unsworth</PERSON> was of the view that she was in
quite good condition for her age. She had changed doctors more than once and it is
likely that she was quite a demanding patient. I am afraid that that would have made her
particularly vulnerable to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. On 28th June, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> found Mr <PERSON>Unsworth</PERSON> and
asked him to come to Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON>’s flat. He said that Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON> had telephoned earlier to
ask him to visit, as she was not feeling well. When he arrived, he said, he had found her
door ajar and could see Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON> lying on the floor behind the door, preventing him from
opening it. He had forced his way in, pushing her to one side. He had examined her and
found that she was dead. When Mr <PERSON>Unsworth</PERSON> and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> reached the flat, Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON>
was lying on her back on the bed, looking as though she had been laid out by an
undertaker. Mr <PERSON>Unsworth</PERSON> was puzzled that Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON>’s clothes did not appear at all
disturbed. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> then behaved rather strangely, as I have described fully in the
decision, requiring Mr <PERSON>Unsworth</PERSON> to examine the body to ensure that Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON> was
indeed dead and addressing her as a ‘ rum old devil’ who had led people on a ‘ merry
dance’. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that the cause of death was a coronary thrombosis. What
convinced me that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had killed Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON> was an entry in his own visits book for
27th June, the day before the death. It says: ‘ Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON> 104 Chartist House blood
tomorrow’. On 28th June the visits book says: ‘ Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON> – bloods – dead’. It is clear
that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> went to see Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON> on 28th June, not because she had summoned him
on account of feeling unwell, but because he had said that he wanted to take a blood
sample. That Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON> should happen to collapse and die on the morning when
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was due to visit to take a blood sample is too much of a coincidence. I am
satisfied that what really happened is that she let him in and he suggested that she
should lie on the bed while he took the blood sample. That is why she did not look at all
disturbed, as might have been expected if <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had had to pick her up off the floor
and put her on the bed. This is the first case in time in which I have found that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
used the excuse of taking a blood sample as an opportunity to give an intravenous
injection and kill the patient. This was to become a frequent practice. Many patients do
not watch their doctor when a blood sample is being taken. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> played on this and
may even have suggested that the patient should turn his or her head away. I am
satisfied that many patients were killed in this way and did not realise that, instead of
taking blood from them, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was in fact administering an injection.
The Period from 1984 to 1989
11.24 During the period that I have just reviewed, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed occasionally and
sporadically. In the period to which I now come, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed quite regularly, between
eight and twelve times a year and, at least until 1988, there were no long intervals
between the deaths.
1984
11.25 After the death of Miss <PERSON>Fox</PERSON>, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not kill again until Saturday, 7th January 1984.
This victim, <PERSON>Mrs Dorothy Tucker</PERSON>, was only 51, but she was overweight and suffered from
140
The Shipman Inquiry
leg ulcers. She could hardly walk and used a wheelchair out of doors. She is another
example of a patient who was probably rather demanding of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s attention. She
might well have been at risk of a heart attack on account of her obesity alone.
<PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON>’s cousin, <PERSON>Mrs Mary Bennett</PERSON>, spoke to <PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON> at about 2pm on
7th January. <PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON> said that she had called the doctor, as she had not been feeling
well. He had given her an injection and told her that she would ‘ feel better in a bit’. She
said that she intended to have a sleep. No one saw or spoke to <PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON> again and
she was found dead in the early evening. The lights were off; the gas fire was on high
and the room was very hot. <PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON> was slumped in the corner of the settee, looking
as if she were asleep. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was called to see the body. He observed that it was
probably ‘ for the best’ that <PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON> should have died rather than continue to suffer.
He certified that the death was due to coronary thrombosis. Although the death was
obviously sudden and had apparently occurred while <PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON> was alone, it was not
referred to the coroner. On the MCCD counterfoil, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he had last seen
Mrs Tucker alive the day before the death.
11.26 Apart from <PERSON>Mrs Bennett</PERSON>’s account that at 2pm <PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON> said that the doctor had just
been, there was no evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> saw <PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON> on 7th January. As it
appeared that <PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON> had been able to speak to <PERSON>Mrs Bennett</PERSON> after the doctor had
given her an injection, it seemed that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> could not have been responsible for the
death, at least not by using what was certainly to become his preferred method of
killing, the intravenous injection of opiate. Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> and Professor <PERSON>McQuay</PERSON> say that
the effect of the drug, given intravenously, is so rapid that the patient would not be able
to have a conversation on the telephone after it had been administered. However, it can
be very difficult to find a vein in a very obese patient and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have decided
to give an intramuscular injection. Alternatively, it is possible that he tried to give an
intravenous injection but the needle slipped out of the vein and the drug entered
<PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON>’s system subcutaneously. In either of those situations, the drug would act
much more slowly and it would be quite feasible to suppose that, if <PERSON>Mrs Bennett</PERSON> had
telephoned within about 15 minutes after the injection, <PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON> would have been
able to answer the telephone and speak sensibly. She might well have begun to feel a
little sleepy, to plan a rest and then to settle on the settee. This is one of only a very few
cases where I have found that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed an active patient by means of a sloweracting
intramuscular or subcutaneous injection. I think he often used the intramuscular
route with patients who were ill in bed. No risk would arise from doing so. The patients
would not go anywhere; no one would feel alarmed if they complained of feeling sick; no
one would summon an ambulance if they fell deeply asleep. The period of time which
would elapse before death would also have the advantage of distancing <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> from
the death. For the patient who was not confined to bed, the slow-acting methods of
killing carried the risk that a friend or relative might arrive, feel alarmed and summon
help in time. The patient might survive and live to tell the tale. However, I think that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> may well have taken that risk in Mrs Tucker’s case.
11.27 This is the first case in time in which witnesses remarked on the high temperature in the
room where the body was found. The gas fire was turned up high. This puzzled me but
I found that this feature was present in such a number of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killings that
141
I concluded that it must be of some significance. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must sometimes have
wanted the body to lie in a warm room. A high ambient temperature would result in the
more rapid onset of rigor mortis but a slower rate of drop in body temperature. These
are the two features on which a pathologist might rely if asked to estimate the time of
death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might wish that the pathologist would not be able to make a reliable
estimate. All I can think is that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> hoped to ‘ muddy the water’ for the pathologist,
in the event that a post-mortem examination followed.
11.28 Not long after <PERSON>Mrs Tucker</PERSON>’s death, there were two cancer deaths. The first occurred in a
residential home from which it would have been difficult for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to take away any
excess drugs, but the second, which took place on 30th January 1984, seems likely to
have been a source of opiates. Just over a week later, on Wednesday, 8th February,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Gladys Roberts</PERSON>. This case is an early example of what became a
very common ploy for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. He would claim that he had called an ambulance or
was arranging admission to hospital but the patient had died and the arrangement had
been cancelled.
11.29 <PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON> was 78 and lived alone. She had a leg ulcer, which was dressed regularly
by the district nurse. On the morning of 8th February, the nurse suggested that the
doctor should examine her leg. At about 12.40pm, <PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON> telephoned her
daughter-in-law, <PERSON>Mrs Enid Roberts</PERSON>, and mentioned that she was expecting the doctor to
look at her leg. She seemed quite well in herself. She promised to telephone again when
the doctor had been. By 3.40pm, she had not done so, so <PERSON>Mrs Enid Roberts</PERSON> telephoned
her. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> answered and told her that <PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON> had died. He said he had been
with her and was telephoning the hospital when <PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON> gave one cough. He had
turned round and saw that she had died. He said she had had a pulmonary embolism.
Because this death occurred so long ago, it has not been possible to check whether
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did telephone the hospital. For more recent deaths in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> made
this claim, it has been possible to check whether he had made the telephone call. He
hardly ever had. In one later case, that of Mrs <PERSON>Hilda Hibbert</PERSON>, he called an ambulance but
not as an emergency. He asked that it should arrive in an hour, by which time
<PERSON>Mrs Hibbert</PERSON> had died. In other cases, such as those of Mrs <PERSON>Lizzie Adams</PERSON> and Mrs
<PERSON>Kathleen Wagstaff</PERSON>, of whose murders <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was convicted, he told the relatives that
he had telephoned for an ambulance. The records showed that he had not.
Accordingly, I strongly suspect that, in cases such as that of <PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON>, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
claimed falsely to have summoned an ambulance as part of his attempt to create the
impression that he had taken appropriate steps to prevent the death. Although I accept
that it is possible that <PERSON>Mrs Roberts</PERSON> might have developed a deep vein thrombosis, it is
too much of a coincidence that she should die suddenly during <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s visit.
11.30 Two months later, on Sunday, 15th April 1984, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mr <PERSON>Joseph Bardsley</PERSON>. He
was a widower, aged 83, and lived alone in sheltered accommodation supervised by
the same warden, <PERSON>Mrs Laithwaite</PERSON>, who had been present on the day of Mrs May <PERSON>Slater</PERSON>’s
death in 1981. Mrs Laithwaite knew Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON> well and saw him every day. She says
that he was in good health on the day of his death. In fact, Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON> suffered from
anaemia and was supposed to have vitamin B12 injections but he did not like them and
had decided that he would not have any more. On that Sunday, Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON>’s cousin
142
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry</ORGANIZATION>
called on him after church and brought him some lunch. She stayed for about an hour.
He was well when she left. At about 3pm, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> called on Mrs Laithwaite to say that
he could not gain access to Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON>. He could see him through the window but
could not attract his attention. <PERSON>Mrs Laithwaite</PERSON>, who knew that Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON> had not asked
for the doctor to visit, asked <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> why he had called on Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON>. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told
her that he had been ‘ in the area’ and wanted to take a blood sample. <PERSON>Mrs Laithwaite</PERSON>
took her key and let herself and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> into Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON>’s bungalow. He was dead,
sitting upright in his usual place on the settee. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not examine Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON>; he
just asked <PERSON>Mrs Laithwaite</PERSON> to inform the next of kin. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that the death was
due to old age. This is not an appropriate cause of death for someone who has been up
and about in reasonable health shortly before the death. He also claimed that he had
seen Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON> 12 days before his death. I doubt that he had done and suspect that
he was seeking to create the impression that he had treated Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON> during his ‘ last
illness’ and was therefore qualified to certify the cause of death. If Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON> had died
naturally that afternoon, it was a sudden death for which the cause was not known. Any
honest doctor would have referred the death to the coroner. I have no doubt
that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> called on Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON> that afternoon, that Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON> let him in and
that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> persuaded him either to let him take a blood sample or to give him an
injection. I am satisfied that he killed him, by giving a lethal injection of opiate. He left,
closing the door behind him. He then pretended to <PERSON>Mrs Laithwaite</PERSON> that he had been
unable to gain access.
11.31 On the afternoon that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON>, he had been called out to see a
patient of Dr Bills named <PERSON>Mrs Jessie Wagstaff</PERSON>. After <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had visited <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON>,
he chose to visit and kill Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON> that Sunday afternoon, rather than return home to
continue his leisure activities.
11.32 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Winifred Arrowsmith</PERSON> on Tuesday, 24th April 1984. She was a widow,
aged 70, and lived alone in a flat in <ORGANIZATION>Chartist House</ORGANIZATION>, the sheltered accommodation where
<PERSON>Mrs Jennifer Unsworth</PERSON> was the warden. <PERSON>Mrs Arrowsmith</PERSON> had a number of medical
problems, including severe arthritis in the knees, which greatly restricted her mobility.
She was mentally very alert and always cheerful. <PERSON>Mrs Arrowsmith</PERSON> was expecting
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to visit on 24th April. She usually left her door on the latch if she was expecting
a visitor, as she was slow on her feet. Between 1.30pm and 2pm, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> telephoned
the home of Mrs <PERSON>Valerie Lomax</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Arrowsmith</PERSON>’s daughter, to tell the family that
<PERSON>Mrs Arrowsmith</PERSON> was dead. His account was that he had called on <PERSON>Mrs Arrowsmith</PERSON>, but
had been unable to get any reply at the door. He had gone down to ask the warden
whether <PERSON>Mrs Arrowsmith</PERSON> might have gone out. <PERSON>Mrs Unsworth</PERSON> took her passkey and
went up to the flat with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. They found <PERSON>Mrs Arrowsmith</PERSON> sitting on her sofa, fully
dressed, looking as though she was asleep. She was dead. When the family visited the
flat the following day, they found signs that <PERSON>Mrs Arrowsmith</PERSON> had done all her usual
chores and had eaten lunch on the day of her death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified the death as due
to coronary thrombosis. In the absence of the medical records, I could not assess the
likelihood that <PERSON>Mrs Arrowsmith</PERSON> might indeed have died a sudden cardiac death.
However, it would have been something of a coincidence if she had had a sudden fatal
heart attack between eating her lunch and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s arrival, which must have been
143
before 1.30pm. The close temporal association between <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s visit and the death
gave rise to a strong suspicion that he had killed her. My conclusion was that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
had visited <PERSON>Mrs Arrowsmith</PERSON> and had gained access in the usual way. He had given her
a lethal injection, probably after asking her to let him take a blood sample. He had then
left the flat, closing the door behind him so as to give the impression that he had been
unable to get in. He then went for <PERSON>Mrs Unsworth</PERSON> and together they ‘ discovered’ the
death. This is exactly the same method he had used with Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON>, only nine days
earlier.
11.33 On 7th August 1984, <PERSON>Mrs Mary Haslam</PERSON> died of cancer. It is likely that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
replenished his stock of opiate at the time of her death. He killed Mrs <PERSON>Mary Winterbottom</PERSON>
on 21st September. <PERSON>Mrs Winterbottom</PERSON> was a widow and lived alone. She was 76 but was
in good general health and was independent. A few days before her death, she was
unwell with what appeared to be influenza or a chest infection. She did not appear to be
seriously ill. On the day of her death, she spoke to her daughter at about 3pm to
3.30pm. She said that she was feeling better and was still waiting for the doctor to arrive.
Soon after 3.30pm, <PERSON>Mrs Norma Miles</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Winterbottom</PERSON>’s niece (who lived nearby)
learned that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was at Mrs <PERSON>Winterbottom</PERSON>’s flat. She went there and met <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>,
who told her that <PERSON>Mrs Winterbottom</PERSON> had died. He asked her if she would like to see her
aunt and then told her that he had taken <PERSON>Mrs Winterbottom</PERSON>’s dentures out. Indeed he
had. <PERSON>Mrs Winterbottom</PERSON>’s body was lying on the bed, dressed in day clothes. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
gave the family the impression that he had found <PERSON>Mrs Winterbottom</PERSON> dead when he
arrived. In due course, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified the cause of death as coronary thrombosis.
On cremation Form B he said that he had last seen Mrs Winterbottom alive two days
before her death. He claimed that he had found her collapsed on the bed and had
unsuccessfully tried to resuscitate her. He said she had been suffering from ischaemic
heart disease. That is quite possible. Even so, it would be a quite remarkable
coincidence if <PERSON>Mrs Winterbottom</PERSON> had had a sudden fatal heart attack in the few minutes
which elapsed after speaking to her daughter on the telephone and just as <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
was about to arrive to see her about an entirely different medical condition. I concluded
that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Winterbottom</PERSON>.
11.34 Two aspects of this case should be highlighted. <PERSON>Mrs Winterbottom</PERSON>’s death was one of
many in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed to have attempted unsuccessfully to resuscitate a
patient who was having a heart attack. Yet he did not summon an ambulance or move
the patient onto a hard surface. The proper procedure is to summon an ambulance;
then to put the patient on a hard surface (usually the floor) and continue with
resuscitation measures until the ambulance arrives. If the ambulance is carrying a
defibrillator, it might then be possible to restore the heart to its normal rhythm. If not,
cardiopulmonary resuscitation should be continued until the patient reaches hospital.
The second matter of interest relates to <PERSON>Mrs Winterbottom</PERSON>’s dentures. I have come
across quite a number of cases in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> took the dentures out of the mouth of
a patient who had died. It does not appear that he did this to all patients who wore
dentures. I do not think there is a single report of a case in which he removed the
dentures of a patient who had died naturally. It seems that he sometimes removed the
dentures of a patient whom he had killed. I found this puzzling, but Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON>
144
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry</ORGANIZATION>
suggested a possible explanation. When a patient has been given a lethal dose of
opiate and falls into a very deep sleep, the relaxation of the muscles might allow the
dentures to become dislodged. If a denture were to slip into the throat, it might cause
the patient to gag and begin to gasp and struggle. This might interfere with the smooth
progress towards death that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> intended. He might, Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> suggests, have
had a bad experience at some time, after which he thought it prudent to remove the
dentures as the patient was falling asleep, if he thought there was a risk of them slipping
into the throat.
11.35 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Ada Ashworth</PERSON> on 27th November 1984. She was a widow who lived
alone and was 87. Her death was a typical <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killing. She was well at about 1pm.
A short time later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> went to her neighbours’ home, claiming that he had arrived
to visit <PERSON>Mrs Ashworth</PERSON> and had found her dead. When the neighbours went in with him,
they saw <PERSON>Mrs Ashworth</PERSON> sitting upright in her chair. Her appearance was typical of that of
many of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> ascribed her death to ‘ old age’, which was quite
inappropriate, as <PERSON>Mrs Ashworth</PERSON> had been up and about that morning.
11.36 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed three patients in the Christmas period of 1984. Mr <PERSON>Joseph Everall</PERSON> died on
17th December, <PERSON>Mrs Edith Wibberley</PERSON> on 18th December and <PERSON>Mrs Eileen Cox</PERSON> on
24th December. All three deaths were typical illustrations of the way in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
killed. Mr <PERSON>Everall</PERSON> was suffering from cancer but had not yet reached the terminal phase
of his illness. He had recently undergone surgery but appeared to be recovering. He
was found dead in the afternoon of 17th December, not long after <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had visited.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that the death was due to cerebro-arteriosclerosis, which, if present,
would pre-dispose the patient to suffering a stroke. Mr <PERSON>Everall</PERSON> was found lying on his
bed and showed no sign of having suffered a stroke. Nor was his family aware of any
symptoms indicative of severe arteriosclerosis. Members of the family were very
shocked by the death but <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> assured them that there was no need for a postmortem
examination and that he would ‘ square it’ with the coroner. In many ways,
Mr <PERSON>Everall</PERSON> was a typical victim. He lived alone, was not in good health and would have
required a good deal of medical and nursing support had he lived longer.
11.37 <PERSON>Mrs Wibberley</PERSON> was a widow who lived alone. She was 76 and was in poor health. She
had had a fall and had broken her hip. She had had a series of transient ischaemic
attacks. She was very dependent on the support of her daughter-in-law and her home
care worker, <PERSON>Mrs Shirley Pleva</PERSON>. On the day before her death, she had another stroke but
appeared stable. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> called and told her that he would return the next day to take
a blood sample. The following morning, <PERSON>Mrs Wibberley</PERSON> appeared to <PERSON>Mrs Pleva</PERSON> to be in
reasonable health. When <PERSON>Mrs Pleva</PERSON> returned after lunch, she found <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> kneeling
on <PERSON>Mrs Wibberley</PERSON>’s bed with her head on his knees and his hand on her neck, as if
feeling for a pulse. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> announced that <PERSON>Mrs Wibberley</PERSON> had had ‘ a massive stroke’.
<PERSON>Mrs Pleva</PERSON> said she would call an ambulance, but <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told her that it would be a
waste of time. He told her to put the kettle on, to contact the relatives and to tell them to
hurry up or Mrs <PERSON>Wibberley</PERSON> would be dead before they arrived. A few minutes later, he
declared her dead. This appears to be another case in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave a lethal
injection under the pretext of taking a blood sample.
145
11.38 <PERSON>Mrs Cox</PERSON> was only 72 when she died. She was a widow and lived alone. She was active
and independent but suffered from angina and bronchitis. Her daughter says that she
was a hypochondriac and used to call <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> out rather frequently. On
24th December, <PERSON>Mrs Cox</PERSON> telephoned her daughter, <PERSON>Mrs Susan Davies</PERSON>, to say that she
was not feeling well; she thought she had bronchitis and had called the doctor, whom
she was expecting at about midday. Shortly after noon, <PERSON>Mrs Davies</PERSON> telephoned her
mother; but there was no reply, so she went round. She found her mother in bed with the
clothes tucked tightly round her chest in a manner which she could not have managed
for herself. She looked as if she were peacefully asleep. She was dead. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was
informed and arrived in due course. After seeing the body, he observed that <PERSON>Mrs Cox</PERSON>
was in exactly the position in which he had left her. He suggested that she must have
died only seconds after he had left. He certified that she had died of a coronary
thrombosis. On cremation Form B, he said that the death had occurred at about 2pm
and that he had seen <PERSON>Mrs Cox</PERSON> two hours before her death, at which time she had
complained of chest pain. He said that she was known to suffer from angina. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
account of this death is incredible. First, no doctor would leave a patient who had a
history of angina alone while suffering from chest pain. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not call an
ambulance or telephone <PERSON>Mrs Davies</PERSON>. In any event, it is clear that <PERSON>Mrs Cox</PERSON> had not had
chest pain; she would have told her daughter if she had. The notion that <PERSON>Mrs Cox</PERSON> could
have died suddenly a few seconds after <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had departed is fanciful. Nor do
patients die of heart attacks lying in bed, looking peaceful and with their arms tucked
inside the covers. It is plain that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Cox</PERSON>. It is also plain that he was
becoming quite confident in giving an explanation which, on rational consideration, can
be seen to be quite implausible. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> seems to have realised that even a highly
improbable explanation would not be questioned.
1985
11.39 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed eleven patients during 1985. On 2nd January 1985, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> hastened
the death of Mr <PERSON>Peter Lewis</PERSON>, who was dying of cancer. He removed the remaining drugs
from Mr <PERSON>Lewis</PERSON>’ home after the death. Mr <PERSON>Frederick Dentith</PERSON> died naturally of cancer on
25th January and I think it is likely that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obtained further supplies of opiate at
that time. Not long after these two cancer deaths, there was a spate of killings.
11.40 Mrs May <PERSON>Brookes</PERSON> was killed on 1st February 1985. She was 74, a widow and lived alone.
She was in reasonable health and spoke to at least two people on the morning of the
day she died, one of them very shortly before <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived for a routine visit at about
1pm. Within half an hour, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> telephoned <PERSON>Mrs Brookes</PERSON>’ daughter to tell her that her
mother was dead. <PERSON>Mrs Brookes</PERSON> was found sitting in her chair, looking quite unruffled.
Later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that he had found <PERSON>Mrs Brookes</PERSON> collapsed on the floor when he
arrived. He said that she was dying. He had lifted her onto her chair. As she weighed
over 13 stone, that seems very unlikely. He certified that the death was due to a
cerebrovascular accident. On Form B he lied, saying that a neighbour had been present
at the moment of death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had almost certainly told <PERSON>Mrs Brookes</PERSON> that he wanted
a blood sample and injected her with an opiate.
146
The Shipman Inquiry
11.41 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mrs <PERSON>Ellen Higson</PERSON> on 4th February 1985. She was 84 and a widow living
alone. She may not have been in good health. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was present at her death, which
seems to have been sudden and unexpected. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that the cause was
renal failure, which is not usually a cause of sudden death. It appears that <PERSON>Mrs Higson</PERSON>’s
home help was present at the death and may even have been present when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
administered the lethal injection.
11.42 Mrs <PERSON>Margaret Conway</PERSON> was killed on 15th February. She was 69 and a widow living alone.
She had had a stroke some time before and had been left with some residual
disabilities. On the day of her death, she spoke to one of her daughters, <PERSON>Mrs Joan
Duncan</PERSON>, at about 2.15pm. She was expecting <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to visit that day. When another
daughter, <PERSON>Mrs Patricia Whittle</PERSON>, arrived at her mother’s house at about 4.15pm, she found
<PERSON>Mrs Conway</PERSON> dead in her chair. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> later certified that the death was due to a
stroke. He gave the family to understand that <PERSON>Mrs Conway</PERSON> had not been ill when he
visited earlier in the afternoon. On cremation Form B, he told lies about the time of his
visit and stated that <PERSON>Mrs Conway</PERSON> had spoken to one of her daughters after his
