visit. These lies were designed to distance his visit from the death and to demonstrate
that <PERSON>Mrs Conway</PERSON> was still alive some time after his departure, which I am quite sure she
was not.
11.43 On 22nd February 1985, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Miss <PERSON>Kathleen McDonald</PERSON>. She was 73 and lived
alone. She was in good physical health and was quite active. She lived in <LOCATION>Carter Place</LOCATION>,
<PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>, where <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had several patients. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> made an unsolicited visit to
<LOCATION>Miss McDonald</LOCATION> during the afternoon of 22nd February. He was later to tell her
neighbour, <PERSON>Mrs Lucy Virgin</PERSON> (who in 1995 was herself to be one of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims)
that it was fortunate that he had called, as he had found Miss <PERSON>McDonald</PERSON> dying. He
said that she had ‘ died in his arms’. He certified that her death was due to a
cerebrovascular accident.
11.44 Four months later, on 26th June 1985, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed two patients on the same day,
Mr <PERSON>Thomas Moult</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mrs Mildred Robinson</PERSON>. The evidence in respect of Mr Moult’s
death is very limited. However, I drew the inference that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed him because the
documentary evidence indicated that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was probably present at the death. As
Mr <PERSON>Moult</PERSON> was only 70 and was not expected to die, I felt that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s presence at the
death was sufficient to draw me to my conclusion. The evidence in Mrs <PERSON>Mildred
Robinson</PERSON>’s case is complex and cannot be conveniently summarised. I concluded that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was present at the death and had probably caused it. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that
both deaths were due to coronary thrombosis and that chronic bronchitis was said to
have been a contributory cause in each case.
11.45 On 23rd August 1985, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Miss <PERSON>Frances Turner</PERSON>. She was 85 and was quite
frail following a fall in which she had fractured her hip. She did not wish to go into
residential care and had recently been assessed for the provision of support at home.
I think her insistence on retaining her independence would have made her a likely target
for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s visits book suggests that he was to make a routine visit on the
day of her death. There is nothing to indicate that Miss <PERSON>Turner</PERSON> was suffering a lifethreatening
illness. It is clear from cremation Form B that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was present at the
147
death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that the death was due to old age, although this was quite
inappropriate. Miss <PERSON>Turner</PERSON> had not been confined to bed before her death and was still
living alone.
11.46 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed three patients during the Christmas period of 1985. These were
Mrs <PERSON>Selina Mackenzie</PERSON> who died on 17th December, Miss <PERSON>Vera Bramwell</PERSON>, who died on
20th December, and Mr <PERSON>Fred Kellett</PERSON>, who died on 31st December. It is not clear from
where <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had obtained his supply of opiates, although he had been briefly
involved in the care of one of Dr <PERSON>Moysey</PERSON>’s patients, <PERSON>Mrs Mary Ogden</PERSON>, who had died a
natural death of cancer on 17th October. It is quite possible that he had obtained opiates
in connection with her death. In any event, I think it likely that, by 1985, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was
more confident about his ability to obtain and conceal illicit supplies of opiate. I think, by
this time, he had probably turned to other means of obtaining his supplies than simply
taking excess supplies from patients who had died of cancer. It is quite possible that he
had begun to prescribe opiates for patients who did not need the drugs and never
received them.
11.47 <PERSON>Mrs Mackenzie</PERSON> was a widow who lived alone. She was 77, had had two strokes and was
quite disabled. However, she was determined to remain in her own home and was
receiving considerable support from social services and from her family. Patients
requiring this level of support appear to have been particularly at risk from <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. On
17th December, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> visited, ostensibly for the purpose of checking that
<PERSON>Mrs Mackenzie</PERSON> was well enough to travel to her daughter’s home in <LOCATION>Nottingham</LOCATION> for
Christmas. He was seen arriving at her home at about 3pm. She was found dead at
about 3.45pm, sitting in her chair. No one had seen her or spoken to her since
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s visit. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that she had died at about 4pm and certified
the cause of death as a cerebrovascular accident. He offered to arrange a postmortem
examination if the relatives wished, but then pointed out the disadvantages. As
I have previously observed, this was one of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s common ploys.
11.48 Miss <PERSON>Bramwell</PERSON> was 79 and lived alone in sheltered accommodation. She had been in
good health and went shopping on the morning of the day of her death. It appears likely
that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was asked to visit her, although it is possible that he made an unsolicited
visit. In any event, he told the warden that, when he arrived, he found Miss <PERSON>Bramwell</PERSON>
dead in her chair. Inconsistently with that, on cremation Form B, he admitted that he had
been present at the death, which he attributed to a coronary thrombosis. This was yet
another coronary thrombosis which happened to occur in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s presence. Yet he
did not call an ambulance. He claimed that the warden of the sheltered accommodation
was present at the death, when the other evidence suggests that she was not.
It appears likely that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was alone with Miss <PERSON>Bramwell</PERSON> when she died.
11.49 Mr <PERSON>Kellett</PERSON> was 79 when he died. His wife was living in a nursing home in <LOCATION>Denton</LOCATION>. He was
in quite good health and visited her regularly. On the day of his death, his niece,
<PERSON>Mrs Valerie Wood</PERSON>, had asked <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to visit him, as she was concerned that he had a
cough. She was speaking to her uncle on the telephone at about 11am, when he told
her that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had just arrived. About 45 minutes later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> telephoned
<PERSON>Mrs Wood</PERSON> to tell her that her uncle had died. His explanation was that Mr <PERSON>Kellett</PERSON> ‘ had
148
The Shipman Inquiry
just sat down in his chair and died’. Later, Mr <PERSON>Kellett</PERSON> was seen sitting in his chair; he
looked very peaceful. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that the death was due to coronary thrombosis.
1986
11.50 In 1986, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed eight patients. The first was Mrs <PERSON>Deborah Middleton</PERSON> on
7th January. <PERSON>Mrs Middleton</PERSON> was 81 and lived with her daughter, <PERSON>Mrs Barbara Taylor</PERSON>. She
suffered from bronchitis in the winter but was still very active. She looked after her greatgrandchildren
every afternoon when they came out of school. On the day of
<PERSON>Mrs Middleton</PERSON>’s death, her daughter telephoned the surgery to ask <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to visit her
mother, who had been complaining of feeling tired. She did not seem to be seriously ill
and <PERSON>Mrs Taylor</PERSON> went to work as usual. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> visited in the afternoon, probably at
about 3pm. No one saw <PERSON>Mrs Middleton</PERSON> alive after his visit. She was found dead at about
4.15pm. She was sitting in her chair looking peaceful. She had made all her usual
preparations for the children’s arrival from school. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s explanation was that she
had died of cardiac failure after his visit. On Form B, he said that the death had
occurred at 5pm, which was clearly an attempt to distance the death from his visit. He
lied on Form B, saying that <PERSON>Mrs Middleton</PERSON>’s granddaughter, <PERSON>Mrs Jacqueline Slaney</PERSON>, had
been present at the moment of death. She was not.
11.51 The death of Mrs <PERSON>Dorothy Fletcher</PERSON> on 23rd April 1986 was not typical of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
pattern of killing. <PERSON>Mrs Fletcher</PERSON> lived in <LOCATION>Charnley House</LOCATION>, a residential home for the
elderly. She was only 74 but suffered from dementia. The daily diary of <ORGANIZATION>Charnley House</ORGANIZATION>
shows that, for a week or two before her death, she had had a chest infection and was
treated with an antibiotic. There were also some signs that she had heart failure. Her
condition deteriorated gradually and it seemed that her death was imminent. On the
morning of 23rd April, she was still poorly and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was sent for. It is not clear
whether or not he came at his usual time, which was about lunchtime. In any event,
<PERSON>Mrs Fletcher</PERSON> rallied, seemed much better and ate a ‘ full lunch’. There is then no further
account until the daily diary records that she died at 6.30pm. The diary suggests that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not come to the home until after the death, but the time of his visit appears
to have been left blank and completed later. On Form B, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he saw
<PERSON>Mrs Fletcher</PERSON> at 6pm and she died at 6.20pm. I concluded that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had been
present at the death, a situation I had come to regard as suspicious. The full reasoning
appears in the decision. I think it likely that everyone had thought <PERSON>Mrs Fletcher</PERSON>’s death
was imminent but that she then confounded them by making a recovery. I think <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
would not have wished her to recover and probably ensured that she died that evening
by giving her an injection of opiate.
11.52 On 6th June 1986, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mr <PERSON>Thomas Fowden</PERSON>. He was 81 and lived alone. He
was quite independent and ran his house with the assistance of a home help, <PERSON>Mrs Joan
Ralphs</PERSON>, whose mother, <PERSON>Mrs Lily Higgins</PERSON>, and mother-in-law, <PERSON>Mrs Anne Ralphs</PERSON>, were later
to be victims of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. On 6th June, Mrs Ralphs found Mr <PERSON>Fowden</PERSON> unwell and went to
call the doctor and to telephone Mr <PERSON>Fowden</PERSON>’s nephew, Mr <PERSON>Edwin Fowden</PERSON>. She was only
away a few minutes but, when she returned, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was there, standing over
Mr <PERSON>Fowden</PERSON>, who by that time was either dead or unconscious. Soon afterwards,
Mr <PERSON>Edwin Fowden</PERSON> arrived. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told him that he had given his uncle an injection ‘ to
149
help his breathing’ but that Mr <PERSON>Fowden</PERSON> was ‘ practically gone’. A short while later,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> pronounced Mr <PERSON>Fowden</PERSON> dead.
11.53 In September and October 1986, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed two patients in remarkably similar
circumstances. Both lived on <LOCATION>Thorpe Hall Grove</LOCATION>, <LOCATION>Newton</LOCATION>. On 15th September, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
killed Miss <PERSON>Mona White</PERSON>. She was only 63 and was quite well, although she suffered from
arthritis and angina. She lived alone and had the assistance of a home help once or
twice a week. On 15th September, she was expecting <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>; it is not clear why.
However, when <PERSON>Mrs Elizabeth Shawcross</PERSON> and Mrs <PERSON>Dorothy Foley</PERSON> (the two home helps
who worked in that area) passed by her house at about noon, they saw and spoke to
her, as she was at her door. She was looking out for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> and did not seem to be
very ill. A short time later, <PERSON>Mrs Shawcross</PERSON> spoke to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, who was at his car, outside
Miss White’s house. He told her that he had given Miss White a ‘ little injection for her
pain’. About twenty minutes later, <PERSON>Mrs Shawcross</PERSON> called on Miss White to see if she was
all right. She found her sitting in her chair, dead. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was not there. <PERSON>Mrs Shawcross</PERSON>
ran to fetch <PERSON>Mrs Foley</PERSON> and, by the time they arrived back at <LOCATION>Miss White’s</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had
reappeared. He told them Miss White was dead. He had put the kettle on and was
making himself a cup of tea. He asked the home helps to telephone Miss White’s sister,
<PERSON>Mrs Alison Forder</PERSON>. She and her husband arrived at 12.55pm. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told <PERSON>Mrs Forder</PERSON>
that when he arrived to see <PERSON>Miss White</PERSON>, she was having a massive heart attack and he
knew she was going to die. He had sat with her until she died. It would have been no
use calling an ambulance. There was no need for a post-mortem examination. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
certified the death as due to a coronary thrombosis.
11.54 Only three weeks later, <PERSON>Mrs Mary Tomlin</PERSON>, another client of <PERSON>Mrs Shawcross</PERSON> and
<PERSON>Mrs Foley</PERSON>, was killed. The two home helps had visited <PERSON>Mrs Tomlin</PERSON> at lunchtime. They
found her in bed. She said she was not well and was expecting <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to visit. They
left to continue work, promising to return to fetch her prescription. About an hour
afterwards, <PERSON>Mrs Foley</PERSON> saw <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> go into <PERSON>Mrs Tomlin</PERSON>’s flat. About ten minutes later she
went to the flat and met <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, who told her that <PERSON>Mrs Tomlin</PERSON> was ‘ going’ and
instructed her to put the kettle on. A few minutes later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> joined <PERSON>Mrs Foley</PERSON> in the
living room and told her that <PERSON>Mrs Tomlin</PERSON> was dead. <PERSON>Mrs Tomlin</PERSON> was only 73. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
attributed her death to a coronary thrombosis. It is clear that he had killed her.
11.55 On 17th November 1986, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Beatrice Toft</PERSON>. She was only 59 but she
suffered from emphysema and chronic obstructive airways disease and had an oxygen
cylinder by her bed. She could not do a great deal for herself and was very dependent
on her family. On the day of her death, the meals on wheels service delivered her lunch
and she ate it. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> visited at some time in the afternoon. Mrs <PERSON>Toft</PERSON> was found dead
by one of her daughters at about 4.20pm. She was lying flat on her back in bed, a
position which would have been most uncomfortable for her in life; she usually lay
propped against pillows to ease her breathing. Her dentures were not in her mouth. An
ambulance was summoned; <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> also arrived. He told the paramedics that he had
seen Mrs <PERSON>Toft</PERSON> at 2.30pm that day and that there was no need for them to stay. The
practice is that if a doctor is in attendance at a sudden death and indicates that he is
prepared to certify the cause of death, the ambulance personnel (and police if present)
take no further steps in connection with the death. One of <PERSON>Mrs Toft</PERSON>’s daughters asked
150
The Shipman Inquiry
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> what could have happened to <PERSON>Mrs Toft</PERSON>’s dentures. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> suggested that
she might have swallowed them. No one saw <PERSON>Mrs Toft</PERSON> alive after <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s visit and in
my view it is clear that he had killed her. I think he must also have removed her dentures.
11.56 There were two killings just before Christmas 1986: <PERSON>Mrs Lily Broadbent</PERSON> on
16th December and Mr <PERSON>James Wood</PERSON> on 23rd December. Both died in circumstances
entirely typical of a Shipman killing. <PERSON>Mrs Broadbent</PERSON>’s death was attributed to a coronary
thrombosis, which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed had occurred shortly after he had visited her. She
was found with the gas fire on very high. Mr <PERSON>Wood</PERSON>’s death was attributed to old age,
although he was up and about on the day of his death. He was not seen alive after
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> visited that day.
1987
11.57 In 1987, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed eight patients. On 30th March, he killed Mr <PERSON>Frank Halliday</PERSON>.
Mr <PERSON>Halliday</PERSON> was 76 and in poor health. His sister lived with him but, at the time of his
death, she was spending a week or two in <LOCATION>Scotland</LOCATION>. On the day of his death, a
neighbour asked <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to visit Mr <PERSON>Halliday</PERSON>, as he was not well. Mr <PERSON>Halliday</PERSON> died in the
course of that visit. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s entry in the medical records suggests that Mr <PERSON>Halliday</PERSON>
had been complaining of chest pain for two days. On examination, he was showing
signs from which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> diagnosed a coronary thrombosis. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> supposedly
gave an intravenous injection of 10mg morphine. There is no further description of the
manner in which this was given. The note then says that the neighbour was called. An
ambulance was called and cancelled, as Mr <PERSON>Halliday</PERSON> had died. On cremation Form B,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he had been with Mr <PERSON>Halliday</PERSON> for an hour during his last illness and
that Mr <PERSON>Halliday</PERSON> had been in a coma for 30 minutes before death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account is
obviously false. First, if Mr <PERSON>Halliday</PERSON> had had chest pain for two days, his sister would
have been told and she would have returned from <LOCATION>Scotland</LOCATION>. Second, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
frequently claimed that he had called an ambulance but had cancelled it because the
patient died before it arrived. In this case, putting the medical record together with Form
B, it appears that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had almost an hour in which to call an ambulance before
Mr <PERSON>Halliday</PERSON> died.
11.58 This is the first case I have considered in which the general practitioner records have
survived. These records are also the first of several which contain a note in which
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed to have given a small dose of morphine to relieve the pain of a
coronary thrombosis. Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> says that it is good practice to give a small dose of
opiate to a patient who is in severe pain during a heart attack. However, the dose should
be given very slowly, titrating the amount given against the effect on the patient’s pain,
so that the injection can be stopped as soon as the pain is adequately relieved. The
doctor’s note should say how much was given and over what period. In all the cases in
which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> has claimed to have given morphine for the pain of a heart attack, I have
never seen any suggestion that anything less than the full dose has been given and
I have never seen an estimate of the time over which it was given. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> almost
always claimed to give either 10mg morphine, as here, or 5mg diamorphine, which is
the equivalent. I have seen such entries on several occasions, and in each case, the
patient has died. I believe that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave a much larger dose than 10mg morphine.
151
I do not know whether Mr <PERSON>Halliday</PERSON> was having a heart attack when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived. He
might have been and it may be that it would have been fatal. On the other hand,
Mr <PERSON>Halliday</PERSON> might not have been having a heart attack at all. Whatever was the matter
with him, I think it likely that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave him a lethal dose of opiate. The entry
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> made in the medical records is typical of many I have seen in other
cases where he has killed the patient. This type of record is one of the hallmarks of a
Shipman killing.
11.59 Only two days later, on 1st April 1987, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mr <PERSON>Albert Cheetham</PERSON>, probably
during the early evening, when he was on out of hours duty. He left the body overnight,
with the gas fire on full. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was unable to lock the door behind him. The next
morning he went back to ‘ discover’ the body. He gave a false explanation about the
circumstances of the death. He said that he had called, as he ‘ happened to be in the
area’ and had found Mr <PERSON>Cheetham</PERSON> in a ‘ nervy’ state. He had promised to return the next
day to give him a prescription. Why he could not have done that on the first day is hard
to understand. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> suggested that Mr <PERSON>Cheetham</PERSON> must have sat down to watch
‘ Coronation Street’ and died.
11.60 On 16th April 1987, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Alice Thomas</PERSON>. She was 83 and in poor health.
She had had a number of strokes. She died during a routine visit by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>.
11.61 On 8th May, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mrs <PERSON>Jane Rostron</PERSON>. She was 78. On the day of her death, it
appears that a neighbour requested that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> should visit because <PERSON>Mrs Rostron</PERSON>
was not feeling well. He clearly did visit, probably at about lunchtime. <PERSON>Mrs Rostron</PERSON>’s
daughter found her dead when she visited her mother in the mid-afternoon. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
explanation was that, when he called on her, he found that she had suffered a ‘ slight
stroke’. He had urged her to be admitted to hospital but she had refused. <PERSON>Mrs Rostron</PERSON>’s
daughter found that quite credible. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> suggested that, if <PERSON>Mrs Rostron</PERSON> had taken
his advice, she might still be alive. For reasons fully set out in the decision, I found that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had killed <PERSON>Mrs Rostron</PERSON>. This is an early example of what was to become one of
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s favourite ploys; he blamed the death on the patient’s refusal to go to hospital.
11.62 <PERSON>Mrs Nancy Brassington</PERSON> was <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s next victim. She was 71 and had had a stroke,
from which she was said to have made a full recovery. At about lunchtime on
14th September 1987, a neighbour saw <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s car outside <PERSON>Mrs Brassington</PERSON>’s
house. A while later, she noticed <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> leave the house but <PERSON>Mrs Brassington</PERSON> did not
see him off, which she thought was strange. As soon as <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had gone, she and her
husband went across and found <PERSON>Mrs Brassington</PERSON> sitting in her chair, dead. This was a
classic <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killing.
11.63 Once again, there was a cluster of killings around Christmas. On 11th December,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Margaret Townsend</PERSON>. She was 80 and had a complicated medical
history. She was, I think, a rather demanding patient and might at times have
exaggerated her symptoms. She had been extensively investigated at hospital on
account of abdominal pain but no cause had been found. She died during a home visit
by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. He attributed her death to carcinomatosis due to carcinoma of the
stomach. Whatever had been the cause of <PERSON>Mrs Townsend</PERSON>’s pain, it was not due to
carcinoma of the stomach, which had been ruled out during investigations.
152
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry</ORGANIZATION>
11.64 On 29th December 1987, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Nellie Bardsley</PERSON>. She was only 69. On the
morning of the day she died, she told her daughter that she felt unwell and wanted the
doctor to call. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> visited later in the day. <PERSON>Mrs Bardsley</PERSON>’s medical records are
available and it can be seen that the circumstances of her death are very similar to
those of Mr <PERSON>Frank Halliday</PERSON>. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that he found her having a coronary
thrombosis and gave her 10mg morphine. Later, he told <PERSON>Mrs Bardsley</PERSON>’s daughter,
<PERSON>Mrs Carol Chapman</PERSON>, that he had called an ambulance but had cancelled it, as
<PERSON>Mrs Bardsley</PERSON> had died before it arrived. This last assertion cannot be checked, as the
ambulance records are no longer available.
11.65 On the following day, 30th December 1987, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Elizabeth Rogers</PERSON>.
She was 74 and lived alone in a flat in <ORGANIZATION>Chartist House</ORGANIZATION>. She was very independent. On
30th December, it appears that she telephoned <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to ask him to visit; it is not clear
why, but his visits book does not indicate that it was a matter of urgency. He visited her
and killed her. He then went downstairs to see <PERSON>Mrs Unsworth</PERSON>, told her that he was
admitting <PERSON>Mrs Rogers</PERSON> to hospital and asked her to come upstairs with him to help
<PERSON>Mrs Rogers</PERSON> to pack her necessary things. When they reached the flat <PERSON>Mrs Rogers</PERSON> was,
of course, dead. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> used this kind of ruse, or variants upon it, many times. He
liked to have someone with him when the death was discovered.
1988
11.66 In the New Year of 1988, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Elizabeth Fletcher</PERSON> on 5th January and
<PERSON>Mrs Alice Mary Jones</PERSON> on 15th January. <PERSON>Mrs Fletcher</PERSON> was 90 but very active. She looked
after her granddaughter, who had learning difficulties. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> called on her ‘ as he was
passing’. He claimed that he found her dying and sat with her until it was over. That was
a lie, as was his claim on cremation Form B that <PERSON>Mrs Fletcher</PERSON>’s sister-in-law had been
present at the death.
11.67 <PERSON>Mrs Jones</PERSON> had very poor eyesight. Shortly before her death, she had had an ailment for
which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had visited several times. She was reasonably well on the morning of
her death, but when her sister-in-law arrived between noon and 1pm, she found
<PERSON>Mrs Jones</PERSON> dead in her chair. The torch and magnifying glass she used for reading were
in her hands. An ambulance was called and the paramedics said that the police and
coroner must be informed, but then <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived and said that that would not be
necessary. He said he had seen Mrs <PERSON>Jones</PERSON> at 11am. He certified the death as due to a
stroke. On cremation Form B, he said that the death had occurred at noon and he had
seen Mrs <PERSON>Jones</PERSON> at 10.30am. He was distancing his visit from the death.
11.68 A short time after these two deaths, there was a spate of killings within a week in
February 1988. Mrs <PERSON>Dorothea Renwick</PERSON> was killed on 9th February, Miss <PERSON>Ann Cooper</PERSON>
(who was 93 and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s oldest victim) and Mrs <PERSON>Jane Jones</PERSON> on 15th February, and
Mrs <PERSON>Lavinia Robinson</PERSON> on the following day, 16th February. <PERSON>Mrs Renwick</PERSON> was 90 and had
had a stroke but was well and was eating her lunch when her daughter left her on the
day of her death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> visited soon afterwards to provide repeat prescriptions.
<PERSON>Mrs Renwick</PERSON> was found dead in the early evening. The gas fire was on high. The next
day, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> suggested that the death was ‘ a good thing’, as <PERSON>Mrs Renwick</PERSON>’s daughter
would now be able to look after herself.
153
11.69 Miss <PERSON>Cooper</PERSON> was 93 but was in good health and lived independently. Her niece asked
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to call as she thought Miss <PERSON>Cooper</PERSON> was becoming confused. He visited that
afternoon and found Miss <PERSON>Cooper</PERSON> doing some washing. He later claimed that he had
advised her to see a specialist and that she had been well when he left. Miss <PERSON>Cooper</PERSON>
was found dead not long after he left. It is clear he had killed her.
11.70 On 15th February 1988, <PERSON>Mrs Jones</PERSON> called <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> out, as she wanted an antibiotic for
her cold and bad chest. When her niece, <PERSON>Mrs Vera Panther</PERSON>, called on her at about
10.30am, she did not appear to be seriously ill. At about 12.30pm, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived at
Mrs Panther’s home to say that he had found <PERSON>Mrs Jones</PERSON> in a state of collapse. He had
given her an injection to ‘ help her breathing’. She was now very poorly and he asked
Mrs Panther to come to her aunt’s home. When they arrived, she was dead.
11.71 <PERSON>Mrs Robinson</PERSON>’s death was extremely sudden. She was 83 but was fit and active. She
lived in <ORGANIZATION>Chartist House</ORGANIZATION>. On 16th February 1988, her son called on her in the early
afternoon. When he left at 2.15pm, she was well. At about 3pm, he received a message
that his mother had died. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had called.
11.72 After this spate of four killings within a week, there was an interval of seven months.
There is no clear evidence as to why <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> stopped killing for so long. However, it is
quite possible that the occurrence of four sudden deaths within the week had caused
comment, possibly from a doctor or member of staff at the <LOCATION>Donneybrook</LOCATION> practice. I think
it likely that there was some such explanation for this temporary cessation. When
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed again, on 18th September 1988, his victim, <PERSON>Mrs Rose Adshead</PERSON>, was very
ill, suffering from cancer and was in great pain. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> hastened her death. I have
already observed that the killing of a terminally ill patient entails much less risk of
discovery than the killing of a healthy patient. The following month, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> resumed
the killing of patients who were not terminally ill. He killed <PERSON>Mrs Alice Prestwich</PERSON> on
20th October and Mr <PERSON>Walter Tingle</PERSON> on 6th November.
11.73 <PERSON>Mrs Prestwich</PERSON>’s death exemplifies several features typical of a Shipman killing. She was
69 and awaiting an eye operation. I believe that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> took the view that it was not
‘ worth’ spending the resources of the health service on patients who were old, would not
live long and, in his view, would not enjoy a good quality of life. I think he probably
thought that of Mrs Prestwich. He called on her in her flat in <LOCATION>Ogden Court</LOCATION>, at the request
of the warden, <PERSON>Mrs Christine Simpson</PERSON>, who had noticed that morning that
<PERSON>Mrs Prestwich</PERSON>’s legs were swollen. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Prestwich</PERSON> while he was alone
with her; then he went to fetch <PERSON>Mrs Simpson</PERSON>. He told <PERSON>Mrs Simpson</PERSON> the implausible tale
that <PERSON>Mrs Prestwich</PERSON> had ‘ died while I was examining her’. As they were leaving the flat
together, <PERSON>Mrs Simpson</PERSON> expressed incredulity that <PERSON>Mrs Prestwich</PERSON> could have died so
suddenly. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> replied ‘ We’ll go back and check her if you like. I wouldn’t want her
waking up in the Chapel of Rest’.
11.74 Mr <PERSON>Walter Tingle</PERSON> was another typical <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> victim. He lived in sheltered
accommodation. He was 85 and in poor health. He was also depressed about his
health. He sometimes said that he ‘ had had enough’. On the morning of 6th November
1988, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was called and when he arrived a warden was present. Mr <PERSON>Tingle</PERSON> almost
certainly told <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> that he ‘ had had enough’. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> may have contrived to
154
The Shipman Inquiry
send the warden away. He gave Mr <PERSON>Tingle</PERSON> an injection. Within a few minutes, Mr <PERSON>Tingle</PERSON>
was dying.
11.75 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed two patients just before Christmas, making a total of 11 killings in 1988.
Both patients were due to spend Christmas with their families. At about 9.30am on
Saturday, 17th December, Mr <PERSON>Harry Stafford</PERSON> telephoned his daughter-in-law to say that
he had a cold and had called the doctor. At about 10.30am, <PERSON>Mrs Brenda McEvilly</PERSON>, one of
Mr <PERSON>Stafford</PERSON>’s neighbours, saw <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrive at Mr <PERSON>Stafford</PERSON>’s house. A while later, she
saw <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> leave the house and knock at the doors of three neighbouring houses,
with no apparent response. He then came to her house. He told her that Mr <PERSON>Stafford</PERSON> had
called him out and had said that he would leave the door unlocked. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that
Mr <PERSON>Stafford</PERSON> was very unwell and asked <PERSON>Mrs McEvilly</PERSON> to go with him to Mr <PERSON>Stafford</PERSON>’s
house. When they arrived, <PERSON>Mr Stafford</PERSON> was sitting up in his armchair. He was dead.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> apologised to <PERSON>Mrs McEvilly</PERSON> that he had not told her the truth when he had said
that Mr <PERSON>Stafford</PERSON> was very unwell. Soon afterwards, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> telephoned Mr <PERSON>Stafford</PERSON>’s
daughter-in-law to tell her of the death. He said that, soon after he had arrived at the
house, Mr <PERSON>Stafford</PERSON> had collapsed and died. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> fabricated an entry in the medical
records to suggest that, when he had arrived, Mr <PERSON>Stafford</PERSON> had given a history of chest
pain and breathlessness for two days. On examination, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had found signs from
which he diagnosed a coronary thrombosis with left ventricular failure. Mr <PERSON>Stafford</PERSON> had
died. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed he had given intravenous injections of Cyclimorph (injectable
morphine; the dose was not stated) and 80mg Lasix, a strong diuretic. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told the
family that he arrived to find Mr <PERSON>Stafford</PERSON> having a ‘ massive’ heart attack and there was
nothing he could do.
11.76 Two days later, the nephew of Miss <PERSON>Ethel Bennett</PERSON> called on her between 11am and
noon. She told him she was expecting <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to call. At about 2.45pm, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> went
to the home of Mrs <PERSON>Susan Cropper</PERSON>, Miss <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON>’s great-niece, to visit her son, <PERSON>Daniel</PERSON>,
who was unwell. He told Mrs Cropper that Miss <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON> was unwell, had pleurisy and
ought to be in hospital but she had refused to go. He asked Mrs Cropper to go round to
persuade her. When <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had gone, Mrs Cropper tried to telephone her great-aunt
but there was no reply. She could not leave her son, so asked her father, Mr <PERSON>Alan
Bennett</PERSON>, to go round. He went to Miss <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON>’s home at about 6pm and found her
dead. The gas fire was on full. When <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived, Mr <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON> suggested that the
police should be called. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that this was not necessary and there was no
need for a post-mortem examination. He repeated that Miss <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON> had refused to go
to hospital. He fabricated an entry in the medical records, which suggested that
Miss <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON> had had left-sided chest pain for seven days. He claimed that, after
examining Miss <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON>, he had diagnosed pleurisy and advised hospital admission but
she had refused. He claimed that he had prescribed an antibiotic. Miss <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON> had
been found dead later in the day and the cause of death was bronchopneumonia.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> lied on Form B. He said that he had visited at 1pm and that the death occurred
at 4pm. If the death were natural, he could not have known the time at which it occurred.
He was seeking to distance the death from his visit. He claimed that a neighbour had
heard Miss <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON> moving about at 3pm. That was a lie, designed to show that
Miss <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON> was alive some time after his visit. In fact, it was an implausible lie as, if
155
Miss <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON> had died of bronchopneumonia at 4pm, she was unlikely to have been
moving about an hour earlier.
1989
11.77 From this time onwards, medical records have in some cases, although not all, been
available to the Inquiry.
11.78 In 1989, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed 12 patients. The first killing of the year took place on
31st January, when he killed Mr <PERSON>Wilfred Leigh</PERSON>. On 8th March 1989, for the first time,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed a patient in the surgery. This was Mrs <PERSON>Mary Hamer</PERSON>. She was 81 and in
good health. She had an appointment during the morning. It is not known for what
reason. However, she looked quite ‘ normal’ when she went into the consulting room.
A few minutes later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told the receptionist to send the next patient in, as
Mrs <PERSON>Hamer</PERSON> was undressing in the examination room and would be some time. He
finished his list of two or three more patients before telling the receptionist that he
thought <PERSON>Mrs Hamer</PERSON> had died. The receptionist went into the examination room and saw
<PERSON>Mrs Hamer</PERSON> on the bed, fully dressed. She was dead. Later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told <PERSON>Mrs Hamer</PERSON>’s
family that, when she came into the consulting room, she told him she had chest pain.
He examined her and thought she was having a coronary thrombosis. He advised that
she should be taken to hospital and she agreed. He gave her a small dose of morphine
to relieve her pain and went to telephone the hospital. When he returned, she had died.
He said that he had not attempted to resuscitate her, as he thought she had suffered
brain damage. He certified the cause of her death and said that a post-mortem
examination was not necessary.
11.79 <PERSON>Mrs Josephine Hall</PERSON>, who died on 5th June, had a long psychiatric history. She suffered
from agoraphobia, insomnia, depression and anxiety. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was intolerant of what he
seems to have seen as her weakness. He once said that he would like to treat her
agoraphobia by taking her into <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> and leaving her there, so that she would have to
make her own way home. Mrs Hall made quite frequent demands on <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s time.
I fear that this would have made her a preferred target for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. In April 1988,
following a psychiatric referral at which the consultant reported that <PERSON>Mrs Hall</PERSON> was ‘ fed
up with life’, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> noted in her records: ‘ No use, what do we do?’ <PERSON>Mrs Hall</PERSON> also
took medication to control her blood pressure. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed her during a routine visit.
In the fabricated medical record, he claimed that he had found her with symptoms of a
stroke or transient ischaemic attack. He claimed that her blood pressure was very high,
which he attributed to her failure to take her medication. He claimed that he was
considering a domiciliary visit by a consultant and that he would review her the next
day. <PERSON>Mrs Hall</PERSON> was found dead later that afternoon. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that the cause of
death was a cerebrovascular accident. It is typical of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> that he would, where
possible, choose to attribute the death to a cause that had some basis in the past
medical history.
11.80 On 12th May 1989, Miss <PERSON>Beatrice Clee</PERSON> was killed. She was frail and had poor eyesight
but lived independently. She was well at about 10am on 12th May. She was found dead
at about 3pm, sitting upright in her armchair, apparently asleep. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had been
there in the interim. He made a medical record suggesting that he had visited on
156
The Shipman Inquiry
account of Miss <PERSON>Clee</PERSON>’s leg ulcer, which was satisfactory. He then fabricated entries to
suggest that Miss <PERSON>Clee</PERSON> had complained of symptoms of heart trouble. It is clear that
these were designed to provide a plausible explanation for her sudden death.
11.81 On 6th July 1989, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mrs <PERSON>Hilda Fitton</PERSON> during an unsolicited visit. She had
seen him in his surgery the day before on account of a chest condition, for which
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not think treatment was necessary. The following day, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> called on
Mrs <PERSON>Fitton</PERSON> and killed her. He fabricated a note in her medical records to suggest that
she had woken in the night with palpitations, chest pain and breathlessness. He claimed
that she was very worried but on examination there was little abnormality. He thought
she might be having a heart attack and suggested hospital but <PERSON>Mrs Fitton</PERSON> did not wish to
be admitted. He wrote the letters ‘ TLUK’, which meant ‘ to let us know’, and noted that
he would visit her the following day. This false account was intended to create the
impression that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had taken proper care of the patient but that the death, when
discovered, was explicable. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> often used this type of ploy, particularly with the
letters ‘ TLUK’. However, the ploy was not a very convincing one as, if <PERSON>Mrs Fitton</PERSON> had
really described palpitations, chest pain and breathlessness, good medical practice
would have required that some arrangement be made for her care, either by calling a
relative or arranging the attendance of the district nurse. No doctor would leave
a patient alone suffering from the symptoms that <PERSON>Mrs Fitton</PERSON> supposedly described. In
those circumstances, no doctor would leave the onus on the patient to let him know if
she needed attention.
11.82 On 14th August, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Marion Carradice</PERSON>. On 22nd September, he killed
<PERSON>Mrs Elsie Harrop</PERSON> and, only four days later, on 26th September, <PERSON>Mrs Elizabeth Burke</PERSON>.
There were then three killings within three days in October 1989. These were Mrs <PERSON>Sarah
Jane Williamson</PERSON> on 15th October, Mr <PERSON>John Charlton</PERSON> on 16th October and Mr <PERSON>George
Vizor</PERSON> on 18th October. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was called out to <PERSON>Mrs Williamson</PERSON> on a Sunday
afternoon. She was very poorly and might well have been having a heart attack.
However, it appears that, instead of treating <PERSON>Mrs Williamson</PERSON> in the way that most doctors
would have done, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave her a dose of opiate which ensured that she died.
11.83 Finally, for that year, on 6th November 1989, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mr <PERSON>Joseph Wilcockson</PERSON>. He
was 85 and a widower. He lived alone although he was very close to his granddaughter,
Miss <PERSON>Lisa Wilcockson</PERSON>, who saw him every day and often went out with him in the
evenings. His general health was quite good, although he had a painful varicose ulcer
on his leg, which was dressed periodically by the district nurse. The leg ulcer did not
prevent him from enjoying an active social life. For many years, he had been involved in
the organisation of local working men’s clubs. He greatly enjoyed visiting the clubs in
the <LOCATION>Hyde</LOCATION> area, where he was well known. He and Miss <PERSON>Wilcockson</PERSON> had been to two or
three clubs the night before he died. On the day of his death, his home help called in
between 9am and 10am. She found him a little subdued. She did not know but, that
morning, he had contacted the local newspaper to place an entry in the ‘ In Memoriam’
column to commemorate the anniversary of his wife’s death. At about noon, the district
nurse arrived to dress Mr <PERSON>Wilcockson</PERSON>’s leg. She found him sitting in his usual chair,
dead. He was still warm and the bandage on his leg had been disturbed. She
telephoned the surgery and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> attended. The district nurse told him what she had
157
found. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not tell her that he had visited Mr <PERSON>Wilcockson</PERSON> that morning. In fact,
he had visited, as he was later to tell Miss <PERSON>Wilcockson</PERSON>. He told her that he had found her
grandfather in reasonable health. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that Mr <PERSON>Wilcockson</PERSON> had died as the
result of a coronary thrombosis. He said that a post-mortem examination was not
necessary. On cremation Form B, he gave the time of death as 12.30pm and said that
he had seen Mr <PERSON>Wilcockson</PERSON> alive at 10.30am. Both of those times were probably
inaccurate. The death must have occurred before 12.30pm and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s visit almost
certainly took place after 10.30am. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> probably gave false times in order to
distance the death from his visit. I suspect that, on this occasion, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> only narrowly
avoided being caught red-handed by the district nurse. He did not tell her that he had
been at Mr <PERSON>Wilcockson</PERSON>’s flat that morning, in circumstances where it would have been
the natural reaction to do so. It is quite likely that, because of the district nurse’s
involvement, this death might have been the subject of comment amongst the surgery
staff. I think the death of Mr <PERSON>Wilcockson</PERSON> probably gave <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> a bad scare. He did not
kill again for ten months.
The Years 1990 and 1991
11.84 As on the previous occasion when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had ceased killing for several months, his
first victim, on resumption, was terminally ill with cancer. On 18th September 1990,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> hastened the death of Mrs <PERSON>Dorothy Rowarth</PERSON>. There would have been very little
risk that he would be detected in such a killing. I have the impression that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was
regaining his confidence. The next ‘ healthy’ victim was killed on 30th December 1990.
Mrs <PERSON>Mary Dudley</PERSON> was only 69 but she had a history of heart trouble. On Christmas Day,
her close companion, a man, died quite suddenly. She spent Christmas with her family
and returned to her own home on Saturday, 29th December. The following morning,
30th December, she was not feeling well and called <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. She was not seriously ill
and still intended to go to her son’s house for lunch. In the late morning, she called on
her neighbour, Mrs Ivy Murphy, as usual, for a chat. A while later, <PERSON>Mrs Murphy</PERSON> saw
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrive at Mrs <PERSON>Dudley</PERSON>’s flat. After about 15 minutes, <PERSON>Mrs Murphy</PERSON> went to
<PERSON>Mrs Dudley</PERSON>’s flat and found the door ajar. She went in and met <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> in the hall. He
told her that <PERSON>Mrs Dudley</PERSON> was dead and <PERSON>Mrs Murphy</PERSON> saw that that was so. She heard
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> speak on the telephone to a relative and say that <PERSON>Mrs Dudley</PERSON> would not live
long. Later, he was to tell <PERSON>Mrs Dudley</PERSON>’s sons that he had found <PERSON>Mrs Dudley</PERSON> having a
heart attack. He said that he had given her some morphine for the pain, but she had
died. He created a false medical record, suggesting that <PERSON>Mrs Dudley</PERSON> had been suffering
from chest pain for two weeks.
11.85 <PERSON>Mrs Dudley</PERSON> was the last patient whom <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed while working at the <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook
Surgery</ORGANIZATION>. It appears that it was at about this time that he began to plan his departure
from <LOCATION>Donneybrook</LOCATION>. A year later, on 1st January 1992, he set up as a sole practitioner,
from rooms within <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook House</ORGANIZATION>, where he remained until his new premises at
<LOCATION>Market Street</LOCATION> were ready. During the Donneybrook years, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had killed 71
patients. The circumstances of a further 30 deaths give rise to suspicion.
158
The Shipman Inquiry
159
CHAPTER TWELVE
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s Unlawful Activities: The Market Street Years
12.1 In this Chapter, I shall give a chronological account of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s years as a sole
practitioner between January 1992 and his arrest in September 1998. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> moved
to new premises at <LOCATION>Market Street</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> on 24th August 1992. Between then and his
arrest, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed 143 patients. It is plainly not practicable for me to summarise the
facts of each of these cases in this narrative. I shall do so only where the circumstances
mark a turning point in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s conduct or are in some other way unusual. For the
majority of cases, the reader must refer to the individual decisions in Volumes Three to
Six.
12.2 In Chapter Eleven, I sought to illustrate the development of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s criminal
behaviour during the <LOCATION>Donneybrook</LOCATION> years and the various methods he used to cover up
his conduct. These methods were to be repeated time and time again during his time at
Market Street. There are many cases in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> made false entries in the
medical records, told lies on cremation forms, lied to relatives, claimed to have arrived
to find patients dead when they were not, claimed to find patients ‘ breathing their last’
when they were not, claimed that patients had refused to be admitted to hospital when
they had not and claimed to have summoned an ambulance when he had not. In this
Chapter, I shall explain how <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obtained large quantities of diamorphine and
demonstrate the relationship between his drug supplies and the pattern of killings.
I shall also refer to particular groups of deaths and point to some trends in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
methods of concealing his crimes.
1992
12.3 From 1st January 1992, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was a sole practitioner working from rooms within
<ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook House</ORGANIZATION>. He remained there until his new premises in <LOCATION>Market Street</LOCATION> were
ready in August 1992. Although I suspect that he might have been responsible for the
death of <PERSON>Mrs Annie Powers</PERSON> on 10th January 1992, I have not found that he killed any
other patient during this period of almost eight months. However, it does appear that he
obtained some diamorphine by illicit means. The only transaction involving <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
recorded in the <ORGANIZATION>Norwest Co-op Pharmacy</ORGANIZATION> controlled drugs register during this period
was the dispensing of two 30mg ampoules of diamorphine on 16th March 1992. These
were prescribed in the name of a male patient who subsequently transferred to another
doctor and has since died. The Inquiry has not investigated his death, which was plainly
unconnected with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. However, the patient’s medical records have been
obtained and reveal no record that he was prescribed diamorphine in March 1992, nor
any condition which would have justified such a prescription. It seems therefore that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obtained the drugs for his own purposes.
12.4 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s new premises at 21 Market Street were immediately adjacent to the <ORGANIZATION>Norwest
Co-op Pharmacy</ORGANIZATION>. There was a ceremonial opening of the surgery with a good deal of
publicity. Only a few weeks later, on 7th October, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mrs <PERSON>Monica Sparkes</PERSON>.
<PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON> was 72 and lived alone. For some weeks before her death she had been
‘ bad on her feet’ and had had a number of falls. As a result, her daughter-in-law,
160
The Shipman Inquiry
Mrs <PERSON>Avril Sparkes</PERSON>, asked <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to visit and, on 21st September, he directed an
increased dose of Stemetil, which <PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON> took for vertigo. On 6th October,
<PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON> was well and, when her son visited, was ironing in preparation for a holiday
in the <LOCATION>Lake District</LOCATION>. She was expecting <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to call the following day. At about
lunchtime on 7th October, Mr <PERSON>Phyllis Holt</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON>’ sister-in-law, telephoned her but
the call was answered by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, who told her that <PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON> had had a slight
stroke. He said he had called for an ambulance but, as there was an emergency at
<LOCATION>Manchester Airport</LOCATION>, there were no ambulances available. Indeed, there had been an
incident at the airport that day although the Inquiry’s investigations suggest that this
would not have resulted in there being no ambulances to deal with other emergencies.
Whether <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> knew of the airport incident because he had requested an ambulance
for <PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON> or for some other reason is not clear. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> also told <PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON>’
sister-in-law that he had told <PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON> to lie on the bed and await his return. He said
he had to go to the surgery. Members of <PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON>’ family then tried to contact her by
telephone but there was no reply. It is clear that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had killed <PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON> during
his visit to her home. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> returned to <PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON>’ home at about 3.30pm and then
telephoned her sister-in-law, <PERSON>Mrs Dorothy Sparkes</PERSON>, to say that he had found her dead.
He certified that the death was due to a stroke. In the medical record, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said only
that <PERSON>Mrs Sparkes</PERSON> had had a stroke and that he had called an ambulance to take her to
<ORGANIZATION>Tameside General Hospital</ORGANIZATION>. In short, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had resumed killing in much the same
way as he had killed before.
1993
12.5 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> resumed a regular pattern of killing in February 1993 and it is hard to resist the
inference that there must have been a connection between this resumption and what he
perceived to be the constraints imposed by practice at the <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook Surgery</ORGANIZATION>. For
several months in this year, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s pattern of killing can be closely related to his
supply of diamorphine. Between February and August, he issued fourteen prescriptions
for a single 30mg ampoule of diamorphine, a most unusual amount to be prescribed for
therapeutic purposes, but a dose that would be lethal for an opiate-naï ve patient. Six of
the prescriptions were dispensed within a few days after the death of the patient in
whose name they were issued and four on the day of the death itself. The two live
patients in whose names prescriptions were dispensed say that they never received
them. None of the medical notes of the patients concerned record the administration of
diamorphine, although three (those of Miss <PERSON>Mary Andrew</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Edna Llewellyn</PERSON> and
Mrs <PERSON>Amy Whitehead</PERSON>) refer to the intravenous administration of 10mg morphine sulphate
or morphine on the day of death. Those cases will be discussed further below.
12.6 It is now clear that, during 1993, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was using 30mg ampoules of diamorphine to
kill and was replenishing his stock as and when necessary. On 22nd February 1993, for
example, he obtained two ampoules of diamorphine in the names of Mr <PERSON>Harold Freeman</PERSON>
who died on 20th February and <PERSON>Mrs Louisa Radford</PERSON> who died a natural death on
22nd February. Two days after obtaining those ampoules, on 24th February, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
killed <PERSON>Mrs Olive Heginbotham</PERSON> and Mrs <PERSON>Hilda Couzens</PERSON>.
161
12.7 His actions in respect of these two patients were very similar. He visited both during the
afternoon and killed them. On his return to the surgery, he gave instructions to the
receptionist to make arrangements for each to be visited at home by a consultant
geriatrician from <ORGANIZATION>Tameside General Hospital</ORGANIZATION>. Both appointments must have been later
cancelled. Mrs <PERSON>Couzens</PERSON>’ body was found during the evening of 24th February. By about
noon the next day, 25th February, <PERSON>Mrs Heginbotham</PERSON>’s death had not been discovered.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> went to the house, knocked at a neighbour’s door and asked for a key. The
neighbour did not have a key but accompanied <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to <PERSON>Mrs Heginbotham</PERSON>’s house,
where, by looking through a window, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed to be able to tell that she had
died. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> then departed, leaving the neighbour to deal with the situation. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
later claimed that <PERSON>Mrs Heginbotham</PERSON> had died during the early hours of the morning but
the evidence shows that she had died before dark on the previous day. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
replaced his stock of diamorphine by obtaining a 30mg ampoule in the name of <PERSON>Mrs
Heginbotham</PERSON> on 25th February and a further ampoule on 26th February in the name of a
patient who is still alive.
12.8 On 22nd March 1993, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mrs <PERSON>Amy Whitehead</PERSON> and obtained a 30mg
ampoule of diamorphine in her name. On 8th April, he killed Miss <PERSON>Mary Andrew</PERSON> and
obtained a further ampoule of diamorphine in her name four days later. On 17th April, he
obtained an ampoule in the name of Mrs <PERSON>Sarah Ashworth</PERSON> and killed her. It is not clear
whether the killing occurred before or after the obtaining of the drug. On 26th April, he
killed <PERSON>Mrs Fanny Nichols</PERSON> and, on 27th April, he killed <PERSON>Mrs Marjorie Parker</PERSON>. On that day,
27th April, he obtained an ampoule in the name of each of those two women. He killed
<PERSON>Mrs Nellie Mullen</PERSON> on 2nd May and Mrs <PERSON>Edna Llewellyn</PERSON> on 4th May. On 5th May, he
obtained an ampoule in the name of each of those two women. On 12th May, he killed
<PERSON>Mrs Emily Morgan</PERSON>, and on 13th May he killed Mrs Violet Bird. On 20th May, he obtained
an ampoule in the name of Mr <PERSON>Ernest Ralphs</PERSON>. He killed <PERSON>Mrs Jose Richards</PERSON> on 22nd July.
On 14th August, he obtained another ampoule in the name of Mr <ORGANIZATION>Ralphs</ORGANIZATION> and killed
<PERSON>Mrs Edith Calverley</PERSON> three days later on 17th August. On 27th August, he obtained
another ampoule in the name of a patient who is still alive.
12.9 With the 14 ampoules he obtained between February and August 1993, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed
13 patients during the same period. I think it possible that the fourteenth ampoule was
used in an attempt to kill Mrs <PERSON>Mary Smith</PERSON> on 31st August 1993. If that was an attempt, it
failed. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was disturbed while visiting <PERSON>Mrs Smith</PERSON> at home. Her step-daughters
arrived unexpectedly at her flat. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was leaning over <PERSON>Mrs Smith</PERSON>, who was
unconscious. <PERSON>Mrs Smith</PERSON> slept deeply until the following morning, when she awoke, with
no apparent ill effects. I felt unable to reach a definite conclusion about what had
happened. It is possible that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was injecting her and intended to kill her but had
to stop the process before completion, as the step-daughters arrived, with the result that
<PERSON>Mrs Smith</PERSON> did not receive a lethal dose. It may well be that this incident gave him rather
a fright, as he did not kill again until December 1993.
12.10 In some of the 1993 cases, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> admitted, either in the medical records or orally to
relatives, that he had given the deceased a small dose of morphine or diamorphine.
In the case of Mrs <PERSON>Amy Whitehead</PERSON>, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was asked to visit on the morning of
22nd March, as <PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON> had stomach trouble and had started to suffer from
162
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry</ORGANIZATION>
diarrhoea in the night. She cannot have been very ill, as she did all her usual household
tasks including putting the washing on the line. She also made some lunch. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
arrived in the late morning and killed <PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON>. Shortly before 1pm, he telephoned
the home of Mrs <PERSON>Whitehead</PERSON>’s son and spoke to his wife. He told her that he had visited
<PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON> and had found her in heart failure. He had given her an injection. He
suggested that the son and daughter-in-law should come over but said that they should
not rush as, by the time they arrived, <PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON> would be dead. The daughter-inlaw
said that they would come immediately but <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he could not stay; he
had to look after the living and not the dead. He agreed a time at which he would meet
them at <PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON>’s house. In the medical records, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> fabricated an entry to
suggest that he found <PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON> suffering from a coronary thrombosis. He claimed
that he had given her an intravenous injection of 10mg morphine sulphate. If
<PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON> had been suffering the severe pain of a heart attack, a modest dose of
morphine would have been appropriate, although 10mg would probably be rather a
large dose. The injection should be given slowly and stopped when the pain is relieved.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not say that he had done that. In any event, 10mg morphine would not
have killed <PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON>.
12.11 At some time that day, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obtained 30mg diamorphine in <PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON>’s name.
It is not possible to discover whether he obtained the diamorphine before he went to
<PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON>’s home or afterwards. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was not supposed to carry controlled
drugs in his medical bag, as he did not maintain a controlled drugs register. He could
legitimately have a controlled drug in his possession which had been prescribed for a
particular patient and which he was about to administer. Any controlled drug not
administered to the patient should have been destroyed. If <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had collected
30mg diamorphine for <PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON> before he visited her, it would have been very
suspicious indeed, as he thought she had only a stomach upset. If he prescribed and
collected 30mg diamorphine after he visited her, that too would be suspicious, as he
had noted in the medical record that he had administered 10mg morphine sulphate, a
dose which would be about one sixth of the amount of opiate which he later collected
from the pharmacy. It is clear that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave <PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON> much more than 10mg
morphine and he almost certainly gave her 30mg diamorphine, which would be a
lethal dose.
12.12 I mention this case for two reasons. First, it is an example of how startlingly callous
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> could be when breaking the news of a death. His attitude during the telephone
call to <PERSON>Mrs Whitehead</PERSON>’s daughter-in-law was shocking. Second, it illustrates why the
admission in the notes that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had given a modest dose of opiate should be
treated with suspicion. He almost certainly gave much more. The advantage to him of
saying that he had given some morphine or diamorphine would be that, if a post-mortem
examination were to be called for and if toxicological tests were to be ordered, he would
have a ready explanation for the finding of morphine in the body. Also, if the mark of an
injection was evident, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> would have been able to explain it.
12.13 The death of Miss <PERSON>Mary Andrew</PERSON> on 8th April 1993 is strikingly similar to that of Mrs <PERSON>Amy
Whitehead</PERSON>. In the morning, she telephoned <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery to ask for a visit; she had
back pain. During the morning, she was not seriously ill. She was visited by two
163
neighbours, <PERSON>Mrs Judith Page</PERSON> and, later, <PERSON>Mrs Martha Marley</PERSON>, who herself was killed by
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> in March 1998. <PERSON>Mrs Marley</PERSON> left when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived. Not long afterwards,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> telephoned Miss <PERSON>Andrew</PERSON>’s brother and told him that his sister had died. He
said he had given her an injection of morphine and had gone to ask Mrs Page to look
after her but he had found that <PERSON>Mrs Page</PERSON> was not at home. He said that, when he
returned, Miss <PERSON>Andrew</PERSON> was dead. In the medical records, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that he had
found Miss <PERSON>Andrew</PERSON> suffering from a coronary thrombosis and congestive heart failure.
He had given her 10mg morphine but she had died. We know from the controlled drugs
register at the <ORGANIZATION>Norwest Co-op Pharmacy</ORGANIZATION> that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obtained 30mg diamorphine in
Miss <PERSON>Andrew</PERSON>’s name four days after her death. It is clear that he was replacing the 30mg
ampoule that he had used on Miss <PERSON>Andrew</PERSON>.
12.14 The death of Mrs <PERSON>Edna Llewellyn</PERSON> a month later, on 4th May 1993, is a variation on the
same theme. She suffered from heart disease and, on the morning of 4th May, she had
an attack of angina. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was called but, by the time he arrived, <PERSON>Mrs Llewellyn</PERSON> was
much better. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> went into the bedroom and was alone with <PERSON>Mrs Llewellyn</PERSON>. Her
daughter-in-law and a friend, who were also in the house, stayed in the living room. After
a few minutes, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> went to collect something from his car and returned to the
bedroom. A few minutes after that, he emerged from the bedroom and announced that
<PERSON>Mrs Llewellyn</PERSON> had died of a ‘ massive’ heart attack. In my judgement, he had killed her.
In the medical records, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that he had given 10mg morphine
intravenously, ostensibly to relieve pain. The following day, he obtained 30mg
diamorphine from the <ORGANIZATION>Norwest Co-op Pharmacy</ORGANIZATION>. Once again, it is apparent that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was carrying opiates in his medical bag. Once again, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> replenished his
stock by collecting about six times as much drug as that which he claimed to have
administered.
12.15 <PERSON>Mrs Violet Bird</PERSON> was only 60 at the time of her death on 13th May 1993. The
circumstances of her death are similar, although not identical, to those I have just
described. Once again, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> attributed the death to a heart attack. Once again, he
claimed to have given an intravenous injection of opiate, this time 10mg diamorphine,
which is twice what he claimed to have given in the other cases. Once again, he
obtained 30mg diamorphine shortly after her death. In fact, he did not obtain this until
20th May and he obtained it in the name of Mr <PERSON>Ernest Ralphs</PERSON>, who was still alive.
Nonetheless, it is hard to resist the inference that he was replacing the ampoule of
diamorphine that he had used to kill Mrs Bird.
12.16 The death of Mrs <PERSON>Jose Richards</PERSON> on 22nd July 1993 is also similar to the others.
<PERSON>Mrs Richards</PERSON> was 74. She suffered from chronic obstructive airways disease. However,
she had no history of heart trouble. Her state of health immediately before the death is
not clear, as a crucial date in the medical records has been overwritten and is virtually
illegible. It may be that <PERSON>Mrs Richards</PERSON> had suffered an episode of congestive heart failure
on the day before her death. On the other hand, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have fabricated an
entry to suggest that. Whatever happened on the day before the death, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> visited
of his own volition on 22nd July. Mrs Richards spoke to a friend at about 12.15pm and
seemed perfectly well. When the friend called at her house at about 1.45pm, she found
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> there. He announced that <PERSON>Mrs Richards</PERSON> had ‘ just gone’, meaning that she was
164
The Shipman Inquiry
dead. He told the friend that he had given <PERSON>Mrs Richards</PERSON> an injection for her pain and
that the injection had killed her; he had not realised how frail she had become. He was
very matter of fact. He had made himself a cup of tea. <PERSON>Mrs Richards</PERSON> was sitting in her
chair. Her dentures had been removed. In the medical records <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that he
had found <PERSON>Mrs Richards</PERSON> suffering from a heart attack. He had given her an intravenous
injection of 10mg diamorphine. <PERSON>Mrs Richards</PERSON> had died half an hour later. Three weeks
later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obtained another 30mg diamorphine in the name of Mr <PERSON>Ernest Ralphs</PERSON>.
12.17 According to the available records, after August 1993, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> never again prescribed
single 30mg ampoules. Whether he changed his method because he was fearful that
the unusual pattern of prescribing might be noticed, or whether he simply found a better
way of obtaining diamorphine, I do not know. However, in November 1993, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
took advantage, for the first time, of a new and more prolific source of diamorphine.
Mr <PERSON>Raymond Jones</PERSON>, who was suffering from terminal cancer, began to require large
amounts of diamorphine. He was provided with a syringe driver, a device which feeds a
regular supply to the patient by means of subcutaneous injection. So far as I have been
able to discover, Mr <PERSON>Jones</PERSON> was the first of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patients to be provided with a
syringe driver. There were to be more in the future, as this had become the preferred
method of administering analgesics to patients suffering from protracted pain who were
unable to take oral medication. Following Mr <PERSON>Jones</PERSON>’ death, on 27th November 1993,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> took possession of two or three boxes, each containing ten 100mg ampoules
of diamorphine. He did not return them to the pharmacy for destruction. I am satisfied
that he kept them for his own purposes. From this time onwards, I think that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
always had a plentiful supply of diamorphine and it appears that the frequency with
which he killed patients increased accordingly. He killed three patients in December
1993, making a total for that year of 16.
1994
12.18 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed two patients in January 1994, one of whom, Miss <PERSON>Joan Harding</PERSON>, died in
his surgery. Miss <PERSON>Harding</PERSON> had some history of anxiety and depression. She was a
regular attender at <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery and it is quite likely that he regarded her as a
nuisance. When she went in for her appointment in January 1994, she was complaining
of pains in her elbow and back. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> noted this and then examined her. According
to his note, which is clearly false, he observed signs that Miss <PERSON>Harding</PERSON> had had a heart
attack. He claimed to have ordered an ambulance and then he recorded that the patient
had collapsed and he had been unable to resuscitate her. He claimed that he and Sister
<PERSON>Morgan</PERSON>, the practice nurse, had been present at the death. In fact, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had
injected Miss <PERSON>Harding</PERSON> with a lethal dose of opiate. When she was unconscious, he went
to fetch Sister <PERSON>Morgan</PERSON> and asked her to help with resuscitation. So far as Sister <PERSON>Morgan</PERSON>
was concerned, these were genuine attempts but I am quite sure that, for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, they
were a charade. An ambulance was summoned, but not until after Miss <PERSON>Harding</PERSON> was
dead. It was cancelled soon after it was ordered.
12.19 On 9th February 1994, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Elsie Platt</PERSON>. Later that month, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had a
scare. He injected <PERSON>Mrs Renate Overton</PERSON> with diamorphine, intending to kill her. However,
she was kept alive by the intervention of a team of paramedics and remained
165
unconscious until her death 14 months later. This incident could easily have led to an
inquiry into his treatment of Mrs Overton but, in the event, did not. During the evening of
18th February 1994, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was called out to see <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON>, who was suffering from
an asthma attack. She was a rather demanding patient and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might well have
regarded her as a nuisance. <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON>’s daughter was in the house but, when she
was satisfied that her mother’s asthma attack had been successfully treated, she went
upstairs to her room, leaving <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> alone with her mother in the living room. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
then injected <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON> with diamorphine. After a short time, he called <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON>’s
daughter downstairs and staged an emergency. <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON> was unconscious on the

Before long, the ambulance arrived and the paramedics succeeded in starting <PERSON>Mrs
Overton</PERSON>’s heart. They took her to <ORGANIZATION>Tameside General Hospital</ORGANIZATION>. She was deeply
unconscious and had suffered irreversible brain damage. She lived in a persistent
vegetative state for 14 months.
12.20 I am quite sure <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> intended to kill <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON> on 18th February 1994. Whether he
underestimated the dose which would be needed to kill her, or whether the vigour of the
resuscitation procedures prevented her death, or whether the ambulance paramedics
arrived as she was on the point of death and prevented her death by the use of their
defibrillator, I do not know. At the time of admission, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told the paramedics and
the hospital staff that <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON> had suffered a heart attack at home and that he had
given 10mg diamorphine to relieve her pain. In normal circumstances, that might be a
reasonable thing to do, although the dose was on the high side. However, these were
not normal circumstances, as <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON> was known to be asthmatic and had just had
an asthma attack. Staff at the hospital realised that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had given far more opiate
than he should have done. Why there was no formal complaint or report is to be
investigated in Phase Two of the Inquiry. However, I think <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must have felt
extremely vulnerable in the days and weeks following this incident. If his apparent
negligence were investigated, there must have been a danger that his possession of
illicit supplies of opiate and his more sinister intentions would be uncovered. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
did not kill for three months after the episode involving <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON>. It may be that he
destroyed his cache of diamorphine, as he did not kill again until the day on which he
next obtained a supply.
12.21 On 17th May, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Mary Smith</PERSON>. She was the patient whom he might have
killed in August 1993 had he not been disturbed. By May 1994, <PERSON>Mrs Smith</PERSON> was suffering
from lung cancer but she had not yet reached the terminal phase. On 17th May,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obtained a supply of 1000mg diamorphine in <PERSON>Mrs Smith</PERSON>’s name, almost
certainly on the pretext that she was to be issued with a syringe driver. She did not have
a syringe driver; indeed she did not need one, as she was not in severe pain before her
death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must have used a small proportion of the diamorphine to kill <PERSON>Mrs Smith</PERSON>
and kept the rest.

12.24 In addition to the three patients killed in April 1995, <PERSON>Mrs Renate Overton</PERSON> died on
21st April, as the result of the injection that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had given her in February 1994.
Following her death, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was questioned on behalf of the then <LOCATION>South Manchester</LOCATION>
Coroner about the circumstances in which <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON> had become unconscious
14 months earlier. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> explained that he had been called out to see <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON>,
who was having an asthma attack. He claimed that he had stabilised her and had then
gone upstairs to tell her daughter that <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON> would probably need some
hydrocortisone. When he came down, he found <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON> ‘ flat on the floor’. He
commenced resuscitation and an ambulance was called. He said that he was just about
to give up his attempt to revive <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON> when the ambulance arrived and the
ambulance men ‘ found a bleep on the machine’. They managed to restore a heartbeat
but, in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s view, <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON> was already brain dead. She had been in a coma
ever since. He said nothing about the administration of morphine or diamorphine. Had
there been an inquest and had anyone looked carefully at the hospital records, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
would have been required to explain his administration of diamorphine to <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON>
the previous February. In the event, the coroner decided not to hold an inquest and, on
