12.25 I am quite satisfied that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON> and intended to do so. If he had
stood trial in 1995, following her death, he could not have been convicted of murder
(although he could have been convicted of attempted murder), as the law then provided
that the prosecution must prove that the death had occurred within a year and a day of
the act causing death. This was an old rule, doubtless intended to avoid the danger that
a defendant might be convicted of an offence where the causal link between the act and
the death would be uncertain. The law was changed by the Law Reform (Year and a
Day Rule) Act 1996, because, nowadays, it is not at all uncommon for a victim to live for
many months or even years on a life support system after the act which caused the
eventual death. <PERSON>Mrs Overton</PERSON>’s case was one such.
12.26 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed four patients in June 1995. One of these was <PERSON>Mrs Bertha Moss</PERSON>, who died
in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery. In all, six of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patients were killed on surgery premises.
The first of these deaths, that of Mrs <PERSON>Mary Hamer</PERSON>, occurred at the Donneybrook Surgery
in March 1989. I have described the circumstances in Chapter Eleven. Miss <PERSON>Joan
Harding</PERSON> was killed at the Market Street Surgery in January 1994. There were two surgery
167
deaths in 1995, those of Mrs <PERSON>Moss</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mrs Dora Ashton</PERSON>, who died on 26th September
1995. Mrs <PERSON>Edith Brady</PERSON> was killed at the surgery on 13th May 1996 and <PERSON>Mrs Ivy Lomas</PERSON>, of
whose murder <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was convicted, died at the surgery on 29th May 1997. All six of
these deaths are remarkably similar.
12.27 Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> says that it is most unusual for a patient to die in a general practitioner’s
surgery. The reasons are obvious. If the patient is very ill, he or she will not have been
well enough to travel to the surgery. If a sudden untoward event, such as respiratory or
cardiac arrest, occurs, as occasionally does happen, expert medical help is on hand
immediately and the patient can be transferred to hospital by ambulance with the
minimum delay. Occasionally, a patient will die on surgery premises. However, the
evidence suggests that, when this happens, it has followed an emergency in which
virtually everyone on the premises has been called upon to help. That this is the
common experience of other doctors makes the circumstances of the six deaths in
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgeries the more unusual. In each case, the death occurred while <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
was alone with the patient behind closed doors, although, on two occasions, he
involved members of his staff in ostensible attempts at resuscitation.
12.28 As I have said, a death in a surgery is a most unusual event. Six of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patients
died in his surgery in just over eight years. Yet no questions were asked. The coroner
was never informed and no real suspicion was aroused.
12.29 <PERSON>Mrs Bertha Moss</PERSON> was only 68 when she died. She had high blood pressure and lateonset
diabetes. She had suffered a deep vein thrombosis in the past. She was a smoker
and was at high risk of suffering a heart attack. However, she was very active and
independent. On the day of her death, before her appointment at <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery,
she did her shopping in <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>. While waiting in the reception area, <PERSON>Mrs Moss</PERSON> chatted
with an old friend, <PERSON>Mrs Jessie Morley</PERSON>. Mrs <PERSON>Moss</PERSON> seemed perfectly well. <PERSON>Mrs Morley</PERSON> saw
the doctor and, as she was leaving, said goodbye to <PERSON>Mrs Moss</PERSON>, who still seemed
perfectly well. A short time after <PERSON>Mrs Moss</PERSON> went into <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s consulting room, he
came to the reception desk and asked the receptionist, <PERSON>Mrs Jane Kenyon</PERSON>, to find the
telephone number of Mrs <PERSON>Moss</PERSON>’ next of kin. He telephoned <PERSON>Mrs Brenda Hurst</PERSON>, one of
Mrs <PERSON>Moss</PERSON>’ daughters, and told her that her mother had had a heart attack. He asked her
to come to the surgery. <PERSON>Mrs Hurst</PERSON> telephoned two of her sisters, <PERSON>Mrs Betty Clayton</PERSON> and
Mrs <PERSON>Jayne Gaskell</PERSON>. They all agreed to go to the surgery. They did not know that their
mother was dead. When <PERSON>Mrs Gaskell</PERSON> arrived, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told her that <PERSON>Mrs Moss</PERSON> had come
into the surgery and then said that there was ‘ nothing he could do’. <PERSON>Mrs Gaskell</PERSON> then
began to realise that her mother had died. When the other daughters had arrived,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave a fuller explanation of what had supposedly occurred. He said that
<PERSON>Mrs Moss</PERSON> had had a heart attack. He had taken an electrocardiograph (ECG) and,
whilst he was putting the equipment away, <PERSON>Mrs Moss</PERSON> had had a ‘ funny do’. He had done
his best to revive her but had been unable to do so. He said that the ECG trace showed
that she had had a slight heart attack. All this was untrue. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> then said that there
was no need for a post-mortem examination, as he had been present at the death. He
said that the family would not want to have her body ‘ cut up’. Later, in conversation with
another daughter, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> suggested that it was all for the best that <PERSON>Mrs Moss</PERSON> had died
when she did, as she would have had to have her legs ‘ chopped off’ on account of her

The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry</ORGANIZATION>
diabetes and she would not have wanted to spend the rest of her life in a wheelchair

12.35 On 6th June 1996, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obtained an enormous haul of diamorphine, no less than
12,000mg. Mr <PERSON>Keith Harrison</PERSON>, who died a natural death on that day, had been suffering
from cancer and had been using a syringe driver. The district nurses were responsible

tallies with the amounts prescribed by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> in Mr <PERSON>Harrison</PERSON>’s name until the day of
the death. It appears that, after hearing of Mr <PERSON>Harrison</PERSON>’s death, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> prescribed
12,000mg diamorphine in Mr <PERSON>Harrison</PERSON>’s name, collected it from the pharmacy and kept
it. This would have been enough to kill about 360 healthy average-sized morphine-naï ve
adults. This acquisition was followed by a marked increase in the frequency with which
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed. He killed 11 patients during the next two months. The last died on
29th July. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> took a holiday from 3rd to 19th August and a locum took his place at
the surgery. Soon after his return to work, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> resumed killing. There were deaths
on 30th August, 12th September, 20th September, 23rd October, 20th November,

12.36 At about this time, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s techniques of concealment became noticeably more
sophisticated. He had always told demonstrable lies on cremation Forms B but, as time
went by, these became more elaborate. For example, in cases where he was obliged to
170
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry</ORGANIZATION>
admit that he had been present at the death, he began to claim that others had been
present with him, when the truth was that he had been alone with the patient at the time.
One such case was that of Mrs <PERSON>Edith Brady</PERSON>, who died in the surgery. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed
that ‘ self and staff’ had been present at the death. In fact, <PERSON>Mrs Massey</PERSON>, the practice
manager, had been summoned to watch him go through a charade of cardiac
massage, when he knew that <PERSON>Mrs Brady</PERSON> was already dead. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that a
neighbour had been present at the death of Mrs <PERSON>Margaret Vickers</PERSON> on 25th June 1996. In
fact, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mrs Vickers before going to fetch the neighbour, telling her that
<PERSON>Mrs Vickers</PERSON> had had a stroke and that he had called an ambulance to take her to
hospital. When he and the neighbour went back to the house, <PERSON>Mrs Vickers</PERSON> was dead.
12.37 In cases where <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was not obliged to admit that he was present at the death but
had to admit that he had visited earlier on the day of the death, he began more
frequently to claim that someone had seen the deceased alive between the time of his
visit and the discovery of the body. This would demonstrate to anyone who thought
about the matter that he could not possibly have been responsible for the death. He
adopted this technique on one or two occasions in 1994 and 1995 but, in 1996 and
1997, he used this ruse more frequently and more inventively. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> visited
<PERSON>Mrs Marjorie Waller</PERSON> on 18th April 1996. He killed her and left her on her bed. Later, when
he completed Form B, he claimed that, after his visit, neighbours had taken <PERSON>Mrs Waller</PERSON>’s
prescription to the chemist and, on their return, had found her dead. In other words, she
had been seen alive after his departure. This was untrue. Mrs <PERSON>Nellie Bennett</PERSON> was killed
on 25th June 1996 but her body was not found until the following day. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was
called and certified the cause of death. On Form B, he said that the death had occurred
at 8am on 26th June and stated that a neighbour had seen Mrs <PERSON>Bennett</PERSON> alive during the
evening of 25th June, which was untrue. Mrs <PERSON>Elsie Barker</PERSON> was killed on 29th July 1996.
On Form B, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that her nephew had spoken to her on the telephone after
the time at which he had in fact visited and killed her. He also tried to conceal the fact
that he had visited her on 29th July and stated on Form B that he had not seen her since
24th July. However, somewhat inconsistently, he made entries in the medical records
showing that he did visit on the day of her death.
12.38 Associated with these more elaborate lies on Forms B were increasingly elaborate
pretences that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> could estimate the time of death with accuracy. In many cases
in which the death was discovered some time after his visit, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> would state the
time of death on Form B with some precision. Even some quite early Forms B show such
estimates. Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> has said that it is impossible for a general practitioner to make
such an estimate. It is usually undertaken only by a pathologist and requires, as a
starting point, an internal body temperature, usually taken rectally. An estimate of the
ambient temperature of the room in which the body has lain is also needed. The
calculation is quite complex. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> often claimed that he could estimate the time of
death from body temperature. This was pure charlatanism. His motive for doing so must
have been that he wished to create the impression that the death had taken place
several hours after his visit.
12.39 On 20th November 1996, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> visited <PERSON>Mrs Irene Heathcote</PERSON>, probably at about 4pm,
and killed her. Friends tried to visit her in the evening but there was no reply at the door.
171
Her body was not found until the following morning. The gas fire was on very high in the
room. When <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was called, he placed a thermometer under her armpit and then
announced that she had died the previous evening. In the medical records, he noted
that her body temperature indicated that she had died at 8pm and he gave 8pm as the
time of death on cremation Form B. It was absurd to claim that he could estimate the
time of death in this way. He also claimed, falsely, that <PERSON>Mrs Heathcote</PERSON> had been seen
alive by neighbours at 7.45pm. That would, of course, be well after his own visit.
12.40 Another case in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s estimate of the time of death was absurd was that of
Mr <PERSON>Thomas Cheetham</PERSON>. He had cancer but was not yet at the terminal stage. In the early
afternoon of 4th December 1996, <PERSON>Mrs Cheetham</PERSON> went shopping while her husband was
watching the racing on television at his neighbour’s house. He was keeping a look-out
for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, whom he was expecting. When <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived, <PERSON>Mr Cheetham</PERSON> went back
to his own house. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave him a lethal injection. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was about to leave
when he must have noticed <PERSON>Mrs Cheetham</PERSON> returning from town. He waited outside the
door, pretending that he had not been inside. She went inside and found her husband
dead, sitting in his usual chair. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> followed her in. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> pretended that he
could estimate the time of Mr <PERSON>Cheetham</PERSON>’s death, which he said had taken place
between one and two hours earlier. He also pretended that he had not seen Mr
<PERSON>Cheetham</PERSON> alive for twelve days before his death.
12.41 On many occasions, particularly in the early years, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> admitted to relatives, on
Forms B and in medical records that he had been present at the death or had seen the
deceased on the day of the death, some time before the death was discovered. In the
later years, there were more occasions when he avoided making that admission if he
could. It may be that he would always have preferred to avoid making that admission.
On many occasions, he had no option. If he was at the house when a relative or
neighbour arrived or telephoned, there could be no avoiding it. On other occasions,
I think it likely that he was unsure whether or not he had been seen at the deceased’s
house and felt it prudent to say from the outset that he had been there. It seems that this
would not give rise to surprise, as he had created for himself a reputation of being an
old-fashioned doctor who often called on his patients unannounced. In 1994 and 1995,
there were one or two cases in which he avoided admitting that he had seen the patient
on the day of death. Whether by chance or design, there were more such cases in 1996.
In some cases, I can see why he would be particularly anxious to avoid admitting that he
had visited the patient on the day of the death. When <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was called to the death
of Mrs <PERSON>Leah Fogg</PERSON>, whose body was found in the early evening of 10th June 1996, he did
not admit that he had visited <PERSON>Mrs Fogg</PERSON> that afternoon, despite the fact that, on the
previous Friday, her daughter had asked him to visit, as she thought her mother, a
widow, might benefit from bereavement counselling. <PERSON>Mrs Fogg</PERSON> had been in good
physical health, although she was 82. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had not seen her for some weeks. In
fact, on 10th June, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had visited her and killed her and had left without being
noticed. <PERSON>Mrs Fogg</PERSON> lived on a busy road in an area where <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had few patients and
no doubt he thought, rightly, that he had not been noticed. I think <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> recognised
that <PERSON>Mrs Fogg</PERSON> was in such robust health that, if she were to be found dead within a short
time of his visit, some suspicion might be aroused.
172
The Shipman Inquiry
12.42 In 1996 and 1997, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed four patients who were immediate neighbours and
good friends. Mr <PERSON>Thomas Cheetham</PERSON> and his wife <PERSON>Elsie</PERSON> lived at 17 <LOCATION>Garden Street</LOCATION>. Two
brothers, Mr <PERSON>Sidney Smith</PERSON> and Mr <PERSON>Kenneth Smith</PERSON>, both bachelors, lived at number 15.
On 30th August 1996, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mr <PERSON>Sidney Smith</PERSON> in the living room of his house
while his brother waited in the kitchen. I have already described the circumstances of
the death of Mr <PERSON>Thomas Cheetham</PERSON> on 4th December 1996. Only two weeks later, on
17th December, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mr <PERSON>Kenneth Smith</PERSON>. Since the death of his brother, he
had decided that he wished to remain in his own home, although <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had
suggested that he should go into a residential home. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed him during a
routine visit. Finally, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed <PERSON>Mrs Cheetham</PERSON> on 25th April 1997.
1997
12.43 In 1997, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed 37 patients. On 2nd January, he killed <PERSON>Mrs Eileen Crompton</PERSON>, who
lived at <ORGANIZATION>Charnley House</ORGANIZATION>, a residential home for the elderly. <PERSON>Mrs Crompton</PERSON> was one of
only three of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims who lived in a nursing or residential home. She was 75
and had quite severe Parkinson’s disease. She deteriorated during the last few weeks of
her life and no longer recognised her sons. However, there was no particular concern
about her physical condition and she continued to eat quite well. On 2nd January 1997,
she seemed very ‘ flushed’ in the morning and was kept in bed. Her mouth was
congested with mucus. The doctor was sent for. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived at about lunchtime.
Without examining her, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> announced that <PERSON>Mrs Crompton</PERSON> was in heart failure and
that, unless he gave her an injection immediately, he was ‘ going to lose her’. He told the
deputy manager of the home, <PERSON>Mrs Patricia Heyl</PERSON>, that the drug he was going to use was
very powerful. Its purpose was to ‘ kick-start’ the heart. He went out to his car and came
back with a syringe and ampoule. He injected <PERSON>Mrs Crompton</PERSON> in the back of the hand.
Within a minute, she was dead and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said, ‘ Oh dear, this is what I feared would
happen’. In the medical records, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> recorded a history of influenza with
bronchopneumonia and claimed that he had examined <PERSON>Mrs Crompton</PERSON> and had treated
her with benzylpenicillin, an antibiotic, which would have been appropriate treatment if
<PERSON>Mrs Crompton</PERSON> had had a very severe chest infection. It could not ‘ kick-start’ the heart
and would not have caused the patient’s sudden death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified that the
cause of death was bronchopneumonia. I am satisfied that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> administered a
strong opiate and not an antibiotic.
12.44 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed again on the following day, 3rd January, and on seven further occasions
before the end of February. On 20th March, he almost certainly replenished his drug
supplies by obtaining about 1000mg diamorphine following the natural death of
Mr <PERSON>Squire Barber</PERSON>. Two days later, on 22nd March, he killed <PERSON>Mrs Rose Garlick</PERSON> and, on
27th March, he killed Mrs May <PERSON>Lowe</PERSON>. At the end of April, there were three killings, one on
21st April and two on 25th April. On that day, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mrs <PERSON>Jean Lilley</PERSON> at lunchtime
and <PERSON>Mrs Elsie Cheetham</PERSON> in the early afternoon. They lived only about a mile apart.
12.45 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed four patients in May 1997, one of whom was <PERSON>Mrs Ivy Lomas</PERSON>, the last of
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims to die in the surgery. <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> had heart disease and suffered from
anxiety and depression. She was a very regular attender at <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery. On
29th May 1997, <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> took a bus into <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> for her appointment at the surgery. She
173
walked unaided into the consulting room. A minute or so later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> took her into the
examination room. About ten minutes later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> came into the reception area and
apologised for keeping his patients waiting. He said that he had had a problem with the
ECG machine. He dealt with two or three more patients before returning to the
examination room. He then called the receptionist, <PERSON>Mrs Carol Chapman</PERSON>, and told her
that he had tried to take an ECG on <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> but could not get a trace. At first, he had
thought that the machine was broken, but then he realised that she had died. He asked
<PERSON>Mrs Chapman</PERSON> to contact <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON>’ son. <PERSON>Mrs Chapman</PERSON> was unable to contact him and
she telephoned the police. A police officer attended and looked at the body but, as
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he could certify the cause of death, the officer took no action. Later
that day, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON>’ daughter that her mother had come into the surgery
looking unwell. That was probably true. He said that he had taken her through to the
treatment room while he saw another patient. When he returned, she was dead. She had
had a ‘ massive heart attack’. He had tried to revive her but had failed. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave a
different account in evidence at his trial and claimed that <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> had collapsed as
she climbed onto the couch in the examination room. He had done all he could to revive
her. He claimed that he had not called an ambulance because he himself was skilled in
resuscitation techniques. <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> was buried. In 1998, her body was exhumed and
morphine was found in the tissues.
12.46 <PERSON>Mrs Vera Whittingslow</PERSON> suffered from syringomyelia, a rare neurological disorder which
had resulted in loss of mobility. She used a wheelchair. She had hypertension and, at
times, her blood pressure was very high. On 24th June 1997, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> made a routine
visit to her home. He took <PERSON>Mrs Whittingslow</PERSON>’s blood pressure and told her husband that
it was very high, too high for her to be moved to hospital. He sent Mr <PERSON>Whittingslow</PERSON> to the
chemist to fetch a prescription, as a matter of urgency. While Mr <PERSON>Whittingslow</PERSON> was away,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed his wife. When Mr <PERSON>Whittingslow</PERSON> returned, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was taking his bag
back to his car. He told <PERSON>Mr Whittingslow</PERSON> that his wife was fine and they had been having
a chat. When Mr <PERSON>Whittingslow</PERSON> went inside, he found his wife apparently unconscious; in
fact she was dead. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> returned to the house; he pretended to be surprised and
said that <PERSON>Mrs Whittingslow</PERSON> was ‘ dying’. He said that he would wait in the living room
while Mr <PERSON>Whittingslow</PERSON> ‘ said goodbye’ to his wife.
12.47 In early July, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> acquired more diamorphine, probably 800mg, from Mrs <PERSON>Maureen
Jackson</PERSON>. She had cancer and was using a syringe driver. The district nurses were
responsible for the drug record card. Their record tallies with the amounts prescribed
until 3rd July, when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> prescribed 2300mg but delivered only 1500mg to
<PERSON>Mrs Jackson</PERSON>’s home. On 7th July, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave <PERSON>Mrs Jackson</PERSON> an overdose of
diamorphine and hastened her death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was present with the district nurse when
the remaining stock held at the house was destroyed.
12.48 By the end of July, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had killed three more patients and there was another killing
on 10th August, shortly before he went on holiday. He returned to work on 26th August
and killed four patients in September. On 1st November 1997, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obtained
1000mg diamorphine, which he prescribed in the name of Mr <PERSON>Lionel Hutchinson</PERSON> who did
not receive it. There were four killings in November and five more in December, one of
which was on Christmas Eve.

1998
12.49 On 7th January 1998, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> obtained a further 1000mg diamorphine, again
prescribed in the name of Mr <PERSON>Lionel Hutchinson</PERSON>, to whom it was not administered.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed patients on 22nd and 26th January, 2nd February, 9th February,
13th February, 15th February, 18th February and 27th February. On 4th March 1998, he
killed Mr <PERSON>Harold Eddleston</PERSON>, who had been his patient for only about a week and whose

12.50 Since 1993, when the doctors from the <LOCATION>Clarendon Practice</LOCATION> moved to premises on
<LOCATION>Market Street</LOCATION>, just opposite <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had had an informal
arrangement whereby those doctors (by then known as the <ORGANIZATION>Brooke Practice</ORGANIZATION>) would sign
cremation Forms C for his patients. One of those doctors, Dr <PERSON>Rajesh Patel</PERSON>, told the
Inquiry that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> often used to come across to the <PERSON>Brooke</PERSON> surgery premises to make
the request personally, bringing with him the patient’s medical records. He would give a
very full explanation of the medical history leading to the death. It was always very
plausible. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> never actually showed the records to the <LOCATION>Brooke Practice</LOCATION> doctor but
the presence of the records in his hand added authority to his words of explanation. It
appears that the doctors of the <LOCATION>Brooke Practice</LOCATION> became accustomed to the large
number of deaths among <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s patients and attributed it to their perception that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had a large patient list with a high proportion of very elderly people whom he
would strive to keep at home, rather than having them admitted to hospital.
12.51 However, during March 1998, one of the <ORGANIZATION>Brooke Practice</ORGANIZATION> doctors, Dr <PERSON>Linda Reynolds</PERSON>,
became concerned about the number of Forms C the practice was being asked to
complete. On 24th March, she raised her concerns with the <LOCATION>South Manchester</LOCATION> Coroner,
Mr <PERSON>Pollard</PERSON>. She told him that, during the previous three months, she and her colleagues
had signed 16 cremation Forms C for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, who was a sole practitioner. The <ORGANIZATION>Brooke
Practice</ORGANIZATION>, with 9500 patients, had had only 14 deaths within the same period. On the
same day, the <ORGANIZATION>Greater Manchester Police</ORGANIZATION> initiated a confidential investigation into
Dr <PERSON>Reynolds</PERSON>’ concerns. I think it likely that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> learned of that investigation in early
April. However, that issue has been the subject of evidence during Stage One of Phase
Two of the Inquiry and I have not yet reached a definite conclusion on the point. My
provisional view is that he became aware of it in early April and it is likely that he knew
that the source of the report about him was the <PERSON>Brooke Practice</PERSON> doctors who signed his
cremation Forms C.
12.52 Quite apart from the evidence, which I have heard in Phase Two Stage One, there is
evidence from Phase One from which I infer that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> knew of the suspicions of the
175
<ORGANIZATION>Brooke Practice</ORGANIZATION> doctors. First, after killing <PERSON>Mrs Martha Marley</PERSON> on 24th March, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
did not kill again for seven weeks. During the next few months, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> presented only
two Forms C to the doctors of the <LOCATION>Brooke Practice</LOCATION>. They related to patients who had
died naturally. This is very unlikely to have been coincidence. It seems to me that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> knew he was under suspicion and was probably expecting that any death
which came to the attention of the <ORGANIZATION>Brooke Practice</ORGANIZATION> doctors would be referred to the
Coroner. When <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed again, on 11th May, his victim was Mrs <PERSON>Winifred Mellor</PERSON>,
who was a <ORGANIZATION>Roman Catholic</ORGANIZATION>. <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON>’s daughter, <PERSON>Mrs Susan Duggan</PERSON>, has told the
Inquiry that cremation would be contrary to her mother’s religious beliefs. She thinks it
more than likely that her mother, who confided in <LOCATION>Shipman</LOCATION>, made him aware of her
beliefs and of her wish to be buried after her death. Thereafter, he did not kill again until
12th June. As it happens, his victim on that occasion, <PERSON>Mrs Joan Melia</PERSON>, was also buried,
although there is no evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> would have known in advance what
arrangements would be made after her death. His last victim, <PERSON>Mrs Kathleen Grundy</PERSON>, was
also buried after her death on 24th June.
12.53 Second, in July 1998, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was visited by Dr <PERSON>Alan Banks</PERSON>, a medical adviser
employed by the <ORGANIZATION>West Pennine Health Authority</ORGANIZATION>, for the purpose of a routine discussion
about <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s prescribing practices. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> took the opportunity to volunteer to
Dr Banks the information that there had been an unusually high number of deaths
amongst his elderly patients during the first three months of the year. He said that he
and his staff had carried out an audit of the deaths and had found that there was no
cause for concern. In fact there was no such audit. Moreover, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s death rates
were not significantly higher during the first three months of 1998 than in the last three
months of 1997. The numbers of deaths during both periods were abnormally high, as
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had killed a substantial number of patients. The reference to the first three
months of 1998 suggests that he knew that that was the period about which concern
had been expressed.
12.54 It may be that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> intended to resume killing, as he had done on previous
occasions, when he felt that suspicions had died down. On 6th July 1998, he obtained
100mg diamorphine from a patient who had cancer. He did not use it to kill but the fact
that he obtained it suggests that he intended to do so when the opportunity arose. Very
soon after that, on 9th July, <PERSON>Mrs Woodruff</PERSON>, the daughter of <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>, visited
<PERSON>Mrs Claire Hutchinson</PERSON>, one of the witnesses who had apparently signed <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s
will. <PERSON>Mrs Hutchinson</PERSON> later told <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> that <PERSON>Mrs Woodruff</PERSON> was making inquiries about
her mother’s will. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must then have realised that <PERSON>Mrs Woodruff</PERSON> was likely to report
her concerns to the police. He had not killed again by the time of his arrest on
7th September 1998. Thus <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s career of killing was brought to an end.

CHAPTER THIRTEEN
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s Character and Motivation
Introduction
13.1 The Inquiry’s Terms of Reference require me to consider the extent of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
unlawful activities. They do not expressly require me to consider the motives behind
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s crimes or the psychological factors that underlay them. However, I decided
that I ought to consider and report on those matters, as well as I am able. I consider that
some understanding of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s character will be of assistance in Phase Two,
particularly when the Inquiry comes to consider improved systems of death certification
and the issues surrounding the monitoring and supervision of doctors. For this reason,
I considered that an investigation into <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s psyche fell within the Inquiry’s Terms
of Reference. I also think the relatives of the victims and the public will wish to
understand why <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> committed so many murders. For the relatives, some
understanding of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s motives, or lack of them, might assist them in coming to
terms with what has happened.
13.2 In seeking to reach an understanding of why <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> murdered so many of his
patients, I would naturally have wished to obtain thorough psychological and psychiatric
assessments. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> has refused to take part in the Inquiry proceedings and has
continued to deny responsibility for the deaths of his patients, in the face of
overwhelming evidence of guilt. It seemed, therefore, that he would be most unlikely to
agree to such assessments. For the reasons I outlined in Chapter Three, it is plainly
impracticable to force him to undergo any examination or assessment. In any event,
without genuine cooperation on his part, the interviews which would necessarily form
part of any assessment would not be fruitful. I have, therefore, had to make do with such
materials as are available without <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s cooperation.
13.3 In order to assist my understanding, I decided to seek the advice of a team of
experienced forensic psychiatrists. I did not want to limit myself to the opinion of a single
expert. I wanted the psychiatrists to discuss the issues and to reach a consensus if they
could; if not, I wanted them to express their differing views. I consulted a team from the
<ORGANIZATION>Institute of Psychiatry, King’s College</ORGANIZATION>, <LOCATION>London</LOCATION>. The team comprised:
Professor <PERSON>John Gunn</PERSON>, Professor of <ORGANIZATION>Forensic Psychiatry</ORGANIZATION>;
Professor <PERSON>Pamela Taylor</PERSON>, Professor of <ORGANIZATION>Special Hospital Psychiatry</ORGANIZATION>;
Dr <PERSON>Clive Meux</PERSON>, Honorary Senior Lecturer in Forensic Psychiatry;
Dr <PERSON>Alec Buchanan</PERSON>, Clinical Senior Lecturer in Forensic Psychiatry.
13.4 The psychiatrists did not think it appropriate to provide formal reports because they
were unable to carry out the examinations that would normally precede the preparation
of a full psychiatric assessment. It was agreed that they would read the relevant material
assembled by the Inquiry team and would then meet me, Counsel to the Inquiry and
Dr <PERSON>Esmail</PERSON>, the Inquiry’s Medical Advisor, for a full discussion of the issues. That
discussion took place in private because some of the material, which I felt it right to
allow the psychiatrists to see, will not go into the public domain.
178
The Shipman Inquiry
13.5 At an early stage, the Inquiry had obtained some confidential documents relating to
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, mainly his medical and prison records. It was necessary for the legal team to
consider whether they contained material of direct relevance to the crimes themselves.
They might for example have contained admissions. The documents were obtained, on
summons, from the relevant authorities. With the exception of three pages of records
dating from 1975, they did not contain anything of direct relevance and they were
therefore put aside. However, when the Inquiry came to consider issues of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
character and possible motive, I had to consider whether or not the documents should
be disclosed to the psychiatric team. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was entitled to refuse permission for
such disclosure. I was entitled to override that refusal, but would only do so to the extent
that I considered it necessary for the proper purposes of the Inquiry. I hoped and
believed that the documents would assist the team to gain insight into <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
personality. I recognised that such disclosure would be an infringement of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
right to privacy and confidentiality. I specifically considered Article 8 of the European
Convention on Human Rights. My decision was that the limited disclosure I had in mind
was necessary in order to assist in achieving the objects of the Inquiry. Those objects
are designed to contribute to the prevention of crime, to secure the future health and
welfare of citizens and to protect their future rights and freedoms. I considered that the
Inquiry’s need to inform itself properly on these matters (and the public interest in the
Inquiry’s proper conduct of its investigation) outweighed <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s right of complete
confidentiality.
13.6 This confidential material has not been put into the public domain. Nor will it be. The
psychiatrists have received it in confidence. This Report contains no direct reference to
it. It has been used only to inform the opinions of the psychiatrists, who in turn have
given me advice and guidance. As I shall explain below, the main source of information
on which my opinions and conclusions will rest is the evidence about <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s crimes,
which is now in the public domain. All I say about the private material is that there is
nothing within it that is inconsistent with the conclusions I have expressed.
13.7 In the event, the confidential material has not enabled the psychiatrists to gain any real
insight into <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s character as they and I had hoped. The psychiatrists have also
been hampered by the very limited nature of the information available to the Inquiry
about <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s family background and relationships. I decided, at an early stage, that
it would be inappropriate to intrude upon the privacy of his children. They have enough
to cope with. For similar reasons, I also decided that <PERSON>Mrs Shipman</PERSON> should not be asked
about her personal relationship with her husband. When she gave evidence, counsel
were permitted to ask her about factual matters relating to his practice and the various
specific events on which she might be able to shed light. I did not think it reasonable to
intrude on her privacy by allowing questions about her relationship with her husband. In
any event, I am quite sure that he kept aspects of his character secret from his family.
These decisions leave gaps in our knowledge of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> but I think they were correct.
13.8 The psychiatrists would have wished to have an understanding of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s motivation
in becoming a doctor. It is not known when he first developed the ambition to practise
medicine. It is possible that this was related to the suffering and death of his mother
when he was in late adolescence. It is not known what hopes and ambitions he
179
entertained for his medical career. It may be that he felt fulfilled by his career or he may
have been disappointed and dissatisfied that he became a general practitioner in a
small town rather than, say, an eminent surgeon or a member of the influential elite of the
medical profession.
13.9 The psychiatrists stressed to me that the ideas we discussed could not be regarded as
authoritative opinions. They did their best to consider possible explanations for
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s conduct but, with the materials available, were unable to reach any
conclusions. I am grateful for the assistance they have given me but, in the end, I have
been unable to attempt any detailed explanation of the psychological factors underlying
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s conduct. All I can do is to draw attention to features of his behaviour which
might throw some light on his personality and motivation. The views I express in this
Chapter are not those of the psychiatrists, but are my own, and have been reached by
the usual judicial process of drawing common sense inferences from evidence.
13.10 As I have described in the previous three Chapters, I am satisfied that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed
more than 200 patients over a period of 23 years. After some possible early
experimentation, his usual method of killing was to give an intravenous injection of a
lethal dose of diamorphine, which led to death within a few minutes. With a few victims,
mainly patients who were terminally ill, he sometimes gave an intramuscular injection,
which would take effect and result in death within the hour. There is a suspicion that he
sometimes gave large doses of sedatives, such as <LOCATION>Largactil</LOCATION>, to elderly patients with
reduced respiratory function, so as to induce deep prolonged sleep and to make the
patient vulnerable to death by bronchopneumonia.
Motive
13.11 Save for the case of Mrs <PERSON>Kathleen Grundy</PERSON>, which I will discuss in greater detail below,
I have found no evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was motivated by monetary gain. Very few of his
patients left him any money. Those who did, such as <PERSON>Mrs Mavis Pickup</PERSON>, left relatively
modest tokens of appreciation for his services and, as they saw it, his friendship.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was, however, acquisitive. There were occasions when he asked for an item of
property belonging to a patient he had just killed. In 1985, he asked the family of
<PERSON>Mrs Margaret Conway</PERSON> if he could have her budgerigar for his aunt; his request was not
granted. In 1997, he asked the brother of Miss <PERSON>Lena Slater</PERSON> for her sewing machine,
which he was allowed to have. He asked Mrs <PERSON>Joan Sellars</PERSON>, the niece of Miss <PERSON>Mabel
Shawcross</PERSON>, for her antique bench, saying that it had been Miss <PERSON>Shawcross</PERSON>’ intention
that he should have it after her death. <PERSON>Mrs Sellars</PERSON> did not agree. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> ran a patient
fund for the provision of equipment for the surgery and encouraged donations and
bequests. Although this fund was not registered as a charity, there is no reason to think
that the money was used for anything other than proper purposes. It was administered
by a patient, a retired police officer. There is much suspicion that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> pilfered
money and items of property from the homes of his victims, although the evidence is not
sufficiently clear for me to reach any positive conclusions in individual cases. I am quite
satisfied that any such acquisitions, whether with or without permission, did not supply a
motive for murder.
180
The Shipman Inquiry
13.12 <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s murder on 24th June 1998 was, on the face of it, motivated by monetary
gain. She was one of his wealthiest patients. She had a comfortable detached cottage in
an attractive area of <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>. She owned a second property and some investments. Her
estate was worth about £386,000. I have already outlined the way in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
forged a will in her name, using his own typewriter. The forgery of her signature and of
those of the ‘ witnesses’ was very poor. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> sent the will to <LOCATION>Hamilton Ward</LOCATION>, a firm of
solicitors in <LOCATION>Hyde</LOCATION>, with a forged covering letter, ostensibly from <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>. Mr <PERSON>Burgess</PERSON>
of that firm was puzzled to receive it, as <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> was not a client of his firm and the
firm had had nothing to do with drafting the will. It was not addressed to anyone in
particular at the firm. He put it to one side.
13.13 In the will, <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> had left all her property to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> and nothing to her dearly
loved daughter and grandchildren. The will said that she wished to give all her estate to
her doctor to reward him for ‘ all the care he has given to me and the people of <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>’.
She added that he was ‘ sensible enough to handle any problems this may give him’.
I will return in due course to what the wording of the will reveals of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. For the
moment, I consider only whether <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> really was motivated by money in killing
<PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>. Soon after he had killed her on 24th June, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> wrote, on 28th June, to
<LOCATION>Hamilton Ward</LOCATION>. He typed the letter on his own portable typewriter, the same one he had
used to forge the will. He introduced himself as a friend of <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> who had helped
her to make her will, and informed the solicitor of her death. He signed it ‘ <PERSON>J. Smith</PERSON>’ or
possibly ‘ <PERSON>S. Smith</PERSON>’. The police were later to find that <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> knew no one with
either of those names. Copies of the will and the forged letters are to be found at the end
of Chapter One.
13.14 <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s daughter, <PERSON>Mrs Angela Woodruff</PERSON>, is a solicitor in practice in <LOCATION>Warwickshire</LOCATION>.
In 1986, <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> had made a will in favour of her daughter and this was held in safe
custody at Mrs <PERSON>Woodruff</PERSON>’s office. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> knew <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> quite well. Not only had
she been a patient of his for many years, they were both involved in local affairs.
<PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> was very proud of her daughter and grandchildren and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must have
known that <PERSON>Mrs Woodruff</PERSON> was a solicitor.
13.15 The forging of <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s will led directly to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s downfall. I have little doubt
that his killing of her would not have been detected but for his forgery of her will.
However, the will was so obvious a forgery and so entirely uncharacteristic of
<PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> that <PERSON>Mrs Woodruff</PERSON> was bound to investigate it. In fact, she reported her
suspicions about the will to the police. The forgery was soon uncovered and the rest
is history.
13.16 It seems to me that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> could not rationally have thought that he would get away
with <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s estate. The whole venture was grossly incompetent. Discovery was
inevitable. I will return later in this Chapter to discuss what might have been <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
state of mind at the time he forged this will and killed <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>. It does appear that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> planned the forgery of the will well in advance of the killing, which suggests
that money was his motivation. However, I am not convinced that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> decided to
kill <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> because he wanted her money. I think his thought processes must have
been much more complex than that.
181
13.17 There is no suggestion that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> interfered in any way with the bodies of the
patients he had killed. He might on occasions have ‘ arranged’ them, for example by
putting a book or newspaper on the victim’s knee to create the impression that he or she
was reading just before death. In 1988, when killing <PERSON>Mrs Alice Jones</PERSON>, whose sight was
poor, he put her magnifying glass and torch in her hands after she had lapsed into
unconsciousness. But these minor arrangements seem much more likely to be related to
a desire to create unsuspicious circumstances than to any underlying motive for the
crime. There does not appear to have been any overtly sadistic or erotic motivation for
his crimes. The psychiatrists say that they cannot speculate on whether there might
have been some underlying sexual or necrophiliac element within his motivation.
However, there is no evidence from which I could infer that there was.
13.18 In short, if one defines motive as a rational or conscious explanation for the decision to
commit a crime, I think <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s crimes were without motive. The psychiatrists warn
me that it is possible that, in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s own mind, there was a conscious motivation. All
I can say is that there is no evidence of any of the features that I have observed, in my
experience as a judge, that commonly motivate murderers.
Other Explanations
13.19 If I am to find any explanation for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s crimes, it seems to me that I must look, so
far as I can, within his personality. What kind of a man works hard to become a doctor,
takes the Hippocratic oath and, within only a few years, embarks on a career of killing
his patients?
13.20 Our personalities are governed by a mixture of genetic factors and the effects of our
experiences. Very little is known about <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s family or early years. His mother died
of cancer when he was in late adolescence. The psychiatrists think it possible that the
fact and circumstances of her death might have had a profound effect upon his psyche.
The only evidence on this subject available to the Inquiry is that of <PERSON>Mrs Florence
Bateson</PERSON>, for many years a patient of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, who said (in a statement made in
connection with the death of her father, Mr <PERSON>George Charnock</PERSON>) that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> used often
to speak to her about his mother and had said to her husband, Mr <PERSON>Norman Bateson</PERSON>, that
he had seen her suffer from cancer when he was 17. I cannot assess the impact of his
mother’s death or indeed any other potentially formative experiences. In seeking to
describe <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s personality, I am dependent upon what he has revealed of himself
through his actions and the descriptions of people who have known him and have
described him and his behaviour to the Inquiry.
Professional Reputation
13.21 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had the reputation in <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> of being a good and caring doctor. He was held in
very high regard by the overwhelming majority of his patients. He was also respected by
fellow professionals. His patients appear to have regarded him as the best doctor in
<PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>. His register was full and there always seems to have been a waiting list. Patients
liked him for a variety of reasons. Many would say that he ‘ always had time’ for them. His
surgeries overran but no one minded because they understood his wish to take
182
The Shipman Inquiry
whatever time was necessary for each patient. He never hurried them out. He always
had time for a few words of a personal nature. Elderly patients and their families were
particularly grateful for his willingness to visit at home. Other doctors might be reluctant
to visit and might try to insist that a patient be brought to the surgery. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> never did
that. With the benefit of hindsight, one can see that this willingness to make home visits
created many opportunities for killing. At the time, it seemed to his patients only to show
that he was considerate of their welfare. There must, in fact, have been many occasions
when the consideration he showed for his patients was not simply a cover for criminal
actions.
13.22 There is, however, a deeply sinister aspect to the way in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> created for
himself the reputation of being a very caring doctor. He encouraged people to regard
him as an ‘ old-fashioned family doctor’ who would willingly visit his patients at home and
made a habit of calling on them when he was in their area. I am sure he promoted this
view of himself quite deliberately. Dr <PERSON>Patel</PERSON>, of the <ORGANIZATION>Brooke Practice</ORGANIZATION>, told the Inquiry of an
occasion when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had asked him to sign a cremation Form C for one of his
patients. On reading the Form B, Dr <PERSON>Patel</PERSON> noticed that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had been present at the
death. He observed that this was rather unusual. In what might now be seen as an
example of attack being the best form of defence, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> responded rather
aggressively, putting the young <PERSON>Dr Patel</PERSON> firmly in his place. He asserted that young
doctors nowadays do not visit their patients as he and his generation did. The
implication was that they were not as caring as he. He also let it be known that he
thought it preferable that elderly patients should be allowed to die at home ‘ with dignity’
instead of being subject to the ‘ hustle and bustle’ of a hospital ward. It may be that many
of his patients agreed. Certainly, there would be more work for the general practitioner
in caring for an elderly patient at home. However, it is hard to resist the conclusion that
these habits created many opportunities to kill which would not otherwise arise and that
his reputation in respect of these matters was a useful ‘ cover’ for his killings.
13.23 There is no doubt that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was also industrious. When he took his first post in
general practice at the <LOCATION>Abraham Ormerod Medical Centre</LOCATION>, <PERSON>Todmorden</PERSON>, his partners
found him keen and hardworking. He was always willing to take on more than his fair
share of out of hours work. He volunteered to do the donkey-work involved in the
introduction of a new method of filing. In 1998, he had a list of almost 3100 patients. That
represented a very large caseload, substantially greater than the average list of singlehanded
general practitioners in <LOCATION>Tameside</LOCATION>, which was under 2500. He worked long
hours. There was never any delay in arranging an appointment and it appears that there
were very few occasions when he failed to visit a patient on the day of request. He was a
regular attender at continuing education sessions at <LOCATION>Manchester</LOCATION> and <ORGANIZATION>Liverpool
Universities</ORGANIZATION> and at <ORGANIZATION>Tameside General Hospital</ORGANIZATION>. In general, he seems to have been a
good administrator and appears to have maintained the loyalty of his staff. He had a
poor relationship with one member of the practice staff at the Donneybrook Surgery,
whom he regarded as incompetent. The other doctors did not share that view and it may
be that there was a personality clash. Several members of the <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook</ORGANIZATION> staff chose
to leave their employment with that practice in 1992 and move with him to his new
premises at <LOCATION>Market Street</LOCATION>.
183
13.24 His Achilles’ heel as an administrator appears to have been his keeping of medical
records. He was a poor record-keeper. His notes were usually scanty and often
incomplete in important respects. That criticism does not only apply to the entries
associated with a killing. Why his record-keeping was so poor, I cannot say. He might
not have thought the records very important and so gave them a low priority, but on
other occasions there were without doubt more sinister reasons for this failure, as I have
explained in previous Chapters.
Personal Relationships
13.25 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> does not appear to have had many friends. His professional associates never
became friends. Dr <PERSON>Doreen Belk</PERSON> was a fellow student of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> at medical school in
<LOCATION>Leeds</LOCATION> and also went to <LOCATION>Pontefract</LOCATION> to work as a house officer. She and her husband lived
in hospital accommodation very close to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> and his wife. Yet they never became
friends. Dr <PERSON>Belk</PERSON> found <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> cold, aloof and unapproachable. He appeared to have
‘ a chip on his shoulder’ and a grudge against life. When he had settled in <LOCATION>Todmorden</LOCATION>
and later in <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>, he involved himself in many local activities. In <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>, he worked with
the <ORGANIZATION>St John Ambulance Brigade</ORGANIZATION>. He was active in medical politics; for a time he was
secretary of the <ORGANIZATION>Local Medical Committee</ORGANIZATION>. He was a school governor. At least one of his
sons was keen on rugby and played for the <LOCATION>Ashton-under-Lyne Rugby Union</LOCATION> team.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> and his wife were regular supporters of the club. All these were activities that
would usually result in the acquisition of a large circle of friends. Yet the evidence is that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had very few.
13.26 Many patients describe <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> as having a wonderful bedside manner, especially
with the elderly. He would make much of them and would sometimes tease them gently.
They liked it. He made many of them feel that he was a real friend as well as their doctor.
Yet he would kill them. Perhaps the most poignant example of this is the case of
Mrs Mavis Pickup. In August 1997, Mrs Pickup’s husband, <PERSON>Kenneth</PERSON>, died of a heart
attack. They had been happily married for nearly fifty years and she was devastated, of
course. Soon after the death her son, Mr <PERSON>James Pickup</PERSON>, went to see <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to thank
him for the care he had given his father over the many years he had suffered from heart
disease. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was curiously brisk about Mr Pickup’s death but showed great
concern about his widow. He asked after her in a most sympathetic way and told her
son that, if there was anything he could do, if she needed any kind of help, not limited to
medical matters, he ‘ would always be there for her’. He killed her three weeks later after
she had telephoned the surgery, upset because children had been knocking on her
door and running away.
13.27 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had a reputation for ‘ calling a spade a spade’ but many of his patients seemed
to like him for that. Some of his remarks were quite inappropriate but people seemed to
accept them as being typical of the man. For example, when Mr <PERSON>Stephen Dickson</PERSON> asked
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> on 28th February 1998 how long his father-in-law, Mr <PERSON>Harold Eddleston</PERSON>, who
had cancer, was likely to live, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> replied ‘ I wouldn’t buy him any Easter eggs’.
Mr <PERSON>Dickson</PERSON> did not take offence because he thought this kind of remark was typical of
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s style. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mr <PERSON>Eddleston</PERSON> four days later.
184
The Shipman Inquiry
13.28 Many of the families of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims report that his usually kind and sympathetic
attitude disappeared when their relative had died. They would naturally be very
distressed. He would be curt and dismissive and would sometimes say the most
inappropriate and hurtful things. When he had just killed Mrs <PERSON>Mary Coutts</PERSON> in April 1997,
and her son and daughter-in-law, who were in a state of grief and shock at the
suddenness of her death, were asking him about the circumstances, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said,
‘ Well, I don’t believe in keeping them going’. After the death of Mrs <PERSON>Margaret Conway</PERSON> in
1985, he took it upon himself to inform her 14 year old granddaughter (who happened to
have an appointment that afternoon at the surgery) that her grandmother had died,
despite her mother having contacted the surgery to say that he should not do so. The
girl was shocked and distressed.
13.29 It seems that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s attitude towards his patients was quite unpredictable. At times
he was encouraging and sympathetic but at times he was cold, brusque and offhand.
Often, he seemed unable to empathise with the bereaved.
Aggression, Conceit and Contempt
13.30 Other well-marked traits of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s personality were aggression, conceit, arrogance
and contempt for those whom he considered to be his intellectual inferiors. Perhaps the
most striking illustration of his conceit is what he wrote about himself in <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s
forged will, to which I have already referred. He wrote (as if the words were
<PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s) that he was to be rewarded for all the care he had given her and the
people of <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON>. I think he enjoyed referring to himself in the third person in this
flattering way.
13.31 Another example of his conceit may be seen in a letter he wrote in August 1998 to the
<ORGANIZATION>NHS Appeals Tribunal</ORGANIZATION> in connection with a decision of the local <ORGANIZATION>Health Authority</ORGANIZATION> about
funding of his practice staff, in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> felt able to claim:
‘ We are a proactive practice, we have the highest level of screening for
cholesterol, blood pressure, diabetes and asthma in the <ORGANIZATION>West Pennine
Health Authority</ORGANIZATION>. We are a flagship – the <ORGANIZATION>Health Authority</ORGANIZATION> can always
compare the quality of this practice to any other and ask why the other
practice is underperforming’.
13.32 It may be that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was ahead of his time in the practice of preventive medicine. He
had clinics for the monitoring of diabetes, congestive heart failure and high blood
pressure. He had begun to call patients in for regular health checks at a time when
many doctors had not yet begun to do so. Yet his boasting was a most unattractive trait.
13.33 Although <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was generally admired, there were quite a large number of people in
<PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> who disliked him. Their usual criticism was that he was arrogant. He appeared
arrogant and conceited, even during his trial. When he gave evidence, he boasted
about his achievements at the practice. He was asked about a patient’s blood pressure,
which on a particular occasion was 140/80. Counsel suggested to him that that was a
perfectly acceptable level. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> replied that it might be for many doctors but he
aimed for ‘ perfection’.
185
13.34 He plainly thought he was by far the best doctor in <LOCATION>Hyde</LOCATION>. His patients seemed to agree.
Dr <PERSON>Patel</PERSON>, who worked for him as a locum in the early 1990s, said that patients
would often refuse to be seen by him and would prefer to wait until <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had
returned to work. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> would not allow a locum to immunise his child patients. He
had to do it himself.
13.35 <PERSON>Dr Bills</PERSON>, who worked with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> during the <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook</ORGANIZATION> years, says that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
often described a patient’s condition to his colleagues in very florid terms, for example,
saying that the patient had pneumonia, when in truth he or she had only a moderate
chest infection. Then, when the patient recovered, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> would claim credit for the
cure. One of his more frequent boasts was about his success in treating heart disease.
He prescribed medication very freely and liked to impress upon his colleagues how
successful his treatment was. This was strange because, if anyone had examined the
number of deaths from coronary heart disease among his patients, it would have been
found to be quite high. That was not because a large number of his patients died of that
disease but because coronary thrombosis was his favourite ‘ cause of death’ for a
patient he had killed.
13.36 To some extent, one can see why <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> became conceited. He obviously relished
his good reputation in <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> and the adulation accorded to him by so many of his
patients. He seems to have enjoyed almost celebrity status among his patients. One of
his victims, <PERSON>Mrs Florence Lewis</PERSON>, was delighted when she was taken on to his list. Her
son said that it was almost as if she had won the lottery.
13.37 Another manifestation of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s conceit was the delight he took in ‘ taking on’ and
getting the better of officials and those in authority. He conducted a long-running battle
with the <ORGANIZATION>Health Authority</ORGANIZATION> about his expensive prescribing habits and funding for his
practice. There might be many doctors whose sympathies would lie with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> on
these issues, but the point is that he seemed to revel in this kind of dispute and the
language in which he addressed the officials was at times unpleasant, aggressive and
conceited.
13.38 Another unattractive trait was <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s habit of humiliating people whom he felt were
not doing their jobs properly. One example concerned a young female sales
representative from a drug company, who attended a meeting of the <ORGANIZATION>Donneybrook</ORGANIZATION>
doctors. She was nervous and inexperienced and perhaps not quite as knowledgeable
about her products as she should have been. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was quite ruthless in his criticism
of her and seemed to enjoy the fact that he had reduced her to tears.
13.39 Dr <PERSON>Hardman</PERSON>, a medical referee, recalls attending a lecture at which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was in the
audience. He kept interrupting and disagreeing with the visiting lecturer in a very
pompous way. His behaviour became an embarrassment to those who knew him.
13.40 I have been able to form my own view of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s arrogance by listening to tapes
of the police interviews of September and October 1998. He was interviewed on
7th September and on 5th October. On each occasion, he began confidently and
treated the police officers in a patronising and arrogant way. They continued steadily
and, as the evidence was put to him, his attitude gradually changed until, at the end of
186
The Shipman Inquiry
5th October, when it was clear that the police knew that he had falsified medical records
on his computer, he broke down and was unable to continue with the interview.
Dishonesty
13.41 An important trait in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s personality is that he is profoundly dishonest.
His dishonesty was first revealed in 1975 when it was found that he had dishonestly
obtained large quantities of pethidine by deception and kept them for his own use.
I have described these offences in Chapter One. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> pleaded guilty to offences
of dishonesty – in effect, forgery and theft – which had taken place over a
prolonged period.
13.42 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> regularly obtained large quantities of diamorphine by similar dishonest means
during the 1990s. As I have explained earlier in this Report, I have every reason to
believe that he employed the same methods in the 1980s, although the records, which
would prove the point beyond doubt, have been destroyed.
13.43 Obtaining drugs was not <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s only dishonesty. He was an accomplished and
inventive liar. He could lie spontaneously to get himself out of a difficult situation and did
so on countless occasions. Even at a very early stage in his career, in July 1975, when
the <ORGANIZATION>Home Office Drugs Inspectorate</ORGANIZATION> and <ORGANIZATION>West Yorkshire Police Drugs Squad</ORGANIZATION> first
suspected him of stealing pethidine, he so impressed them in interview that they took
matters no further, at least for the time being. He had told them a pack of lies.
13.44 Sometimes, when he had killed a patient, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was caught almost red-handed. Yet,
he was able to invent an explanation for the death without showing any noticeable
discomfiture. This arose in the case of Mrs Maria West. <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> was entertaining her
friend, <PERSON>Mrs Marian Hadfield</PERSON>, during the afternoon of 6th March 1995. The two women
were sitting in Mrs West’s front room when <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> wanted to use the bathroom.
The bathroom was upstairs and the staircase led out of the kitchen. While she was
upstairs, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived, to find Mrs West apparently alone. When <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> came
downstairs, she could hear voices in the front room, realised the doctor had arrived and
stayed in the kitchen. Within a few minutes of his arrival, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed Mrs West.
<PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> realised that the conversation had stopped. A few moments later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
came into the kitchen. One would have thought that he would have been completely
thrown off balance by the realisation that <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> had been only a few feet away
while he was killing <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON>. He looked a little surprised to see her but confidently
explained that <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> had collapsed and died. He had come into the kitchen to look
for her son, so he said.
13.45 There are many other examples of the confidence with which he would tell lies and act
them out. He would quite often tell a relative (untruthfully) that he had summoned an
ambulance on finding the patient in a serious condition. He would then say that the
patient had died, so he had cancelled the ambulance. On some occasions, he would
actually go through the charade of picking up the telephone, dialling a number and
pretending to speak to the ambulance control centre to make the cancellation.
187
13.46 His dishonesty is well illustrated by the way in which he fabricated medical records to
invent plausible explanations for deaths that he had caused. He also made countless
false entries on MCCDs and cremation certificates. Indeed, in respect of his duties of
certification, he was frequently dishonest, even in cases where he had not killed the
patient and had no need to invent a cover story. He was, in short, a consummate and
inveterate liar.
Addiction
13.47 There is evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was addicted to pethidine in the 1970s. He claimed that
he was addicted to it and it seems likely that he was. He certainly obtained large
quantities and injected himself (the marks were seen by Detective Sergeant <PERSON>McKeating</PERSON>
at the time) and it is clear that he suffered a number of blackouts. It is possible that he
was already abusing that drug while working as a house officer in the <ORGANIZATION>Department of
Obstetrics and Gynaecology</ORGANIZATION> at <LOCATION>Pontefract General Hospital</LOCATION>, where pethidine would
have been in regular use. There is no evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> ever resumed any
personal abuse of controlled drugs after his rehabilitation in late 1975 and early 1976.
13.48 When challenged about his drug taking in 1975, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that he had taken to it
because he had become depressed and unhappy about his work and his relationships
with his partners. His partners were unaware of any signs of depression and did not
think there were difficulties within the practice. The psychiatrists say that the reason why
many people become addicted to drugs is that they are depressed or anxious or deeply
unhappy. There is no obvious reason why <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> should have been depressed,
anxious or deeply unhappy in the 1970s. He had achieved his ambition to become a
doctor, which, at that time, was a considerable achievement for someone from his
background. He was married and had a young family. Although the marriage had not
taken place under ideal circumstances (<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was a student and <PERSON>Mrs Shipman</PERSON> was
pregnant) it does not appear to have been unhappy. It has certainly stood the test of
time. However, the psychiatrists stress (and I accept) that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have had all
manner of underlying problems. We simply do not know. It seems to me that whatever
problem it was that drove him to pethidine addiction in the 1970s was almost certainly
never resolved and probably became a permanent part of his make-up.
13.49 The psychiatrists say that a person who has one addiction is quite likely to be subject to
other forms of addiction. I think it likely that whatever it was that caused <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to
become addicted to pethidine also led to other forms of addictive behaviour. It is
possible that he was addicted to killing.
What Does This Constellation of Traits Reveal?
13.50 This is not an attractive constellation of traits. However, it is by no means unique or even
particularly uncommon. I have talked to the psychiatrists about <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
characteristics. They have made some tentative suggestions about his underlying
personality but stress that these are only theories and cannot be demonstrated without
formal assessment. They suggest that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> may have a rigid and obsessive
personality. They think he may be isolated and may have difficulty in expressing
188
The Shipman Inquiry
emotions. They suggest that his arrogance and over-confidence are almost certainly a
mask for poor self-esteem. They think that, for most of his adult life, he was probably
angry, deeply unhappy and chronically depressed. They suggest that he has a deepseated
need to control people and events. Once he fears that he cannot control events,
he feels threatened and reacts so as to take or regain control.
13.51 It is clear that these traits are not in themselves enough to explain why <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> became
a serial killer. On the evidence available, the psychiatrists cannot explain how this
melange of characteristics could lead to such extreme conduct. Even if <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> also
has unresolved feelings of grief about the loss of his mother at an impressionable age,
there is still not enough to explain his later conduct. There must be something else,
much more significant. The psychiatrists say that they cannot discover this without many
hours of discussion with him. They postulate the theory that he could be psychotic,
although they stress there is no evidence that he is. They think that his actions must be
the product of a diseased mind but are unable to shed any light on the nature of that
disease. They suggest the possibility that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> might have developed a fear of
death and a need to control death. It is possible that he has a morbid interest in death.
It is possible that he might have experienced a ‘ buzz’ of pleasure from association with
death. It is also possible that death might have given him a sense of relief from
some intolerable pressure or anxiety. In short, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> may have had a need to kill.
Any of these attitudes towards death, present in conjunction with an addictive
personality, prone to obsessive and repetitive behaviour, might go some way towards
providing an explanation.
13.52 There is not a great deal of evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had a morbid interest in death or
derived pleasure from killing or from the circumstances of death. There is some,
however. Mrs <PERSON>Judith Page</PERSON>, a patient of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> who worked as a home help, reported
that one morning, during a consultation in his surgery, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> remarked to her that in
the course of her work, he supposed she must sometimes find a client dead. She
agreed that this had happened on one or two occasions and added that she had found
it very upsetting, as she had become fond of her elderly clients. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s response
was to ask her whether she did not find that it gave her ‘ a buzz’.
13.53 Some evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had a morbid interest in death may be seen on the
occasion of <PERSON>Mrs Mavis Pickup</PERSON>’s death, when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> came to the house to examine
Mrs Pickup’s body and to certify the cause of death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s young son, who was
then aged about 11 or 12, was with him, sitting outside in the car. While <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was
waiting for the arrival of the funeral director, he went outside to bring his son in to see the
body. The boy declined to come.
13.54 There are some circumstances from which I think it is reasonable to infer that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
either enjoyed killing or felt compelled to go in search of a victim. On 15th April 1984, a
Sunday, he was on out of hours duty. In the afternoon, he was called out to see a patient
(who died later that day). He dealt with her and was then free to return home. Whereas
most doctors would be only too pleased to return home and resume their leisure
activities, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> preferred to make an unsolicited visit to Mr <PERSON>Joseph Bardsley</PERSON> and,
189
under the pretext that he needed to take a blood sample, injected Mr <PERSON>Bardsley</PERSON> and
killed him.
13.55 The case of Mrs <PERSON>Leah Fogg</PERSON>, who died on Monday, 10th June 1996, shows <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
urge to kill as soon as he became aware of an available victim. On Friday, 7th June
1996, <PERSON>Mrs Fogg</PERSON>’s daughter, <PERSON>Mrs Marjorie Stafford</PERSON>, visited <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> because she was
concerned that her mother was depressed and not coping with the loss of her husband
some years before. <PERSON>Mrs Stafford</PERSON> had noticed a sign in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s waiting room that said
that counselling services were available at the surgery. <PERSON>Mrs Stafford</PERSON> hoped that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> would arrange for her mother to receive bereavement counselling. However,
she was concerned that her mother should not know that she had been to see <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>,
as it was ‘ behind her back’. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> promised to call on <PERSON>Mrs Fogg</PERSON> and said that he
would do so unannounced. He did so three days after their talk, and killed <PERSON>Mrs Fogg</PERSON>.
It would have been far less risky to wait a few weeks before killing her.
13.56 Usually, when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had killed, he did not linger at the scene. This may have been
because he was very busy and was due back at the surgery. However, I have the
impression that after a death, when the relatives had assembled, he would enjoy acting
as ‘ master of ceremonies’. He would be the centre of attention and would take control.
He would present himself as omniscient. He would give instructions about the removal
of the body. He would give his explanation for the death, often saying that, although it
might have been a surprise to the relatives, it had been no surprise to him. He might add
remarks such as ‘ she was riddled with cancer’, as he said of Miss <PERSON>Lena Slater</PERSON>. Relatives
would often be grateful to him and pleased that he had been present at the death.
13.57 The evidence that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was fascinated by death is slight but not negligible. There is
no evidence from which I could directly infer that he had a fear of death or a need to
control it. There is some evidence that he is an addictive personality and it is possible
that killing was a form of addiction. I do not think he can have had any concept of the
value or sanctity of human life. I regret to say that I can shed very little light on why
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed his patients. I do, however, think that it is possible to gain some insight
into his thinking from an examination of which patients he chose to kill.
The Selection of Patients
13.58 Statistically, it is clear that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killed mainly elderly women living alone. He also
killed some men and they too were usually elderly and living alone. In general, he killed
people who were in poor health. Some of the earliest killings were of patients who were
terminally ill or very unwell. Many of his victims were frail and in poor general health.
I have already referred to what <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said about the elderly to the family of Mrs <PERSON>Mary
Coutts</PERSON> after her death, namely, that he did not believe in ‘ keeping them going’.
Mrs <PERSON>Kathlyn Kaye</PERSON>, the daughter of <PERSON>Mrs Annie Powers</PERSON>, told the Inquiry that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told
her elderly parents that, if they were animals, he would have them put down. He may
have regarded this as a joke but Mr and <PERSON>Mrs Powers</PERSON> did not. Nor did <PERSON>Mrs Kaye</PERSON>, when
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> repeated the remark to her. I think this remark reveals something of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
attitude to elderly people.
190
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry</ORGANIZATION>
13.59 <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> seemed to think that he knew when a patient ought to die. He quite often said
that it was ‘ for the best’ that the patient should have died when he or she did. It was
better that ‘ she should not suffer’. The patient would not have wanted to ‘ live in a
wheelchair’, or ‘ be a vegetable’, or have to stay in hospital ‘ with wires coming out of her’,
