The data reveal a distinct pattern. Only a small number of cases in the A, B or C categories
occur until 1984, and the majority of these are C cases. Beginning in 1984, there is a marked
increase in cases in the A or B categories, more than 40% of the total annual deaths being in
these categories in the four years 1986–1989. Between 1990 and 1992, only two cases were
categorised as A and one as B. Thereafter, a second peak in the numbers of unlawful killings
occurred during the six years 1993–1998. In these six years, more than half the deaths for
which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had issued an MCCD were categorised either A or B, or were convictions. The
possible reasons for these patterns are discussed in the main body of the Report.

234 APPENDIX A
The Shipman Inquiry
Section 8. Summary and Conclusions
This Appendix has compared the findings from the 2001 review of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s clinical practice
and the determinations of the Inquiry. The relationship between the numbers of cases in each
category and the features typical of the convictions has been investigated, and additional
analyses undertaken to explore the excess numbers of deaths in institutions. Finally, the pattern
of cases in different categories emerging during <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s career as a general practitioner has
been outlined.
The findings indicate that the review and the Inquiry have reached similar conclusions about
the total numbers of unlawful killings. The Inquiry had very much more information available,
and its exhaustive case by case investigation supports the view that the excess of deaths is in
the region of 220–240. One implication is that a system to monitor the death rates of patients of
general practitioners would have detected the excess.
The review relied on the documentary evidence contained in clinical records and cremation
forms to assess the level of suspicion in each case. There was a reasonable level of
consistency between these assessments of suspicion and the definitive determinations of the
Inquiry. However, caution should be taken in assuming on this basis that review of records
would be sufficient to detect cases of murder by health professionals. Prior to the review, the
key features of murder had been established during the trial, and were used to inform the
review. Furthermore, there were some cases in which the evidence obtained by the Inquiry led
to a classification different to that following review of records and cremation forms only.
The cases in A and B categories differ substantially from those in the other categories. In the A
and B cases, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> is more likely to have attended the patient on the day of death, to be
recorded as being present at death, and death was more likely in the afternoon, to have
occurred in less than 30 minutes, to be certified as due to heart conditions or strokes, and less
likely to occur at weekends. This distinctive pattern is not found among the closed or E
category cases, and is much less common among D cases. Nevertheless, from this limited
evidence, it is not possible to entirely rule out the possibility that a few D cases were in fact
unlawful killings.
The excess of deaths in institutions is not due to a large number of unlawful killings. Although a
small number of deaths in institutions were categorised A or B, the findings indicate that the
most likely explanation is that, between 1978 and 1984, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was the preferred general
practitioner for people in one residential home in particular. From 1993, there was a second
peak in excess deaths among people in institutions, and this was associated with deaths in a
nursing home. It is likely that this finding is largely a consequence of an increased use of
nursing homes during these years.
The emergence of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s activities during his career can be traced through the variations
in the annual numbers of cases categorised as A, B or C. In the early years, the number of C
category cases steadily accumulates. From the mid–1980s, the numbers of A and B cases rise
steeply, to be followed by a period of few deaths (1990–1992). In the 1990s, the numbers of A
and B cases rise again and, in the final six years, consistently more than half of all deaths
certified by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> fall into these categories; in one year (1997), the proportion exceeds
three-quarters.

248 APPENDIX E
APPENDIX E 249
<PERSON>Lizzie Adams</PERSON>
Introduction
Mrs <PERSON>Lizzie Adams</PERSON> died on 28th February 1997 at the age of 77. She was a widow and lived
alone.
Personal Background
She had been a professional ballroom dancer and was still keen on dancing. For her age, she
was physically very fit. On 26th February, she had returned from a holiday in <LOCATION>Malta</LOCATION> with a group
of friends, including her dancing partner. She had a cold and a cough. On 27th February, she
spent the day shopping in <LOCATION>Stockport</LOCATION> with her daughter, <PERSON>Mrs Doreen Thorley</PERSON>. By the end of the
day, she was tired and a little troubled by her cough. At 4pm, <PERSON>Mrs Thorley</PERSON> collected a
prescription for an antibiotic (<ORGANIZATION>Ceporex</ORGANIZATION>) from <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery, had it dispensed and took it to
her mother.
The Circumstances of the Death
The following morning, Friday, 28th February, <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> told <PERSON>Mrs Thorley</PERSON> that the antibiotic
had ‘ nearly blown her head off’. On her daughter’s advice, at 12.10pm, <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> telephoned
the surgery. A note made by the receptionist on the visits request form recorded that
<PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> felt dizzy, sick and wobbly and wanted a visit. During that morning, <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON>
washed some clothes by hand and hung them out on the washing line. She also partly cooked
her evening meal. When <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived at the house, in the early afternoon, the ironing board
and iron were set up in the kitchen ready for use.
In the mid-afternoon, Mr <PERSON>William Catlow</PERSON>, a friend of <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON>, arrived at the house. He found
the door unlocked and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> in the front room looking into a china cabinet. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told
him that <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> was ill and he was sending her to hospital. When Mr <PERSON>Catlow</PERSON> reached her,
he found <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> apparently asleep in her chair. He took her hand, which felt warm. He
said to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, ‘ I think she’s fainted’. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> came over and said, ‘ She’s gone’. He said he
would cancel the ambulance. There was no medical examination.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> telephoned the <PERSON>Thorley</PERSON> home and, according to Mr <PERSON>Thorley</PERSON>, said that he had ordered
an ambulance to take <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> to hospital. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> then telephoned <PERSON>Mrs Thorley</PERSON> at work
and said her mother had to go to hospital. He did not say she was dead. <PERSON>Mrs Thorley</PERSON> went
quickly to her mother’s home where she found her mother in her chair, with her legs crossed in
a comfortable way. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told her that her mother had died of pneumonia. He was very
abrupt with her. He told her there was no need for a post-mortem examination, as he had been
present at the death.
The <ORGANIZATION>Defence Case
Shipman</ORGANIZATION>’s account, which the jury must have rejected, was that, when he arrived in the early
afternoon, <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> was obviously poorly. She came to the door slowly to let him in. She led
him back into the living room at the rear of the house. She said that she was breathless, she felt
unwell and had a cough. On examination he found that her heart was racing. She was clammy
to touch and looked pale. On listening to her chest, he heard fine crackles. Her lips were
slightly blue. He thought she had bronchopneumonia in both lungs. He told her she should be
250 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
in hospital. She said she had been just as ill as this on other occasions and had stayed at
home. At her suggestion, he went into the front room to find a telephone to speak to
<PERSON>Mrs Thorley</PERSON>. While he was there, Mr <PERSON>Catlow</PERSON> arrived. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> explained the situation and said
that he was about to telephone the daughter, as <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> ought to be admitted to hospital.
After a few seconds, Mr <PERSON>Catlow</PERSON> called him into the living room and said that <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> was
not very well. He carried out a full external examination and concluded that she had died. He
thought the cause was bronchopneumonia. He explained to Mr <PERSON>Catlow</PERSON> that he could not
resuscitate her on account of the infection. He summoned <PERSON>Mrs Thorley</PERSON> and, when she arrived,
he gave her a full explanation of what had happened and asked her if she wanted a postmortem
examination. She did not.
The following day, <PERSON>Mrs Sonja Jones</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON>’ other daughter, went to see <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. He
gave her an account which tallied with his evidence to the court save that she claimed that he
told her he had telephoned for an ambulance and had cancelled it later after <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> had
died. It was admitted at trial that no call was made from the house to the ambulance service.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> denied ever having said he had called for or cancelled an ambulance. <PERSON>Mrs Jones</PERSON> also
claimed that she asked <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> whether there should be a post-mortem examination in view of
the suddenness of the death but he said it was not necessary. That was also disputed.
Certification
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> signed the Medical Certificate of Cause of Death (MCCD), saying that the cause of
death was bronchopneumonia of 12 to 24 hours’ duration. He completed cremation Form B
saying that death had occurred at 14.50, some 50 minutes after his arrival. He said that a
neighbour (Mr <PERSON>Catlow</PERSON>) was present at the death. He certified that he carried out a complete
external examination. In evidence, he claimed that he undertook a modified external
examination, which included looking at the pupils, the retina and listening to the heart through
<PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON>’ clothing. Mr <PERSON>Catlow</PERSON> said that no examination at all was carried out at that time.
The Expert Evidence
Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON> examined the medical records. On the visits request form, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had
written:
‘ Chest infection green phlegm 120pm irregular chest pains central L
shoulder. feels ill bronchopneumonia’.
There was no reference to any proposed treatment or to calling an ambulance or to the death.
The computerised records contained entries made on the day after the death. They said:
‘ Bronchopneumonia due to unspecified organism.
very poorly arrange to admit 1430 hrs’.
‘ O/E – dead
friend present daughter telephoned 1450 hrs’.
<PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON>’ <PERSON>Lloyd George</PERSON> records were found in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s garage after his arrest. They
showed that <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> had suffered from a number of chronic conditions, none of which was
related to the death. 
APPENDIX E 251
Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> said that the antibiotic, <PERSON>Ceporex</PERSON>, could cause stomach upset, dizziness and
nausea. In his view, <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON>’ deterioration on the morning of her death was probably due to
an adverse reaction to the antibiotic. It was possible that her chest infection had worsened but,
as she did not complain to the receptionist of breathlessness or deterioration in her cough
when she telephoned the surgery, that was unlikely. He said that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s note of the
consultation on the visits request form was consistent with a severe chest infection but he
thought it very strange that the fatal outcome had not been recorded. He said that, if
<PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> had been as ill as <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed she was when he arrived, he should have sat
her down in the front room and examined her there. If, as he claimed, he was out of the room
when <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> ‘ fainted’, he should have summoned an ambulance and tried to resuscitate
her. Her chest infection would have been treatable. This was a sudden and unexpected death
which should have been reported to the coroner.
Comment
The following significant points arise in this case:
• The death took place in Mrs <PERSON>Adams</PERSON>’ home and in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s presence. This is a factor
which gives rise to a high degree of suspicion. A natural death at home during a doctor’s
visit is an extremely rare event in the experience of most general practitioners. If <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
were to be believed, natural deaths occurred frequently during his home visits.
• The death was extremely sudden. As Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> has explained, death from
bronchopneumonia is not as sudden as this. The patient is very ill for at least a few hours
before death. <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> had been out shopping the previous day. That morning she had
done her usual household jobs.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s treatment of the condition he claimed to have found was inappropriate,
according to Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON>. If <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> had been truly as ill as <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> later claimed, he
should have summoned an ambulance immediately. If she had been unwilling to go to
hospital, he should have spoken to <PERSON>Mrs Thorley</PERSON>. If <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON> really had collapsed, he
should have tried to resuscitate her. As it was obvious that he had not made any attempt to
do so, he made an untenable attempt to justify his decision. He should have made a
complete and thorough note of the consultation, <PERSON>Mrs Adams</PERSON>’ reluctance to go to hospital,
the steps he had taken in the face of that reluctance and the events leading to the death.
He did not.
• Mrs <PERSON>Adams</PERSON> was found sitting in a chair as though asleep. This position and appearance are
not typical of a death following bronchopneumonia but are entirely typical of those observed
at the deaths of many of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims.
<PERSON>• Shipman</PERSON> told others that the patient had been advised she needed hospital admission but
that she was reluctant to agree. This is an explanation <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was to give on many other
occasions.
<PERSON>• Shipman</PERSON> told others he had called an ambulance and cancelled it but no such call was
made. At trial, he denied having said this. 
252 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
• A witness says that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not carry out any examination to diagnose death whereas
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed he did. This is a feature common to many cases.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s claim on cremation Form B that Mr <PERSON>Catlow</PERSON> was present at the death is
misleading.
<PERSON>• Shipman</PERSON> claimed that he suggested a post-mortem examination but the witnesses said that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told them this was not necessary. The death was not reported to the coroner
although, as Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> said, such a report was plainly called for.
APPENDIX E 253
<PERSON>Muriel Grimshaw</PERSON>
Introduction
Mrs <PERSON>Muriel Grimshaw</PERSON> died on 14th July 1997 at the age of 76. She was in very good general
health. The medical records show regular prescriptions for medication to control her blood
pressure and occasional episodes of lumbago. She attended the surgery periodically for
routine checks but had not consulted her doctor since May 1997, when she had suffered an
episode of back pain. She had recovered from this after buying a new bed. In the weeks before
her death, she had been well and had led a full and active life. After exhumation of her body,
morphine was found in the tissues consistent with the administration of a lethal dose.
Personal Background
<PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON>’s daughter, Mrs <PERSON>Ann Brown</PERSON>, used to see her mother regularly on Sundays and
Thursdays. They spoke by telephone on most days. On Sunday 13th July, <PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON> and
<PERSON>Mrs Brown</PERSON> went to church together. After church, they had a cup of coffee and a chat at
Mrs <PERSON>Grimshaw</PERSON>’s house. They agreed to have lunch together on the coming Wednesday.
<PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON> was in good health and spirits on that day.
The Circumstances of the Death
Each Tuesday, <PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON> went shopping with her friend, <PERSON>Mrs Ryan</PERSON>. Early on the morning of
Tuesday 15th July 1997, <PERSON>Mrs Barbara Ryan</PERSON> called to collect her. There was no answer when
she rang the doorbell, so she contacted <PERSON>Mrs Brown</PERSON>. They entered the house and found
<PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON>, lying dead on her bed, fully dressed in day clothes. The television had been left
on and the curtains were open. It would have been most unusual for <PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON> to leave the
television on when she went to bed; she always unplugged it at night. <PERSON>Mrs Brown</PERSON> telephoned
for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. On arrival, he looked at the body but did not touch it. He announced that
<PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON> had died at about 5.30pm the previous day. He said that it had been a nice way
to go. There would be no need to inform the coroner. He did not say anything about the cause
of death.
Later he signed a Medical Certificate of Cause of Death (MCCD), stating that death had been
due to a cerebrovascular accident with hypertension as an underlying condition. He stated that
he had last seen Mrs <PERSON>Grimshaw</PERSON> alive on 2nd July. However, <PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON>’s medical records
did not contain any reference to a consultation on that day and <PERSON>Mrs Brown</PERSON> said that her mother
had not seen <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> since May.
The Defence Case
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account in evidence, which the jury must have rejected, was that <PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON>
suffered from hypertension and rheumatoid arthritis. He claimed that he had decided to call on
her on 2nd July because he had noticed that the previous day she had asked for a repeat
prescription for her medication. It was true that she had asked for repeat prescriptions; they
were recorded on the computer. He said that he recalled that the last time he had seen her,
which was when she had lumbago in May, she had not been entirely well. When he visited on
2nd July, he found her well, although her back was still a little troublesome. <PERSON>Mrs Brown</PERSON> said
that, to her knowledge, he had not called and her mother’s back was not troubling her in July.
254 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he called to see <PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON> again at about 1pm on 14th July. He found
her having a cup of tea. She said her back was giving no trouble. He went back to the surgery
and made an entry timed at 2.06pm in which he recorded that visit. An entry was indeed made
at that time; it said only that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had seen the patient in her own home. He said that the
next thing he knew about <PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON> was that he was called to her house the next day as
her body had been found. He said that he examined the body and found that rigor mortis was
setting in. From a discussion with <PERSON>Mrs Brown</PERSON> about her mother’s habits, he had been able to
estimate the time of death at 6pm. <PERSON>Mrs Brown</PERSON> denied that any such discussion had taken
place. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he had diagnosed the death as being due to a stroke associated with
hypertension. He considered that <PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON> had not had a heart attack. The onset of her
symptoms had been slow enough to allow her to lie down, whereas, if she had had a heart
attack, she would have fallen where she was. At the time, it did not occur to him to think that a
post-mortem was necessary. He had offered one to <PERSON>Mrs Brown</PERSON> but she had declined.
The Expert Evidence
Post-mortem examination showed no significant abnormality apart from the presence of
morphine in the tissues. There was some atherosclerosis in the coronary arteries but this was
not of such severity as to be likely to cause a heart attack in the absence of stress on the heart
due to physiological trauma. There was no bleeding into the brain and no sign of either a
haemorrhagic or occlusive stroke. Although Dr <PERSON>Rutherford</PERSON> could not rule out the possibility that
<PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON> had had a stroke, there was nothing to suggest that she had and the presence
of morphine in the body tissues drove him to the conclusion that she had died as the result of
morphine toxicity. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had no explanation for the presence of the morphine.
The medical records showed that <PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON> had had hypertension since 1989 but this was
well controlled with medication. Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON> said that it would not have been possible for
any doctor to diagnose the cause of death on the information available and the death should
have been reported to the coroner.
Comment
This is a clear case of murder in which morphine was found in the body. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must have
made a completely unsolicited visit on 14th July to a patient whom he had not seen since May
and who was in completely normal health. He must have found some pretext for giving her an
injection. The significant features are:
• The sudden death of this healthy woman was discovered very soon after a visit at which
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> saw her alone.
• When <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was summoned, <PERSON>Mrs Brown</PERSON> said that he did not even examine the body
sufficiently to establish that <PERSON>Mrs Grimshaw</PERSON> was dead. This is an observation made by many
relatives in cases in which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> has killed. The inference to be drawn is that he already
knew that the patient was dead.
• There was no feasible natural explanation for the sudden death and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was not in a
position to certify the cause of death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> should have refused to certify the cause of
death and the death would then have had to be reported to the coroner. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed
APPENDIX E 255
that <PERSON>Mrs Brown</PERSON> had refused his suggestion that there should be a post-mortem examination.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> often said that relatives had refused or had ‘ not wanted’ a post-mortem
examination. It is not a matter for the relatives to decide. It is for the doctor to decide
whether he or she is in a position to issue the MCCD. In any event, <PERSON>Mrs Brown</PERSON> says that
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> made no such suggestion.

The Shipman Inquiry
<PERSON>Kathleen Grundy</PERSON>
Introduction
Mrs <PERSON>Kathleen Grundy</PERSON> died on 24th June 1998 at the age of 81. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified her death as
having been due to old age but scientific analysis of body tissues following exhumation of her
body in August 1998 showed that she had died of morphine poisoning.
Personal Background
<PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> was a widow and lived alone. She was in remarkably good health for her age. She
led a busy social life and worked for many charitable organisations, including Age Concern
and <ORGANIZATION>Werneth House</ORGANIZATION>, a day centre for the elderly. She spent the evening before her death with a
friend and was in normal health when she went home.
The Circumstances of the Death
In the few days before her death, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had inveigled <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> into agreeing to take part
in a research project into the ageing process, supposedly to be conducted by <ORGANIZATION>Manchester
University</ORGANIZATION>. This was a ruse by which means he obtained a sample of her signature (which he
used in an attempt to forge a will) and also created an excuse to visit her at home. She visited
him on 23rd June, to have her ears syringed, and he told her that he needed a blood sample,
for the research project, which must be taken early in the morning. He arranged to visit her at
about 8.30am the next morning.
The next day, she was due to attend <ORGANIZATION>Werneth House</ORGANIZATION> but she did not arrive. Friends and
colleagues there became concerned and two of them, Mr <PERSON>John Green</PERSON> and Mr <PERSON>Ronald Pickford</PERSON>,
went to her house at about midday. They found her, lying on the sofa, fully dressed. She was
dead and her body was cold. The door to the house was unlocked. They summoned <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
to the house. Following a perfunctory examination of the body, he said ‘ cardiac arrest’. He had
a brief discussion with someone in the coroner’s office, in which it was agreed that a certificate,
which stated the cause of death to be ‘ old age’, would be acceptable. No record was kept of
the conversation with the coroner’s office.
When <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had left the house, Mr <PERSON>Green</PERSON> informed the police, as he was unable to contact
<PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s daughter, <PERSON>Mrs Angela Woodruff</PERSON>. The officers concerned (PCs <PERSON>John Fitzgerald</PERSON>
and <PERSON>Neil Phillips</PERSON>) spoke to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> later. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told them he had called on <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>
earlier that day because she had been unwell. He did not say he had called to take a blood
sample. He said he had spoken with the coroner’s office and he was going to issue a certificate
stating that <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> had died of natural causes. The police officers took a quick look at the
body and, on seeing nothing suspicious, took no further action.
The day after the death, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> spoke to <PERSON>Mrs Woodruff</PERSON>. He told her that he had seen
Mrs <PERSON>Grundy</PERSON> on the day before her death, just for ‘ a routine thing’. He was vague and mentioned
chest pain, possibly due to indigestion. He said he had arranged to collect a blood sample the
next morning. When he arrived, she was not yet dressed. He then said that some old people
complain of feeling unwell a few days before they die and then just die. He implied that this had
happened to <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>. He handed <PERSON>Mrs Woodruff</PERSON> the Medical Certificate of Cause of Death
(MCCD) and said that he had certified the death as being due to old age. 
APPENDIX E 257
The <ORGANIZATION>Defence Case
Shipman</ORGANIZATION>’s account of this death, which the jury must have rejected, was that for some time he
had suspected that <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> might be abusing drugs. He claimed that he had become
concerned about her general health. He decided he would like to take a blood sample in order
to check for diabetes and anaemia and suchlike. When she came into the surgery on 23rd
June, he thought she looked in poor health and he decided to do extensive tests. He arranged
to visit her the next morning to take a blood sample. This had to be available for collection
before 11am, so he visited at 8.30am. She was dressed in her housecoat. She looked old and
moved slowly. He took the sample and left. That morning he was so busy he forgot to send the
sample for testing. He threw it away, thinking that he would have to obtain another. (It should
be noted that in interview with the police he claimed that he had sent the sample to the
laboratory but they had lost it.) Soon after midday, he was summoned to <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s house,
where he did a full examination of the body and found she was dead. She was wearing
different clothes from when he had seen her earlier, implying that she must have dressed
herself after he left. He thought she had been dead since about 10am. When confronted with
the results of the forensic tests following the post-mortem examination, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> suggested that
<PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> must have administered the morphine to herself.
<PERSON>Neighbours</PERSON> and relatives gave evidence that <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> would never have left the door
unlocked. She was very conscious of the need for security. If she had not left the door unlocked
herself after letting <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> out, the inference was that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must have let himself out of
the house after killing her and had been unable to lock the door behind him.
There was no record of a blood sample being received at the pathology laboratory.
The Expert Evidence
Dr <PERSON>Rutherford</PERSON>, the pathologist who conducted the post-mortem examination, found no natural
explanation for <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s death. She had been in good health. Scientific analysis of the
body tissues revealed levels of morphine consistent with the administration of a fatal dose.
Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON> said that there were a number of false entries in the medical records. These
had been created after the death to give credence to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s stories first that <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>
was under the weather when he saw her on 23rd June and second that <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> had been
abusing drugs and might have administered the morphine herself. These false records
comprised veiled expressions of his supposed suspicion that <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> was taking drugs
and of his supposed decision not to confront her with his suspicions.
The Forging of the <PERSON>Will
Shipman</PERSON>’s attempt at forging <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s will was crude and hopelessly incompetent.
First, he sought to obtain the whole of <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s substantial estate, leaving nothing to her
well-loved daughter and grandchildren. Second, he chose to forge the will of a woman whose
daughter was a solicitor, who might therefore be expected to know something about her
mother’s previous testamentary arrangements. Third, he drafted the will using his own oldfashioned
Brother portable typewriter. When the police came to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s premises and took
258 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
possession of the typewriter, the will was immediately linked to him. The product looked
thoroughly unprofessional and it was wholly foreseeable that it would arouse suspicion.
Fourth, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> forged <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s signature and dated the will 9th June 1998. On that day,
he staged a ‘ signing and witnessing’ event in his consulting room. He must have prepared a
document for Mrs Grundy to sign, which purported to provide for her consent to take part in
some medical research supposedly to be conducted by <ORGANIZATION>Manchester University</ORGANIZATION>. This document
required that <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s signature should be witnessed by two others, who also had to sign
and provide their names, addresses and occupations. While <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> was at <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
surgery on 9th June, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> appears to have obtained her signature on this document and
then called two patients from his waiting room into the consulting room where they completed
and signed the witnesses’ part of the document. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must then have copied the three
signatures as well as he could. The forgeries were obviously poor and would have aroused
suspicion even from a non-expert. Expert evidence soon proved them to be forgeries.
Fifth, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> delivered the forged will with a covering letter to a firm of solicitors in <LOCATION>Hyde</LOCATION> with
whom <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> had had no previous dealings. The solicitor who opened the letter was very
puzzled and put it to one side. He had received it on 24th June 1998. Only six days later he
received another letter, apparently signed by someone called <PERSON>Smith</PERSON>, who did not exist. This
had been written by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> on his own typewriter. It informed the solicitor of <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s
death and advised him that her daughter could be contacted at <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON>’s house. The
solicitor contracted <PERSON>Mrs Woodruff</PERSON> and sent her the will. She was immediately suspicious and
set in train the investigations which led to the detection of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s crimes.
Comment
This was a clear case of morphine poisoning, proved by the post-mortem results. Even leaving
that evidence out of account, there are a number of noteworthy features:
• This was a very sudden death of an elderly person in good health. It was discovered shortly
after a visit from <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> during which he was alone with her.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed or implied that <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> must have left the door unlocked after letting
him out. This is a feature of other cases where <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> has been unable to leave the
security system in the condition in which relatives would have expected.
• The medical records had been falsified to show that <PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> was unwell just before her
death and that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had suspected her of drug abuse. Even so, they still showed that
<PERSON>Mrs Grundy</PERSON> had been in good general health and had no potentially fatal conditions or
increased risk factors.
• The cause of death, ‘ old age’, was quite inappropriate for a person who had been in such
good health.
APPENDIX E 259
<PERSON>Pamela Marguerite Hillier
Introduction
Mrs Pamela Marguerite Hillier</PERSON> died on 9th February 1998 at the age of 68. She was a widow
and lived alone. She lived a busy, physically active life. She kept a dog, which she walked
regularly. She took medication for hypertension.
The Circumstances of the Death
About a week before her death, <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> fell and hurt her knee. The injury was not such as to
interfere with her usual activities but, on Sunday 8th February, she found her knee was painful
while driving the car. Early the next day, she telephoned <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery and asked him to
visit. The receptionist’s note of the conversation mentioned the painful knee and nothing else.
When <PERSON>Mrs Jacqueline Gee</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON>’s daughter, left shortly before lunch, after spending the
