two women spoke by telephone at 1.07pm. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must have visited shortly afterwards. At
about 2pm, <PERSON>Mrs Gee</PERSON> telephoned her mother but there was no reply. Later in the afternoon,
<PERSON>Mrs Gee</PERSON> made several more attempts to speak to her mother and became worried when there
was still no reply. She contacted Mr <PERSON>Peter Ellwood</PERSON>, a neighbour, who went in and found
<PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON>. She was flat on her back on the bedroom floor. He arranged for an ambulance to be
called and gave mouth-to-mouth resuscitation but to no avail. When the paramedics arrived,
they said that <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> was dead. Mrs Gee arrived soon afterwards. She found the papers
relating to her mother’s accounts on the table and her lunch in the microwave oven.
Later, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> attended. One of the paramedics said that he would have to inform the police,
as this was a sudden death at home. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that would not be necessary. <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON>
had had a stroke. Mr Gee asked if there would be a post-mortem but <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said this would
not be necessary as he knew <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> had had a stroke on account of the way in which she
had been lying. If she had had a heart attack, she would have been holding her chest or
reaching for something. One witness said that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said, ‘ Let’s put it down to a stroke’.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said he had seen Mrs <PERSON>Hillier</PERSON> earlier and had found her blood pressure rather high. He
had told her to take another tablet. He had not increased the dose earlier as she had not
complained. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was variously described as abrupt, unfriendly, detached and
unsympathetic.
The Day after Death
The following day Mrs Gee and her brother, Mr <PERSON>Keith Hillier</PERSON>, spoke to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> about the death.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> had died because of her high blood pressure. Mr <PERSON>Hillier</PERSON> asked
why, if the blood pressure had been too high, it had not been possible to increase the
medication. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said it was necessary to have three raised readings before the
medication could be increased. Mr <PERSON>Hillier</PERSON> again raised the question of a post-mortem. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
said it was not necessary. He was sure of the cause of death. It would be an unpleasant thing
to do, to put <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> through a post-mortem. The death had been instantaneous and
painless. He could tell from her position that she had not suffered. If she had been in pain she
would have doubled up forwards. 
260 APPENDIX E
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry
Certification
Shipman</ORGANIZATION> signed a Medical Certificate of Cause of Death (MCCD), giving cerebrovascular
accident of a few minutes’ duration as the immediate cause of death. Hypertension of six years’
duration was the underlying cause. As <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> was to be cremated, he completed Form B.
He stated that the death had occurred at about 2pm, about 30 minutes after he had seen
Mrs Hillier. He said he had attended her for 24 hours during her last illness. He said that no one
had been present at the death. She had been found by a neighbour, collapsed and dead. He
said that the mode of death had been syncope of only seconds’ duration. This must have been
an assumption on his part.
Dr <PERSON>Richard Fitton</PERSON> signed Form C. He said that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told him about the case. He carried out
an external examination but did not expect to see any external signs. He was satisfied that the
cause of death was cerebrovascular accident.
The Medical Records
The computerised medical records contained a number of backdated entries, made after the
death, designed to give the appearance of a developing problem with high blood pressure and
some concern over whether <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> was taking her medication as prescribed. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
invented a consultation on 5th February (which Mrs Gee said did not take place) at which
<PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> is said to have complained of osteoarthritis, worse in the left knee than the right, and
her blood pressure was found to be raised at 150/100. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> noted that the patient said she
was off colour and one leg was weak. He purported to wonder whether this was due to the
knee. He noted that there was no sign of a cerebrovascular accident but the blood pressure
was definitely raised. He had had a chat with the patient about her diet and exercise. He
wondered whether to increase her tablets, presumably meaning the blood pressure tablets.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> also created a false entry for the day of the death. For the visit to her home when she
was alive, he noted that she complained of malaise. Her blood pressure was high at 170/106.
He advised her to increase her medication. She was ‘ to let us know’ – about what is not clear.
She was to come to the surgery on the Friday. The second entry for that day records that she
had been found by a neighbour and an ambulance had been called. She was dead. He stated
the time as 14.00 hours. She had collapsed on the bedroom floor and it looked like a
cerebrovascular accident.
The Defence Case
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account in evidence, which the jury must have rejected, was that he had visited
<PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> at 1.30pm. She told him her knees were no better and that the Co-codamol tablets
which he claimed he had prescribed for her on 5th February were not working. He took her
blood pressure and found it to be 170/80, which was worryingly high. He said he would have to
increase the dosage of her medication. She should take a tablet immediately and then lie down
for a couple of hours. She should see him on Friday. Until then, she should take things quietly.
For the knee, he would refer her to a consultant orthopaedic surgeon. She then told him that
there had been two other occasions (6th January and 5th February) on which Mrs <PERSON>Gillian
Morgan</PERSON>, the surgery nurse, had taken her blood pressure and found it to be high. Nurse
<PERSON>Morgan</PERSON> had told <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> to tell <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> when she saw him but she had not done so as, on
APPENDIX E 261
the first occasion, she had missed taking a couple of her tablets and the second time she
thought the rise was a temporary matter associated with the anniversary of her husband’s
death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> asked <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> if she had other symptoms of raised blood pressure and she
admitted that she felt tired and her injured leg felt weak. On his return to the surgery after the
visit of 9th February, he had entered the information she had given him at the dates on which
she had said the readings were taken. He said this was not done with an intention to deceive,
although the entries were plainly misleading. He said he had intended to ask Nurse <PERSON>Morgan</PERSON>
about the raised blood pressure readings but had not done so. He was critical of her for not
having told him of the readings. However, none of these matters were put to <ORGANIZATION>Nurse Morgan</ORGANIZATION>
when she gave evidence. This tale was of recent invention.
Comment
The jury convicted although there was no direct evidence of morphine poisoning. The case
contains a number of features typical of a Shipman killing:
• The deceased was in apparently good health and death was very sudden and wholly
unexpected.
• The death was discovered shortly after a visit during which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was alone with the
patient. No one saw <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> alive or spoke to her after <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s visit.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> deterred the family from seeking a post-mortem examination (by applying some
emotional pressure). He also dissuaded the paramedics from making a report to the police.
• He falsified the records to lend credibility to his claimed cause of death. This is one of the
cases where the audit trail enabled the prosecution to prove that there had been an
elaborate falsification. <PERSON>Mrs Hillier</PERSON> was not suffering from any potentially fatal condition and
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> found it necessary to create evidence that she was.
262 APPENDIX E
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry
Jean Lilley
Introduction
Mrs</ORGANIZATION> <PERSON>Jean Lilley</PERSON> died on 25th April 1997 at the age of 58. She was a married woman. At the time
of her death, she was not in good health. She suffered from heart disease, hypertension and
respiratory problems. She used a wheelchair when out of the house. These illnesses might well
have accounted for her sudden death. However, following exhumation of her body, morphine
was found in the tissues and at the trial it was accepted that she had died as the result of
morphine toxicity. The jury must have found that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had injected her with morphine or
diamorphine.
The Circumstances of the Death
Mr <PERSON>Albert Lilley</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON>’s husband, worked as a long-distance driver. He carried a mobile
telephone with him, which was always switched on. For about two days before her death,
<PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> had had a cold and cough. At about 11am on the day of his wife’s death, <PERSON>Mr Lilley</PERSON>
telephoned his wife from his vehicle to see how she was. She told him she had telephoned to
ask the doctor to visit on account of her cold. She was awaiting his visit.
Mrs <PERSON>Elizabeth Hunter</PERSON>, a neighbour, visited <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> for a chat and a cup of tea in the middle of
the morning. <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> said she was not feeling well and was waiting for the doctor to visit.
From her own home, <PERSON>Mrs Hunter</PERSON> saw <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrive at about noon. She was about to go round
45 minutes later, when she saw <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> leave. Shortly afterwards, she went into <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON>’s
flat and found her on the sofa, apparently asleep. She felt her hand and found it cold. She ran
out to try to stop <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> but he had just gone. She went back inside and tried to resuscitate
<PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> but there was no response and she noticed that her lips were blue. <PERSON>Mrs Hunter</PERSON>
telephoned the surgery and was advised to call an ambulance, which she did. This was at
1.19pm. The paramedics arrived at 1.29pm and pronounced <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> dead. They removed
her body into the bedroom. Then <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived. He was quite brusque with <PERSON>Mrs Hunter</PERSON>, who
was crying. He told her that <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> had had a bad heart and her death had been expected.
He said that, when he had visited earlier, he had tried to persuade her to go to hospital but she
had refused. He did not examine the body or even go into the bedroom. He told the
paramedics that he would sign a Medical Certificate of Cause of Death (MCCD). He told them
that <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> had had a long medical history and her death was not unexpected.
Early in the afternoon, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> spoke to Mr <PERSON>Lilley</PERSON> and told him that he had tried to persuade
<PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> to go to hospital but she would not agree to do so. Mr <PERSON>Lilley</PERSON> found that hard to
believe, as his wife respected <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> and had previously accepted his advice on such
matters without question. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he had been waiting until Mr <PERSON>Lilley</PERSON> came home and
could persuade her to go to hospital but it was now ‘ too late’. Her heart had failed. Mr <PERSON>Lilley</PERSON>
wondered why his wife had not telephoned him if she had wanted to discuss admission to
hospital.
The <ORGANIZATION>Defence Case
Shipman</ORGANIZATION>’s account, which the jury must have rejected, was that he had called upon <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON>
at about 1pm at her request. He found her dressed. She said she was not breathing well and
had pains in the chest and was producing phlegm. He checked her pulse, took her blood
APPENDIX E 263
pressure and listened to her chest where he heard fine crackles, which convinced him that
<PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> should be admitted to hospital. When told of this opinion, <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> was reluctant to
agree and asked him to give her an antibiotic. He tried to persuade her to go to hospital and
suggested that she should telephone her husband or a member of her family. He then left,
saying that if, when she had spoken to her husband, she would agree to be admitted, he would
come back and arrange it. Otherwise he would come back that evening. He was with her for
about 20 to 25 minutes. About 20 minutes after leaving, he was paged by the surgery and was
told that <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> had collapsed. He returned to the flat and found the paramedics in
attendance. <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> was on the bed. He did not examine her as the paramedics had done
so. He made appropriate arrangements to tell members of the family of the death. The following
day, he asked Mr <PERSON>Lilley</PERSON> if he wanted a post-mortem examination but Mr <PERSON>Lilley</PERSON> did not think it
necessary.
Certification
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> completed the MCCD, saying that <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> had died of heart failure due to
ischaemic heart disease and hypertension. He stated she had also suffered from fibrosing
alveolitis and hypercholesterolaemia.
The Expert Evidence
Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON> examined the medical records and accepted that past entries showed that
<PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> had chronic ill health with angina due to narrowing of the arteries and apparent
alveolitis. The cholesterol levels were raised. Hypertension had been treated. The entry for the
day of the death, made by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> after the death, created an impression of a very poor state
of health, entirely consistent with heart failure. There were references to basal crepitations and
a rapid irregular heartbeat. The liver was said to be enlarged, a sign of heart failure. The
Crown’s case was that this entry had been fabricated to give credibility to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account
of the death.
Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> made two points. First, if <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account were true, he had found <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> in
urgent need of medical treatment. She should have been admitted to hospital as an
emergency. Had she refused, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> should have contacted relatives who could have tried
to persuade her. On no account should he have left her. Second, if <PERSON>Mrs Hunter</PERSON>’s evidence was
right, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> left <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> when she was either in extremis or just dead. The cyanosis
observed by <PERSON>Mrs Hunter</PERSON> would have taken two to three minutes to develop after the heart had
stopped. Mrs <PERSON>Lilley</PERSON> must at least have been in cardiac arrest when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> left.
After exhumation, a post-mortem examination was performed and samples taken for scientific
examination. Morphine was found in the tissues at a level consistent with the administration of a
fatal dose. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> could offer no explanation for its presence.
Dr <PERSON>John Rutherford</PERSON>, the pathologist, said that <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> had only mild to moderate
atherosclerosis. There was no sign of alveolitis. There were no blood clots. In short, there was
no clear cause for her sudden death, other than the finding of morphine. He pointed out that to
give morphine to someone suffering from a chest infection, with impaired respiratory function,
would be disastrous. Even a therapeutic dose might kill.
264 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
Comment
The following significant points arise from this case:
• Because <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> was in poor health, her sudden death would readily have been passed
off as due to heart failure, had it not been for the finding of morphine in the body tissues.
When <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had killed a patient, he usually attributed the death to a cause for which
some foundation could be found in the medical records.
• This was a sudden death which occurred in the patient’s home, either while <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was
present or very shortly indeed after he had been with her. If, as <PERSON>Mrs Hunter</PERSON> said, the body
was cold by the time she found it, then <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had been present at the death. For a
patient to die in the presence of his or her general practitioner during a home visit is a very
rare event in the experience of most doctors.
• Mrs <PERSON>Lilley</PERSON> was found sitting in a chair as though asleep. This appearance is typical of many
of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims who have been killed by diamorphine injection. This appearance would
not be typical of a death from heart failure.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s explanation of events was not credible. It would have been most unlikely that
<PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> would have rejected his advice to go into hospital. She would have contacted her
husband on his mobile telephone to discuss the situation.
<PERSON>• Shipman</PERSON>’s account of his actions fell well below acceptable standards of medical practice.
He claimed that <PERSON>Mrs Lilley</PERSON> rejected his advice to be admitted to hospital. If she were so ill as
to require admission as an emergency, he should not have left her without making some
efforts to find a relative who might persuade her to be sensible or without making some
arrangements for her care.
APPENDIX E 265
Ivy Lomas
Introduction
Mrs Ivy <PERSON>Lomas</PERSON> died on 29th May 1997 at the age of 63. The death occurred in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
surgery. He certified the death as being due to coronary thrombosis. Post-mortem examination,
after exhumation of the body, showed a level of morphine in the tissues consistent with the
administration of a fatal dose.
Personal Background
<PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> was not in good health just before her death. Her medical records show that she
suffered from chronic obstructive airways disease and quite frequent upper and lower
respiratory tract infections. She had been a heavy smoker and smoked 40 cigarettes per day.
She could not walk far or lift anything heavy. She also suffered from depression and anxiety
and was a very frequent visitor at <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery. Her adult son suffered a psychiatric
illness and this caused <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> a great deal of worry. On account of her restricted tolerance
of exercise, one of her neighbours took her dog for a walk each day.
The Circumstances of the Death
Two neighbours saw <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> on the day of her death. To one, she mentioned that she had
pains in her chest and arms. However, she was able to go out in the morning to make
arrangements for the care of her son, who was ill. In the afternoon, she took a bus into <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> in
order to attend a 4pm appointment with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. She arrived early and was seen to be looking
a little pale. She walked into the consulting room unaided.
After a very short time, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> took <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> from his consulting room to the treatment
room. After about ten minutes, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> came to the reception area. According to the
receptionist, he looked flushed and apologised for keeping his patients waiting. He said he had
had a problem with the electrocardiograph (ECG) machine. He dealt with two or three more
patients in his consulting room before returning to the treatment room. He then called the
receptionist, <PERSON>Mrs Carol Chapman</PERSON>, into the treatment room and told her that he had tried to take
an ECG reading on <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> but could not get a trace. He said that he had first thought the
machine was broken but then realised that <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> had died. He said he had tried to
resuscitate her but could not do so. He told <PERSON>Mrs Chapman</PERSON> to contact <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON>’ son, <PERSON>Jack</PERSON>. He
saw some more patients. <PERSON>Mrs Chapman</PERSON> could not contact Mr <PERSON>Lomas</PERSON>, so she rang the police.
When PC <PERSON>Reade</PERSON> attended, he was shown the fully clothed body of <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> in the treatment
room. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told him that he would be able to certify death as due to natural causes.
PC <PERSON>Reade</PERSON> was curious (although apparently not suspicious) and asked <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> what had
happened. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> had come in to consult him about bronchial
problems. After treating her for that, he had shown her into the treatment room to rest. He had
carried on seeing other patients. About 15 minutes later, he had gone back to <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> and
had found her dead. He did not mention an ECG to PC Reade. He said he had not tried
resuscitation because <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> had been quite beyond that. Nor had he called an
ambulance. PC Reade claims that he was amazed. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> made a joke in very bad taste
about <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> having been so frequent a visitor to the surgery that he had thought of having
a seat reserved for her, with a plaque.
266 APPENDIX E
The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry
Mrs Lomas</ORGANIZATION>’ daughter, <PERSON>Mrs Carol Dalpiaz</PERSON>, saw <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> that evening. He told her that
<PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> had come into the surgery looking unwell. He said he had taken her to the
treatment room, as he had to see another patient in his consulting room. He did not say that he
had wanted to do an ECG. He said that, when he had seen the other patient, he went back to
see <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> and found her dead. She had gone blue round the mouth. She had had a
massive heart attack and had died. He had tried to revive her but failed. He said nothing about
a post-mortem examination.
The Defence Case
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account in evidence, which the jury must have rejected, was that, when she arrived
for the consultation, <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> said she had had chest pain for four hours that day. She
looked sweaty and had an irregular pulse. He thought she might be having a coronary
thrombosis. He decided to do an ECG so he took her to the treatment room. As she was getting
onto the bed, she collapsed. He turned her onto her back and saw that she was unconscious.
He tried resuscitation. He hit her hard twice on the chest. He put a tube down her throat to keep
the tongue out of the way. He did external cardiac massage and mouth-to-mouth resuscitation.
She did not respond. He checked her eyes and heart and found she was dead. He had not
called for assistance, as he was skilled in first aid. He had not thought it appropriate to call for
an ambulance with paramedics. He then went to see <PERSON>Mrs Chapman</PERSON> and told her he had a
problem with the ECG machine and would see the next patient. He did not tell her <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON>
was dead, as that would have meant taking her off her duties. So he saw three patients, then
told <PERSON>Mrs Chapman</PERSON> of the death and asked her to contact Mr <PERSON>Lomas</PERSON>. He had certified the death
as due to a coronary thrombosis.
The Medical Records
The medical records were voluminous but contained only one reference to heart disease. This
was on the handwritten summary card; the last entry, dated 1991, said ‘ IHD’, which means
ischaemic heart disease. The computerised records contained no reference to heart disease of
any kind and <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> was not receiving any medication for a cardiac condition. The Crown
suggested that the reference to ischaemic heart disease in the summary card was false and
had been made in order to lend plausibility to <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON>’ death supposedly from a heart
attack.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s record of the final consultation said that <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> had been suffering from pain in
the centre of the chest radiating to the arms for four to five hours that day. It was now
continuous and she felt sick and dizzy. Blood pressure was recorded as 100/70, which is low.
The pulse was said to be 64 beats per minute but irregular. She was said to look grey. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
claimed to have diagnosed a coronary thrombosis on the basis of these clinical signs. The
record continues:
‘ 1445 Died family informed’.
The causes of death are set out.
APPENDIX E 267
The Expert Evidence
Dr <PERSON>John Rutherford</PERSON>, the pathologist, conducted the post-mortem examination and found some
signs of emphysema. There was also a moderate degree of atherosclerosis, sufficient to give
rise to a coronary thrombosis. So, in fact, <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> was suffering from heart disease.
However, it appears that, until that day, she had not complained of any symptoms relating to
her heart. Dr <PERSON>Rutherford</PERSON> also took samples for scientific analysis and, in the light of the amount
of morphine found in the tissues, he expressed the opinion that death was due to morphine
poisoning. Had there not been so much morphine in the body tissues, he would have been
prepared to say that the death was due to coronary thrombosis.
Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON> expressed the view that, if <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s claim (that <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> collapsed as she
was mounting the bed) was true, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> should have reacted very promptly by summoning
help with resuscitation and calling an ambulance. On no account should he have left
<PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> alone.
Comment
This was a clear case of murder, as the jury found, but it was clear only because of the
morphine level found in the body tissues. Were it not for that evidence, this death might well
have been passed off as natural, even at a post-mortem examination. The noteworthy features
are:
• This was a sudden death behind closed doors in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery. This is a very rare
event in the experience of other doctors. Such an event gives rise to a high degree of
suspicion. If a patient collapses at a surgery, the doctor should call for assistance and ask
for an ambulance to be summoned. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not call an ambulance or request the
assistance of the staff in his claimed attempt to resuscitate <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON>. In fact, there is little
doubt that no such attempt was made.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> chose an explanation for the death which was plausible in that <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> had
been complaining of chest pain and pain in the arm on the day of her death. It rather looks
as though <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> realised that this was pain of cardiac origin and took the opportunity to
kill <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON>.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave inconsistent accounts of events to others. To <PERSON>Mrs Dalpiaz</PERSON>, he had said he
had tried resuscitation. To PC Reade, he said he had not. To <PERSON>Mrs Chapman</PERSON>, he did not say
that he had. To <PERSON>Mrs Chapman</PERSON>, he said that <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> had died in his presence, as he was
trying to use the ECG machine. To PC Reade and <PERSON>Mrs Dalpiaz</PERSON>, he said <PERSON>Mrs Lomas</PERSON> had died
while he was attending to another patient in another room.
268 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
Joan May Melia
Introduction
Mrs <PERSON>Joan May Melia</PERSON> died on 12th June 1998 at the age of 73. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> certified her death as
being due to lobar pneumonia. Post-mortem examination after exhumation of her body showed
a level of morphine in the tissues consistent with the administration of a lethal dose.
The Circumstances of the Death
<PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> was in good general health in the months before her death. She attended <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
surgery infrequently. On 11th June 1998, she was feeling tired and under the weather. The
following morning, her companion, Mr <PERSON>Derek Steele</PERSON>, took her to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
prescribed an antibiotic. <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> collected her prescription and bought some throat pastilles.
She told Mr <PERSON>Steele</PERSON> that the doctor had said that she had pleurisy and pneumonia. In fact, in her
medical records, he had noted only that she had a chest infection, the nature of which was not
otherwise specified, that air entry was reduced and he could hear rhonchi in the chest.
Mr <PERSON>Steele</PERSON> was surprised at the reference to pleurisy and pneumonia, as <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> did not
seem to be seriously ill. He thought that, if she were suffering from these conditions, she would
have been admitted to hospital. He took her home. She seemed tired, so he suggested that she
should have a rest. He left her and went to his own home nearby. In the late afternoon, he
telephoned her but there was no reply. He went round and found <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> sitting in a chair
with her glasses on and a crossword puzzle on her knee. She was dead and her body already
felt cold. There were signs that she had had something to eat since Mr <PERSON>Steele</PERSON> had left her.
At 5.54pm, Mr <PERSON>Steele</PERSON> sent for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. On arrival, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> looked at <ORGANIZATION>Mrs Melia</ORGANIZATION> and said that
the tablets had not had time to work. He did not touch her. He said he would make out a death
certificate. He was in the house only five minutes. He completed a Medical Certificate of Cause
of Death (MCCD), saying that the cause of death was lobar pneumonia of two to three days’
duration with emphysema as an unrelated contributory condition.
The following day, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON>’s niece that her aunt had been very poorly when he
saw her in the surgery the previous morning. He had told her to go home to bed. There was no
point in taking her to hospital, as she might have died on the way there. He had done what he
thought right at the time. He said nothing about a post-mortem examination.
The Defence Case
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account in evidence, which the jury must have rejected, was that, on the morning of
the day of her death, <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> had complained of a cough with green sputum and of feeling
generally unwell. She had no pleuritic pain. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told her to let him know if she had any
pain and promised her that he would visit her the following day. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> warned her that, if
she were no better, she would have to go to hospital. He advised her to go home, take the
antibiotic and plenty of liquid and keep warm. Mr <PERSON>Steele</PERSON> summoned him that evening. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
examined <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON>’s eyes and pulse but she was clearly dead. She was cold. He thought she
had died of lobar pneumonia. He had diagnosed that condition that morning but did not think it
appropriate to send her to hospital. If all patients who had that condition were to be admitted,
the hospitals would be overrun. Only five per cent of sufferers died of it. It was sensible to send
her home. He had not mentioned a post-mortem examination to Mr <PERSON>Steele</PERSON> because he had not
APPENDIX E 269
realised he was closely connected with Mrs Melia. He had no explanation for the morphine in
her body.
The Expert Evidence
Post-mortem examination by Dr <PERSON>John Rutherford</PERSON>, the pathologist, showed slight emphysema
and moderate narrowing of the arteries. <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> had not had lobar pneumonia. He found
nothing to account for her death. There were food remains in the stomach, showing that
<PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> had eaten a meal not long before death. The scientific analysis of the body tissues
showed the presence of morphine and Dr <PERSON>Rutherford</PERSON> said that the cause of death was
morphine toxicity. This was not challenged by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. Dr <PERSON>Rutherford</PERSON> made the point that a
death from lobar pneumonia can be very rapid but, if this occurs, the patient is extremely ill at
the end. He also said that it would appear that <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> had a chest infection on the day of her
death. The administration of morphine while lung function was impaired would have a
disastrous effect. In other words, even a dose that was normally safe might be fatal.
Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON> said that the medical records showed that <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> had been in good
general health. A chest x-ray taken in February 1998 showed only minor changes. The record
of the morning visit and the prescription of an antibiotic connoted a chest infection of moderate
severity but not one from which the patient might die so quickly. The fact that <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> had
had something to eat after Mr <PERSON>Steele</PERSON> left showed she had not been very ill. This was a sudden
death for which the cause was unknown and the coroner should have been informed.
Comment
Had it not been for the finding of morphine in the body tissues, there would have been very little
evidence of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s involvement in the death:
• First, there is no direct evidence of a visit by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> during the afternoon of 12th June.
No one saw <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrive or leave; no one saw his car outside. There is no record of a
visit in the surgery documents or on the computer. However, the jury must have been
prepared to infer that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had visited <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> and had administered a fatal dose,
almost certainly of diamorphine.
• Second, there are no false, exaggerated or backdated entries in the medical records. The
record of the consultation of the morning of 12th June appears to be a genuine one, properly
reflecting the moderately severe chest infection from which <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> was actually suffering.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> does not appear to have created any false records to lend credibility to the cause
of death he was to certify.
• Third, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not spin any elaborate tale of Mrs Melia having refused treatment or
hospital admission.
• The only suspicious factors (apart from the finding of morphine) were that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
exaggerated the seriousness of her chest infection when speaking to <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> and
certified a cause of death which was incompatible with her ability to walk about and eat a
meal only a short time before death. If <PERSON>Mrs Melia</PERSON> had been cremated, there would have
been no prospect of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> being convicted of her killing.
270 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
Winifred Mellor
Introduction
Mrs <PERSON>Winifred Mellor</PERSON> died on 11th May 1998 at the age of 73. She had lived alone since the
death of her husband in 1989. She had been a patient of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> since 1977 and held him in
very high regard.
Following exhumation of her body in September 1998, scientific analysis showed that she had
morphine in the body tissues consistent with the administration of a fatal dose.
Personal Background
<PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> had been in good health until her death and, although she was a smoker, she did
not smoke heavily. She lived a busy life and was described as being young for her age. She
still played football with her grandchildren. She was active in her church and in the community.
She had recently booked a holiday in the Holy Land. She frequently walked into <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> to shop,
which took ten to fifteen minutes. She developed a cold a few days before her death but did not
appear to her daughter, Miss <PERSON>Sheila Mellor</PERSON>, to be seriously ill on the day before she died, when
the two had lunch together.
The Circumstances of the Death
On the morning of her death, <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> telephoned the school, where she helped the children
with reading, to say that she would not come in that day as she had a cold and a bad chest.
She said she was going to see the doctor. She cannot have been very ill, as she did not
mention any illness to her friends, <PERSON>Mrs Josephine Barnes</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mrs Mary Ball</PERSON>, both of whom
spoke to her on the telephone that morning. Also, she went shopping to <LOCATION>Hyde Market</LOCATION> in the
early afternoon. Her friend, <PERSON>Mrs Margaret Nickson</PERSON>, chatted to her and thought she seemed her
usual cheerful self. It is not clear whether she contacted <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> that day and, if so, how. The
records show that she did not telephone the surgery, nor did she attend for an appointment.
After her death, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told members of the family that he had called on her during the
afternoon at her request and indeed Mrs <PERSON>Gloria Ellis</PERSON>, a neighbour, saw his maroon car outside
her house from about 3pm until about 3.20pm. At the trial, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> denied that he had visited
<PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> that day, until summoned after her death. It must be assumed that the jury was sure
that he had visited her during the day while she was still alive.
At about 6.30pm, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> called at the house of Mr and <PERSON>Mrs Ellis</PERSON> and asked them to let
him into <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON>’s house. He said he could see that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> was not well. When
Mr and <PERSON>Mrs Ellis</PERSON> let <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> into the house, they found <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> sitting in her usual chair.
Her head was to one side and she looked as though she had fallen asleep. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> picked up
her hand, then flicked up her eyes and announced that she was dead. <PERSON>Mrs Ellis</PERSON> was very
distressed and <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was extremely brusque with her.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s first detailed account of events was given to <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON>’s daughter, <PERSON>Mrs Kathleen
Adamski</PERSON>, in a telephone call from <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON>’s home soon after the body was found. He told
her that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> had had heart problems since 1997 but that she had refused treatment.
He said that she had called at the surgery earlier in the day and he arranged to visit her at
3pm. When he arrived, he had found her quite poorly, complaining of heart or chest problems.
She had refused treatment. He did not say why he had left her. He said she had telephoned the
APPENDIX E 271
surgery at about 5.30pm and he had gone to her flat again as soon as he could. He had seen
her through the window but could not gain access so he had gone to a neighbour for a key.
<PERSON>Mrs Adamski</PERSON>, who at this stage had not realised that her mother was dead, asked whether he
was going to send <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> to hospital but he said it was too late. She then realised that her
mother was dead.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> repeated this account later that evening in the presence of all three of <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON>’s
daughters and the priest, <PERSON>Father Dennis Maher</PERSON>, who had called to give the last rites. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
said that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> had telephoned him at the surgery at about 2.30pm complaining of chest
pains. He said he had visited at about 3pm and had offered <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> a spray to put under her
tongue. He also offered to admit her to hospital but she had refused and tried to pass the pain
off as indigestion. He then said that she had been suffering from angina since about August
1997. On several occasions, she had refused treatment and did not want to go to hospital. He
said that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> had telephoned the surgery again at about 5.30pm to say that her pain
was increasing. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had promised to come up as soon as he could. When he arrived, he
could see through the window that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> was sitting in her chair. When he knocked, she
did not respond. He went to find a neighbour who could let him in. When he had examined her,
he found that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> was dead. He said that the cause of death was coronary thrombosis
and asked the daughters if they agreed that he should put that on the death certificate. He said
nothing about a post-mortem examination.
The daughters found the account very strange. First, they were unaware that their mother had
angina and found it hard to believe that she had not told them about it. They were a close
family. They also found it hard to believe that their mother would have refused medical
treatment. She had a very high opinion of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. Finally, they thought she would have
telephoned one of them had she felt ill during the day. There was every sign that Mrs Mellor
had been quite well during the day. She was sitting in her usual chair, with a cup of coffee at
her side. Her shopping remained unpacked in the kitchen. It may be that her sleeve was rolled
up and her arm bruised. That is what the family now recalls, but it should be noted that not all
these matters were mentioned to the police when they first took statements.
The three daughters and Mr and <PERSON>Mrs Ellis</PERSON>, the neighbours who gave <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> access to the
house after the death, were shocked at his abrupt and unsympathetic attitude.
Certification
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> signed the <ORGANIZATION>Medical Certificate of Cause of Death</ORGANIZATION> (MCCD), giving coronary thrombosis
as the cause. He stated that he had last seen Mrs Mellor alive on the day of her death.
The Defence Case
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account at trial, which the jury must have rejected, was that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> had called
into the surgery without appointment at about 4pm on the day of her death. She had given him
an account of having suffered chest pain over a period of several months. He advised her that
she should have an electrocardiogram and see a cardiologist but she was reluctant and went
away, saying that she would think about it. She was to ring him at 5.30pm. As she failed to do
so, he went to see her on his way home and found her dead. He claimed to have carried out a
272 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
full examination to establish death. He accepted the evidence that morphine had been found in
the body tissues and could offer no explanation as to how it came to be there.
The Medical Records
By the time of trial, it had been established that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON>’s records had been altered at about
4pm on the day of the death. New, backdated entries had been created to give the impression
that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> had been suffering from chest pain over a period of several months and had
refused the treatment suggested by the doctor. At trial, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that he had made
those backdated entries to reflect what <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> had told him when she called into the
surgery on 11th May. He said they were not intended to mislead (although they clearly did). The
entries contained information which could not have been recalled so long after the date of the
supposed consultation and could not have been honestly made so as to reflect information
provided on the day of the death. Moreover, in interview, before it had been established that
the records had been backdated, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that the entries were contemporaneous
records of Mrs Mellor’s complaints of chest pain.
The medical records also contained two entries made early in the morning of the day after the
death, both of which related to the day of the death. The first described an attack of angina
pectoris with no signs of congestive heart failure. It was said that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> had refused
treatment. There is no reference to a refusal to go to hospital. The second entry deals with
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s visit at which he declared that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> was dead. The cause of death was given
as coronary thrombosis. The record noted that her daughter and a neighbour were present.
Although the record does not explicitly say so, it gives the misleading impression that the
daughter and neighbour were present at the time of death.
If the falsified records are taken out of account, there is nothing in <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON>’s medical history
to suggest that she was suffering from any serious or potentially fatal condition.
The Post-Mortem Examination
Post-mortem examination revealed no natural cause of death. There was no significant
abnormality of the heart or blood vessels. There was no intracranial bleeding and no blood
clots were seen in the lung. There was some narrowing of the arteries but not such as would
account for sudden death, save under physical stress.
Comment
This was a clear case of morphine poisoning as the post-mortem results showed. Apart from
that evidence, the case contains a number of features worthy of note:
• This was a sudden death of a woman who lived alone and which was discovered shortly
after a visit from <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>, at a time when she was alone. No one saw <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> alive or
spoke to her after <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had visited.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> altered medical records to create a history of angina, which was supportive of his
diagnosis of the cause of death, namely coronary thrombosis.
APPENDIX E 273
• There are important conflicts between the accounts <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave at the time of the death
and the evidence given at trial. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told the family that he had visited <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> at
home at about 3pm. He told them that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> had been suffering from angina for about
a year. At the trial, he said that <PERSON>Mrs Mellor</PERSON> saw him at the surgery in the afternoon and told
him for the first time about the angina symptoms she had supposedly been suffering. This
was a complete volte-face, rendered necessary by the realisation that the police had been
able to trace and time the false records.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that the patient had refused treatment shortly before death. This is one of
his regular excuses for leaving a supposedly sick patient alone when the truth was that the
patient had not been ill and had not required treatment but had been killed by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. He
would then have an explanation for the death when it was discovered.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was ready to give the impression that a daughter and neighbour had been present
at the time of death, when they had not.
