The <ORGANIZATION>Shipman Inquiry
Norah Nuttall
Introduction</ORGANIZATION>
Mrs <PERSON>Norah Nuttall</PERSON> died on 26th January 1998 at the age of 64. She lived with her son, Mr <PERSON>John
Anthony Nuttall</PERSON>. She was a patient of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> and thought well of him.
Personal Background
According to her son, <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> was in good general health before her death. She was very
independent and often went into <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> to shop and see friends. Her medical records show that
she was obese and suffered from a number of chronic conditions. Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON> said that
there was a history of rheumatic fever, which predisposed her to valvular disease of the heart.
There were some references to ischaemic heart disease. The records mentioned
breathlessness on exertion and oedema of the legs. The most frequently recurring entries
related to nosebleeds. Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> considered that <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> appeared to have quite severe
heart disease and had been appropriately treated, although not well investigated.
The Circumstances of the Death
Shortly before her death, <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> developed a cough. This did not keep her indoors and, on
the morning of the day of her death, she went into <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> to see <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. She met some friends
who later said that she appeared to be her usual self. When Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON> came home from work at
2pm, his mother told him that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had given her some medicine and advised her to stay
indoors for a couple of days. She did not say that she was expecting <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to call. Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON>
was not concerned for his mother’s health. Shortly before 3pm, he went out to attend to his
ponies.
When Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON> returned, about 40 minutes later, he saw <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s car outside. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was
leaving the house. He asked <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> what was wrong. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told him that his mother was
not well and he had rung for an ambulance to take her to hospital. In fact he had not. Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON>
ran into the house and found his mother slumped in a chair, apparently asleep. When he spoke
to her, there was no response. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> came in behind him and said that it looked as though
his mother had taken a turn for the worse. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> touched her neck and said, ‘ She’s gone.’
He looked in her eye. When Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON> asked if anything could be done, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that she
had gone and there was nothing he could do. He pretended to cancel the ambulance.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON> that he had been visiting nearby when he had received a call asking
him to visit <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> and he had come at once. That was not true and, in evidence, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
admitted that he had just decided to call on her. He claimed that <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> had told him that
she had chest pains. He said that the death had been due to the failure of the left ventricle of
the heart. He told Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON> that, in such a case, a patient would become unconscious and die
within about 20 minutes and that, even if the paramedics had come, they would not have been
able to save <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON>. He did not mention a post-mortem examination. He said that the
funeral directors could take the body away.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> telephoned <PERSON>Mrs Oldham</PERSON>, <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON>’s sister, and told her that he had received a call
asking him to visit <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON>. He said that, when he arrived, he had found <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> was
poorly. She had taken a turn for the worse and died. 
APPENDIX E 275
The Day after Death
The following day, when Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mrs Oldham</PERSON> went to the surgery to collect the Medical
Certificate of Cause of Death (MCCD), <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> purported to demonstrate from the records that
<PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> had been suffering from breathlessness and high blood pressure.
The Defence Case
The medical record for the day of death shows that <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> attended the surgery
complaining of wheezy bronchitis and was given a decongestant. She was also seen in her
own home, when she was dead on examination.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account in evidence, which the jury must have rejected, was that, when <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON>
came into the surgery on the morning of 26th January, he diagnosed her as suffering from
wheezy bronchitis. She seemed to be all right. He gave her a bottle of cough medicine. At
some time between 3pm and 4pm he decided to call on her. He gave a variety of reasons for
this visit, such as wishing to see how she was managing in her ‘ new’ house, in which she had
then been living for about eight years. His reasons were not credible. He said that <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON>
had been surprised to see him. As she sat down, she said she was glad he had called, as she
was worse than she had been in the morning. She was very breathless, she felt ill and her
ankles had swelled up. She had not wanted to bother him by telephoning the surgery. He
examined her and found her ankles were swollen and her pulse was ‘ thready’. On listening to
her chest, it was clear she had fluid in the lungs. She was very breathless, cold and slightly
blue round the lips. He made a provisional diagnosis of left ventricular failure. He decided to
give her an injection of Lasix, a rapid-effect diuretic, so he went out to the car to collect it. As he
was going out, <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON>’s son arrived. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> explained that he had just called to see
<PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> and that she was seriously ill. He was fetching a drug from his car and would then
call an ambulance. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> followed Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON> into the house and was preparing the syringe,
when he realised that <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> had stopped breathing. He felt her pulse, looked at her pupils
and listened to her heart and chest. She was dead. He decided not to attempt resuscitation, as
it would not succeed and would be distressing for her son. He explained to Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON> what had
happened. He did not suggest a post-mortem examination, as he felt confident of his diagnosis
of left ventricular failure. When he spoke to <PERSON>Mrs Oldham</PERSON>, he advised that a post-mortem was
not necessary. Later Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON> said he did not want a post-mortem. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had not called an
ambulance and had not said that he had done so.
Certification
On the <ORGANIZATION>MCCD</ORGANIZATION>, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> stated that the cause of death was left ventricular failure of 15 minutes’
duration. This was due to congestive heart failure of three years’ duration. Hypertension for four
years and obesity for 20 years had also contributed. As <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> was to be cremated,
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> completed Form B, stating that he had seen Mrs <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON> immediately before death.
The mode of death was syncope of ten minutes’ duration. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed to have carried out
a full external examination after death. He stated that Mr <PERSON>Nuttall</PERSON> was present at the death.
Dr <PERSON>Jeremy Dirckze</PERSON> signed Form C, confirming the cause of death as left ventricular failure. He
said he would have relied entirely on what <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had told him. He had no recollection of the
276 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
case. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that he had shown Dr <PERSON>Dirckze</PERSON> the medical records but Dr <PERSON>Dirckze</PERSON> did
not confirm this.
The Expert Evidence
Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> said that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s claim to have found <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> in a poorly state did not tally
with her son’s description of her condition only a short time before. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s description of the
death was not typical of ventricular heart failure, in which the patient would be gasping for
breath and usually producing froth at the mouth and nose. If, as <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> suggested,
<PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> appeared simply to have stopped breathing, she should have been resuscitated,
especially as she was only 64.
Comment
This was a cremation case in which there were no remains to be examined. Nonetheless, the
jury convicted <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> of murder. The case bore a number of the hallmarks of a <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
killing:
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had no proper reason for his decision to call upon the patient. He lied in claiming
that the patient had requested a visit. He knew that she was not well because she had
called at the surgery that morning. In describing her condition just before death, he
exaggerated it, claiming that she was seriously ill when she was not.
• The death was extremely sudden and quite unexpected. It occurred while the patient was
alone with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. That is an extremely rare event in the experience of most general
practitioners.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> chose a cause of death which was plausible, in that <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> had been suffering
from heart disease for some time.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s description of the manner of death was not typical of a death from the cause he
diagnosed. Nor was the appearance of the body (sitting in a chair as if asleep) such as
would have been expected after a death from heart failure. It was what would be expected
from a death from diamorphine.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not attempt resuscitation in circumstances in which that would have been the
appropriate course if <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> really had gone into acute heart failure.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> lied when he claimed to have rung for an ambulance and pretended to cancel it.
This is a feature that occurs in other cases.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s examination of the body was perfunctory. It was not sufficient to have diagnosed
the fact of death. It was as if he already knew that <PERSON>Mrs Nuttall</PERSON> was dead.
APPENDIX E 277
<PERSON>Bianka Pomfret</PERSON>
Introduction
Mrs <PERSON>Bianka Pomfret</PERSON> died on 10th December 1997 at the age of 49. At post-mortem examination
after exhumation of her body in 1998, the morphine levels in her tissues were consistent with
the administration of a fatal dose.
The Circumstances of the Death
Mrs <LOCATION>Pomfret</LOCATION> was a divorcee and suffered from a depressive illness. Those who saw her in the
few days before her death were aware that she was chesty and was expecting a visit from the
doctor. During the morning of 10th December 1997, her neighbour, Mr <PERSON>Paul Graham</PERSON>, saw her
looking out of her lounge window. At about 5pm that day, <PERSON>Mrs Susan Adshead</PERSON>, a community
mental health support worker, called to visit <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON> but there was no reply to her knock.
On looking through the window, she could see <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON> on the sofa. When <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON>’s
son, <PERSON>William</PERSON>, was brought, he found his mother was dead. She was fully clothed and looked
relaxed. A half-drunk cup of coffee and a burned out cigarette were on the table beside her.
Mr <LOCATION>Pomfret</LOCATION> called an ambulance. The paramedics summoned <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>.
Mr <PERSON>Pomfret</PERSON> said that, when <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived, he said that he had visited <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON> at about
12.30pm that day, because she had telephoned to say that she was unwell. She had told him
she was suffering from chest pains. He did not mention any treatment. He said he had told her
to make an appointment to see him again if need be. He now thought she had had a heart
attack later in the day. He told <PERSON>Mr Pomfret</PERSON> and his wife that <PERSON>Mrs Bianka Pomfret</PERSON> had been
suffering from angina for about ten months. They were very surprised at that. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that
<PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON>’s depression, medication and smoking habit had all played a part in her death.
The <ORGANIZATION>Defence Case
Shipman</ORGANIZATION>’s account, which the jury must have rejected, was that <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON> had requested a
visit on 10th December. On his arrival, she told him that she had had chest pain on several
occasions and gave him details of three such episodes which had occurred in the past. It
sounded to him as though she had angina so he offered her glycerine trinitrate to see if it
afforded her any relief. She refused it, saying that she already took too many tablets. He told
her that she ought to be referred to a cardiologist but she refused. He advised her to make an
appointment for an electrocardiogram (ECG) and left. She came to the door to wave him off.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> went back to the surgery and made several entries in the records. One was for that
day’s visit but others were backdated entries. He said that his intention was to reflect the history
of the previous episodes of chest pain about which <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON> had told him. He said that he
had no intention to create a misleading impression. In fact, he made three misleading entries in
the records spread over a period of nine months. These were false and were designed to lend
credence to his contention that <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON> had been suffering from heart disease over that
period. Some entries contained blood pressure readings, which he could not possibly have
recalled so long after the event. Also, some contained comments (such as ‘ seems better’)
which were designed to give the flavour of contemporaneity. By the time he came to give
evidence, it had been discovered that these entries had been backdated.
278 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
In evidence, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that, when summoned to the house later that day, he had seen the
body and an ECG trace taken by the paramedics, which showed that <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON> was dead.
He thought she must have had a coronary thrombosis. He offered the family a post-mortem
examination but they refused.
The Aftermath
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> completed the Medical Certificate of Cause of Death (MCCD), stating that death was
due to coronary thrombosis with ischaemic heart disease as an underlying cause. He said that
smoking and depressive illness had contributed to the death but were not related to the
immediate cause of death.
The following day, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> spoke to Dr <PERSON>Tait</PERSON>, the consultant psychiatrist responsible for
Mrs <LOCATION>Pomfret</LOCATION>’s care. Dr <PERSON>Alan Tait</PERSON> made a written note of the conversation at the time. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
told him that he had seen Mrs <LOCATION>Pomfret</LOCATION> on 8th December and that she had complained of chest
pains which could have been angina. He arranged an ECG but the results were not significant.
He had seen her the next day and she had been cheerful. On 10th December, he had found
her collapsed with a ‘ thready’ pulse and she had proceeded to ‘ asystole’. She had been
resuscitated and defibrillated but had died. He said that this was a natural death and there was
no reason for a post-mortem examination. In effect, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave Dr <PERSON>Tait</PERSON> a completely different
account of events from that which he had given to Mr <LOCATION>Pomfret</LOCATION> and which he later gave to
the jury.
The Expert Evidence
At post-mortem examination, Dr <PERSON>Rutherford</PERSON> found no natural cause of death. There was no
significant narrowing of the arteries, nor any heart disease nor any sign of intracranial bleeding.
In evidence, he said that the morphine found in the body tissues must have been administered
while <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON> was alive. In his opinion, the cause of death was morphine poisoning and
this was not challenged.
Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON> said that, when the false backdated records were taken out of account, there
was nothing in the records to explain <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON>’s sudden death. This death should have
been reported to the coroner.
Comment
The death is a clear case of morphine poisoning but, even if the evidence of morphine in the
body is taken out of account, there remain a number of significant factors:
• This was a sudden death, which was discovered shortly after a visit from <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> at which
he saw the patient alone. No one saw or spoke to Mrs <LOCATION>Pomfret</LOCATION> after <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s visit.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told the family that <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON> had refused treatment that he had advised.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> often said this of patients whom he had killed.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave a quite different account to Dr <PERSON>Tait</PERSON> from that which he had given to the family.
APPENDIX E 279
• Mrs <LOCATION>Pomfret</LOCATION>’s position and peaceful appearance in death were not typical of those seen in a
patient who has suffered a heart attack. They were entirely typical of those seen in many of
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims who had been killed by diamorphine injection.
• When the falsified entries were excluded, the medical records did not support <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s
stated opinion of the medical cause of death.
• The family says that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not offer a post-mortem but <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he had
offered the family a post-mortem but they had refused it. This particular conflict of evidence
is found again and again in cases where <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> is suspected of killing a patient. In any
event, it was not for <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> to offer the family a post-mortem examination. It was his duty
to decline to sign the MCCD if he was not sufficiently confident that he knew the cause of
death. On his story, he could not have been sufficiently sure that <PERSON>Mrs Pomfret</PERSON> had had a
heart attack and the death should have been reported to the coroner.
280 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
<PERSON>Marie Quinn</PERSON>
Introduction
Mrs <PERSON>Marie Quinn</PERSON> died on 24th November 1997 at the age of 67. Following exhumation of her
body in September 1998, it was found that she had morphine in the tissues consistent with the
administration of a lethal dose.
The Circumstances of the Death
<PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON> was divorced and lived alone. Her only son, Mr <PERSON>John Quinn</PERSON>, worked in <LOCATION>Japan</LOCATION>; he
kept in frequent contact with her. On the morning of the day of her death, <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON> attended a
funeral in <LOCATION>Hyde</LOCATION>. She appeared to friends to be in normal health. At about 2.30pm, she
telephoned her son in <LOCATION>Japan</LOCATION> for a chat. She said nothing that caused him to be concerned for
her health. A close friend, <PERSON>Mrs Ellen Hanratty</PERSON>, who also saw <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON> regularly, said she
seemed in normal health.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account of the events of the day was that <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON> telephoned the surgery at
about 5.45pm, asking for a visit as she had noticed some weakness on the left side. Records
showed that no telephone call was made from her home to the surgery that day. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
claimed he had taken it himself, as he happened to be standing in the reception area,
preparing to leave for the day. He claimed that <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON> told him that she had noticed a
weakness down the left side, over the last hour. He told her he would come; she should leave
the door on the latch and he would let himself in. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was lying. She did not telephone him
and it must have been his own idea to visit <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON>.
Nobody saw <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrive, so the time of the visit is not known. He claimed that it had taken
him about 25 minutes to reach the house, due to rush-hour traffic and he had arrived at about
6.15pm. However, that too was a lie as the computerised records of other patients showed that
he was still seeing patients in the surgery at 6.16pm.
In evidence, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that, when he let himself in, he found <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON> on the kitchen
floor, close to death. He examined her and found a slight carotid pulse and some reaction of
the pupils to light. She was not breathing but he said that she responded abnormally to the
<PERSON>Babinski</PERSON> test. That is a test in which the doctor strokes the underside of the foot. Normally the
big toe reacts by going down. If there is cerebral irritation, such as occurs in a stroke, the big
toe will go up. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> considered that <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON> was still alive but deeply unconscious. He
decided not to attempt resuscitation as he thought she had had a severe stroke and would be
irreparably brain damaged if revived. He waited for two minutes. Had she stirred, he would
have called an ambulance but she did not. He then checked and found that she was dead. He
believed she had had a stroke. In fact he must have given her a dose of morphine and she
must have died very quickly as, at 6.32pm, he telephoned <PERSON>Mrs Hanratty</PERSON>, who was named as
the contact on <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON>’s records.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave an account of what had happened to <PERSON>Mrs Hanratty</PERSON>, Mr <PERSON>Quinn</PERSON> and <PERSON>Mrs Cecilia
Adshead</PERSON>, a friend. In essence, he said that <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON> had telephoned the surgery saying that
she seemed to be having a stroke and was paralysed down one side of her body. He told her
he would come and she should leave the door on the latch. When he arrived he had found her
on the floor, breathing her last, and it had been too late to admit her to hospital. He said she
had had a massive stroke. 
APPENDIX E 281
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> signed a Medical Certificate of Cause of Death (MCCD), giving the immediate cause
as cardiovascular accident of 20 minutes’ duration. This was due to arteriosclerosis, which was
said to have been present for five years, and hypertension, said to have been present for three
years.
The <ORGANIZATION>Medical Records
Mrs Quinn</ORGANIZATION>’s medical records showed that she appeared to suffer from systemic sclerosis,
which causes fibrosis of the skin and other tissues. In <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON>, it affected her oesophagus
and the small blood vessels in the fingers and toes. There was a single entry in the summary
card dated 1992, suggesting that she suffered from arteriosclerosis, and another, dated 1994,
for hypertension. However, there was no other reference to arteriosclerosis and the prosecution
suggested that the summary card had been falsified in order to provide some support for
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s diagnosis of the causes of death. The evidence of hypertension was not clear from
the records. The only high blood pressure reading was 170/100 on a day in October 1997.
However, it appears that <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON> had been taking Nifedipine since 1993. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that
this had been prescribed for hypertension, so it may be that her blood pressure had been well
controlled.
There was an entry in the records made on the morning after <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON>’s death, saying:
‘ rang 1745 weak larm leg visit 1815 dying cert 1820 cva arteriosclerosisi
(sic) hypertension and sclerodema’.
The Expert Evidence
Post-mortem examination showed that there were no blood clots in the brain. The vessels in the
brain stem appeared normal. There was a minimal degree of fatty deposit in the carotid
arteries. The cardiovascular and respiratory systems were in good condition. There was no
obvious sclerosis. Dr <PERSON>John Rutherford</PERSON> was able to rule out cerebral haemorrhage, as there was
no blood clot in the brain. He could not positively rule out occlusion of a blood vessel, as this
was difficult to confirm at autopsy. However, an occlusive stroke does not usually cause
sudden death but rather paralysis and loss of function on one side after which the patient might
recover but, if deterioration continues, death might follow within a period or two to three days.
Dr <PERSON>Rutherford</PERSON> expressed the view that the cause of death was morphine toxicity and this
diagnosis was not challenged on <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s behalf at trial. Nor did he advance any explanation
as to how the morphine had come to be in <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON>’s body.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had given the immediate cause of death as cerebrovascular accident. Dr <PERSON>Rutherford</PERSON>
said that neither the pathological findings nor the clinical circumstances fitted that diagnosis.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had given arteriosclerosis and hypertension as underlying causes. Dr <PERSON>Rutherford</PERSON> said
there was no evidence of cardiovascular disease. There could have been some hypertension.
In other words, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s diagnoses of the causes of death could not be justified on the
medical history. 
282 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
Comment
The evidence in this case is overwhelming, as with all the cases where morphine was found in
the body. Even without that evidence the case would have been strong and there are a number
of noteworthy features:
• This was a sudden death, wholly unexpected to those who knew <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON>.
• The death was closely associated in time with <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s presence. He claimed to have
found <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON> on the verge of death. There are a number of other cases where <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
has claimed to have found a patient already dead or dying, when the truth is that they were
alive when he arrived but dead when he left.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that the patient had telephoned, requesting a visit. This was a proven lie
as no call had been made from <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON>’s house to the surgery. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> sometimes said
this to the families of victims as an excuse for a visit, when the truth was that he had made
his own decision to call.
<PERSON>• Shipman</PERSON> also claimed that he had told <PERSON>Mrs Quinn</PERSON> to leave the door on the latch. He
sometimes said this to families of victims when he needed an explanation for his means of
entry, when he was claiming that on arrival he found the patient already dead.
• Despite his attempts at falsification, the cause of death given by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was not borne out
by examination of the records.
APPENDIX E 283
<PERSON>Irene Turner</PERSON>
Introduction
Mrs <PERSON>Irene Turner</PERSON> died on 11th July 1996 at the age of 67. Her body was exhumed in 1998 and
the levels of morphine found in her body tissues were consistent with the administration of a
fatal dose.
Personal Background
For some years before her death, <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> suffered from non-insulin dependent diabetes
and arterial disease. She had had breast cancer and had suffered a heart attack in 1994.
Notwithstanding that, she led a full and active life and appeared to friends and family to be
generally quite well. She had been to <ORGANIZATION>Torquay</ORGANIZATION> for a week’s holiday just before her death.
On her return, she developed a cold and was bringing up phlegm, which had caused her to
vomit. Her daughter and son-in-law, <PERSON>Mrs Carol</PERSON> and Mr <PERSON>Michael Woodruff</PERSON>, advised her to call
the doctor.
The Circumstances of the Death
On 11th July, <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> rang <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery to ask for a visit. She stayed in bed. She
asked Mr <PERSON>Woodruff</PERSON> to bring a meat and potato pie in for her lunch. When he arrived, she said
she would eat it when the doctor had been. At 2.15pm, <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> telephoned Mr <PERSON>Woodruff</PERSON>
and said that the doctor had not yet been. She must have been in the living room at that time to
use the telephone. That was her last communication.
Mrs <PERSON>Sheila Ward</PERSON>, a neighbour, said that, at about 3.25pm, she saw <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> in his car in the
road. He asked her to go into <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON>’s house. He told her to wait for about five minutes
before going in. He gave no reason why she should delay. When she went in, she found
<PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> dead in bed. She was lying on the bed, with her head on the pillows and her arms
outside the bedclothes, which came up to her chest. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived and told her that
<PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> had died of diabetes. It was all through her body and it had been too late to send
her to hospital.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told Mr <PERSON>Woodruff</PERSON> that there was no need for a post-mortem examination. He had told
<PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> that she should go to hospital but she had not liked the idea. He was cold and
matter of fact.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told Mr <PERSON>Alfred Isherwood</PERSON>, another son-in-law, that <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> had not looked after her
diabetes. He said she had died of ischaemic heart disease. He gave a very complicated
explanation of her supposed condition. He said <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> would not have suffered any pain;
she would just have gone to sleep. He was cold and businesslike. He said <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> had not
wished to go to hospital. Mr <PERSON>Isherwood</PERSON> thought that was strange as <ORGANIZATION>Mrs Turner</ORGANIZATION> held <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> in
high regard and would always accept his advice. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> blamed <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> for leaving it
until so late before she called the doctor. He said she would have survived if she had called
him earlier. 
284 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
The <ORGANIZATION>Defence Case
Shipman</ORGANIZATION>’s account, which must have been disbelieved by the jury, was that, when he arrived,
<PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> was very poorly. She told him that she had been vomiting for five to six days and
may have vomited her diabetes tablets. He thought her diabetes was out of control and that
she was dehydrated. He told her she ought to go to hospital but she would not agree. She told
him that, in the past, she had been worse than she then was and she had recovered without
telling him. Eventually, she agreed to go into hospital if her urine showed both blood and
protein, so he took a sample and left. He was confident that the results would show a serious
situation, so he told her to get ready for hospital and suggested that he should ask her
neighbour, <PERSON>Mrs Ward</PERSON>, to come in. He left and saw <PERSON>Mrs Ward</PERSON> outside. He asked her go to
<PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> in a few minutes, by which time <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> would have been to the lavatory. He told
her he would be back shortly. He went to the surgery and did the test, which was positive, so
he went straight back to the house. He was away only ten minutes. He found <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON>
unconscious. He felt for a pulse, examined her eyes and concluded that she was dead. He
considered that the cause of death was failure of the peripheral circulation.
Certification
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> signed the <ORGANIZATION>Medical Certificate of Cause of Death</ORGANIZATION> (MCCD), giving the date of death as
10th July and purporting to sign it on 10th June 1996. He gave the cause as circulatory failure,
with ischaemic heart disease and diabetes mellitus as underlying conditions. Hypertension was
a contributory factor.
The Expert Evidence
The post-mortem examination revealed signs of diabetes, coronary artery disease and past
heart attacks. These conditions were severe enough to account for a sudden death. However,
the morphine levels found in the tissue samples were consistent with a fatal dose and, in
Dr <PERSON>John Rutherford</PERSON>’s view, morphine was the cause of death. From the post-mortem
examination findings and a perusal of the medical records, it appeared to him that both the
diabetes and hypertension were well controlled.
The Medical Records
The medical records revealed a complicated medical history, which it is not necessary to
record. With reference to the death, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had made a handwritten account of his final visit,
although it was mistakenly dated 10th June 1996. This account was plainly designed to lend
plausibility to the cause of death he had given. The note suggested that he had found
<PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> to be very ill. She had been vomiting for four days. She had a fever, dysuria and
was dehydrated. He thought she was in circulatory collapse. There was tenderness over the
kidneys. She was acidotic. He had prescribed amoxycillin, an antibiotic. He had taken a urine
sample and had left. On his return, he had found her dead from the causes which appeared on
the MCCD.
Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON> did not think this note made medical sense. He said that, if the note were
accurate, it showed a woman in urgent need of hospital admission for intravenous rehydration
and control of the diabetes. She was a medical emergency. There should have been no delay.
APPENDIX E 285
It was inappropriate to prescribe amoxycillin or to take away a urine sample for testing. The
doctor should have called an ambulance. If the patient had refused to go to hospital, he should
have warned her of the risk of imminent death and should have summoned a relative who could
persuade her. Failing all else, he should have asked her to confirm her objection in writing.
Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> doubted the feasibility of obtaining a urine sample from <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> if she was as
dehydrated as the note suggested. He observed that the urine test was not recorded in the
notes. Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> doubted that <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> was as ill as <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed. She would have
looked very ill and would not have been interested in food. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s case did not add up.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that the handwritten record was made while he was at <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON>’s house.
The computerised records, which <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed he had made on his return to the surgery
that evening or the next morning, were different in many respects from the written record. For
11th July, there are several separate entries which say:
‘ Had a chat to patient
very ill not happy re hosp’.
‘ Had a chat to patient
circulatory failure bs 20 (blood sugar 20, which is very high. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed
that <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> had taken this reading herself on her glucometer)’.
‘ Examination of patient
100/60 cold? uti tluk (to let us know)’.
‘ O/E – dead
1540 circ fail dm ihd//hyperte (on examination dead; circulatory failure,
diabetes mellitus ischaemic heart disease//hyptertension)’.
‘ Seen in own home
Dr H.F.Shipman’.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was quite unable to explain the many inconsistencies between the handwritten and
computerised records. It was not possible to establish from objective evidence when either
record was made.
Comment
This was a proven case of morphine poisoning. However, if one takes that factor out of the
equation, there remain several factors which are common to other cases:
• This was a very sudden death of a patient who did not appear to others to be seriously ill on
the day of death. However, she had underlying conditions which could have resulted in her
sudden death at any time. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s victims often had potentially fatal conditions, which he
relied on as a plausible cause of death.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed that he found <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> to be seriously ill and that she refused admission
to hospital. This is a common feature in many <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killings. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> left her alone,
supposedly in a serious condition. She was found dead very shortly afterwards. It would
286 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
have been a remarkable coincidence if she had died naturally during the few intervening
minutes.
<PERSON>• Shipman</PERSON>’s account of his treatment of <PERSON>Mrs Turner</PERSON> was inappropriate. In order to explain the
course of events, he was driven to give an account of his actions which, if true, would have
shown him to be seriously incompetent. This is a common feature of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> killings.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s two sets of records of the events preceding the death were woefully inadequate
and were mutually inconsistent. It was accepted that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was a poor record keeper
and this is amply demonstrated in very many cases.
• The body gave the appearance of having been ‘ laid out’. So neat and tidy an appearance is
not typical of an unattended death from natural causes. Other examples of this feature have
emerged from the Inquiry’s investigations.
APPENDIX E 287
<PERSON>Laura Kathleen Wagstaff</PERSON>
Introduction
Mrs <PERSON>Laura Kathleen Wagstaff</PERSON> died on 9th December 1997, the day before <PERSON>Mrs Bianka Pomfret</PERSON>.
She was aged 81. She was a patient of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> and thought highly of him.
The Circumstances of the Death
<PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON> was in good general health at the time of her death. On the day on which she
died, she went shopping in <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> at about lunchtime. At about 1.45pm, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> left the
surgery without saying where he was going. A neighbour saw <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON> letting him into her
flat, some time between 2pm and 3pm. She seemed pleased and surprised to see him. Another
neighbour saw him arrive and saw him again in the car park after about 20 to 30 minutes.
In the mid-afternoon, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> went to a neighbour and told him that <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON> had died.
He said he would inform the relatives and left. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> erroneously informed <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON>’s
daughter-in-law that her mother had died. He discovered his mistake when he spoke to
Mrs <PERSON>Carol Chapman</PERSON>, a receptionist at the surgery. Then, <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON>’s son, <PERSON>Peter</PERSON>, was
informed and went to the house. He found his mother slumped in her chair. She had been
slightly sick.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told <PERSON>Mr Wagstaff</PERSON> that his mother had telephoned the surgery, asking for a visit. That
was a lie. No call had been made from <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON>’s house to the surgery. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed
that the surgery staff had paged him and, as he was close by, he had visited her. On arrival he
had found <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON> looking ill, very grey, sweating and blue round the mouth. He had
helped her upstairs and settled her in her chair. He said that he had found her pulse was
‘ thready’ so he telephoned for an ambulance. That was a proven lie. He said he went to fetch
his bag from the car and when he returned he found that <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON> had slumped over and
died. He had checked her pulse. He had cancelled the ambulance. That was also a proven lie.
He said nothing about an attempt at resuscitation. He said she had had a heart attack and had
not suffered. She had had heart disease of a kind that could ‘ carry you off’ quite quickly.
Mr <PERSON>Wagstaff</PERSON> was surprised, as he was unaware that his mother had heart problems. In fact she
had not.
The Defence Case
In evidence, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account, which the jury rejected, was that he had been in the reception
area of the surgery at about 3pm when he had taken a call from <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON>. That was a lie.
He claimed that she said she had chest pain and did not feel well. She had not had it before.
She needed him to visit. He took out her notes and went straight there. She let him in and went
slowly upstairs to her flat where she sat in her chair in the corner. He had his bag and notes
with him. She looked grey and cyanosed. Her pulse was fast, over 100 beats per minute. The
blood pressure and heart sounds were normal. There was no sign of a chest infection. He
listened to her heart and thought she might be having a coronary thrombosis. He told her she
ought to be in hospital to confirm the diagnosis. He said he would write up the notes and then
ring for an ambulance. He claimed to have written up the notes but subsequent examination of
the notes revealed no entry in them. He said that he had then realised that her mouth was
open. She had died. He attempted resuscitation but she was too big to move. He tried mouth-
288 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
to-mouth resuscitation and external cardiac massage while she was still in the chair. He did not
call for help from the paramedics as he was doing everything that could be done for her. After
ten minutes, he knew he could not revive her so he set about informing the relatives of the
death.
Certification
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> completed the Medical Certificate of Cause of Death (MCCD) saying that the cause of
death was coronary thrombosis, which had begun 30 minutes before death. An underlying
condition was ischaemic heart disease of eight to ten years’ onset. As <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON> was to be
cremated, he completed Form B of the cremation certificate. He said that he had seen
Mrs Wagstaff on the day of her death. He claimed that a neighbour had been present at the
death, which was untrue.
Dr <PERSON>Alastair MacGillivray</PERSON> from the <ORGANIZATION>Brooke Surgery</ORGANIZATION> signed Form C, confirming that he was
satisfied that the death was due to coronary thrombosis. He said he relied totally on the
account given him by <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>. He would not expect any external signs if <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON> had
died of coronary thrombosis.
The Expert Evidence
Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON> said that <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON>’s records showed that she had been very healthy for
her age. There was no sign of heart disease. There were two entries showing raised blood
pressure but no real hypertension. The entry describing the death was very brief. It said:
‘ call 1500 arrive 1515 def ct collapse died 1520’.
Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> said that, if <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON> had collapsed in the way <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed, the doctor
should have called an ambulance immediately, as every effort should have been made to
resuscitate her. She was a very fit woman for her age. Resuscitation needs more than one
person.
Comment
This was a cremation case so there was no direct evidence that the cause of death was
morphine poisoning. Nonetheless, the jury convicted. The salient features of this case are:
• Mrs <PERSON>Wagstaff</PERSON> was in good health and her death was wholly unexpected.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> made an unsolicited visit to <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON>. His claim that she had summoned him
was a lie. He must have made his mind up in advance that he was going to call on her and
kill her.
• The death took place in <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s presence. Most doctors say that it is extremely unusual
for a patient to die suddenly during a home visit.
• On <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account of the sudden collapse, there was no excuse not to call an
ambulance and to make a proper attempt to resuscitate. No doctor would behave as he did
in the circumstances he described. 
APPENDIX E 289
• His two differing accounts both contained proven lies. In particular he had not telephoned
the ambulance station.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> chose to say that <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON> had died after a coronary thrombosis. However,
her medical records showed that she was not likely to suffer such a death. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> usually
chose a cause of death which would be plausible in the light of the medical history.
However, <PERSON>Mrs Wagstaff</PERSON> was not suffering from any potentially fatal condition.
290 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
<PERSON>Maureen Alice Ward</PERSON>
Introduction
Miss <PERSON>Maureen Alice Ward</PERSON> died on 18th February 1998 at the age of 57. Some time before her
death, she had suffered from cancer but she had been successfully treated. She had other
medical problems and was seeking early retirement on medical grounds.
Personal Background
At the time of her death, <PERSON>Miss Ward</PERSON> appeared to her friends to be in good health. For several
years, she had lived with her mother at <ORGANIZATION>Ogden Court</ORGANIZATION>, sheltered accommodation in <LOCATION>Hyde</LOCATION>, and
had stayed on after her mother’s death. She was about to leave <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> and was looking forward
to moving to <LOCATION>Southport</LOCATION>. Most of her belongings were packed. In addition, she had booked a
<LOCATION>Caribbean</LOCATION> holiday, starting on 1st March. In the afternoon of the day before the day of her
death, Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> attended <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s surgery to discuss her continuing need to take
tamoxifen, an anti-cancer drug. Mrs <PERSON>Carol Chapman</PERSON>, the receptionist, chatted with her.
Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> appeared to be in good health and spirits.
The Circumstances of the Death
On the morning of her death, Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> helped an elderly resident by carrying a heavy bag of
bedding across to the laundry. Then she went shopping in <PERSON>Hyde</PERSON> to buy a new dress for her
holiday. The residents of <ORGANIZATION>Ogden Court</ORGANIZATION> were astonished to learn that Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> had died later
that day. They would have been even more astonished had they known that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was to
certify her death as due to carcinomatosis, which is the expression used to describe cancer
which has become so generalised that it is not possible to specify which organs are affected.
Patients with carcinomatosis do not usually die suddenly.
At about 3.30pm on 18th February 1998, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> called upon Mrs <PERSON>Christine Simpson</PERSON>, the
warden of <ORGANIZATION>Ogden Court</ORGANIZATION>, and asked her to come to Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON>’s flat. He said he had just found
her dead. When <PERSON>Mrs Simpson</PERSON> expressed surprise, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said, ‘ Well she did have a brain
tumour you know’. Knowing that Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> was very strict about security, <PERSON>Mrs Simpson</PERSON> asked
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> how he had gained entry to Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON>’s flat. He said that Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> had been
expecting him and she had left the lock ‘ on the snip’. He said he was bringing her an
appointment letter for her to go to <LOCATION>Stepping Hill Hospital</LOCATION>. When <PERSON>Mrs Simpson</PERSON> reached the flat,
she found Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> lying on her bed, fully dressed in day clothes. Her eyes were closed and
she looked ‘ completely straight and tidy’. In the kitchen there were signs that Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> had
been interrupted while spooning some cat food onto the cat’s dish.
At about 4pm, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> returned to the surgery and told <PERSON>Mrs Chapman</PERSON> that Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> was
dead. He told her that he had been passing the corner of the street and had seen an
ambulance outside <LOCATION>Ogden Court</LOCATION>. He had called in and the paramedics had told him that
Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> was dead. The following day, <PERSON>Mrs Chapman</PERSON> heard <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> tell other members of
staff that he had called at Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON>’s home to give her an appointment. She claims to have
observed out loud that that was not what he had told her the day before. 
APPENDIX E 291
The Defence Case
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account in evidence, which was rejected by the jury, was that he had seen
Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> twice on the day before her death, not once as the records showed. At lunchtime,
he had noticed her in the reception area and she had asked to have a word with him. He took
her to his room and she told him she had been having some ‘ funny do’s’ with headaches and
blurred vision. These had settled and she had not gone to the optician. About a week earlier,
she had found herself on the floor. She had wet herself. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> thought it sounded as if she
might have had an epileptic fit, which can be a sign of a brain tumour. He had been unaware of
these symptoms until 17th February. When he learned of them, he inserted them into the
records at the dates at which Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> said these events had occurred. He thought this was a
proper way to amend the records and he had not intended that the records should be
misleading. They plainly were. When she told him of these events, he advised her that she must
see her consultant at <ORGANIZATION>Stepping Hill Hospital</ORGANIZATION>, Mr <PERSON>Peter England</PERSON>, as a matter of urgency. That
afternoon and the next morning he had made considerable efforts to get Miss Ward an early
appointment but had been unable to get through to the hospital. So he had written her a letter
of referral which she could take with her. He went round to give her this letter and to advise her
to attend the next clinic, but found her already dead.
Certification
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> signed a Medical Certificate of Cause of Death (MCCD), giving the cause of death as
carcinomatosis of eight weeks’ duration (with a secondary tumour in the brain). He said that
breast cancer had been an underlying condition. He stated that he had seen Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> alive
on the day of her death. As Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> was to be cremated, he completed a Form B repeating
that diagnosis but stating that he had last seen Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> alive 24 hours before her death.
He said that the mode of death was collapse of ‘ minutes’ duration. That information was based
on information from the warden who had found <LOCATION>Miss Ward</LOCATION> in a collapsed state. The warden had
been present at the moment of death. These statements were untrue.
Dr <PERSON>Susan Booth</PERSON> signed Form C, saying she too was satisfied that death was due to
carcinomatosis. Any enquiry of anyone who knew Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> (such as the warden) would have
shown this to be false and would have revealed the falsity of the claim on Form B.
The Medical Records
Examination of the medical records showed that several false entries had been made on the
day of the death. Some had been made before the body was discovered, some afterwards.
Some entries were designed to show that, during the previous two months, Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> had
exhibited signs of a brain tumour. On 17th February, a genuine contemporaneous record was
made of the consultation about the long-term need to take tamoxifen. At about 2.45pm, on
18th February, a series of three backdated entries was made, purporting to have been made on
17th December 1997 and 6th and 17th February 1998. These entries included references to
headaches and blurred vision. The headaches had been accompanied by nausea and
unsteadiness in the legs. On 17th February, examination had supposedly shown raised
intracranial pressure. Another entry, made at about 5.45pm on 18th February, suggests that
Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> had told <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> that, about six days earlier, she had found herself on the floor and
292 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
she had wet herself. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> wondered whether she had had an epileptic fit. These entries
were clearly designed to lend credibility to <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s explanation for Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON>’s death.
Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON> said that, without the false entries, there was nothing to support the diagnosis
of carcinomatosis or brain tumour. In any case, a death from those causes is a slow process in
which the patient develops neurological signs and suffers a gradual reduction in
consciousness.
The remaining entries made on 18th February were all made at about 5.45pm, that is after Miss
<PERSON>Ward</PERSON> was known to be dead. One says that Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> was dead on examination. Another says
that she had been referred for further care and was to have an urgent appointment with
Mr <LOCATION>England</LOCATION>. She had been seen in her own home. These entries were designed to support
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account of his reason for visiting Miss Ward at home on the occasion when he
supposedly found her dead.
The doctors responsible for her supervision at <LOCATION>Stepping Hill Hospital</LOCATION> reported that Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON>
had attended for periodic review and had made no complaint of any symptom referable to a
brain tumour.
Comment
Although this was a cremation case, with no direct evidence of morphine poisoning, it was a
very clear case of murder:
• This was a sudden death discovered very shortly after <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> had had access to
Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> alone and the opportunity to inject diamorphine.
• The cause of death was not plausible given the past medical history. Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> was not
terminally ill. Even with heavily altered records, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s explanation for the death did not
make medical sense.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave a false account of events on Form B where his entries were plainly designed
to distance him from the death.
• Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON>’s appearance was not what one would expect if Miss <PERSON>Ward</PERSON> had had a sudden
collapse. On the other hand her appearance was consistent with her having been given an
injection of diamorphine.
APPENDIX E 293
Maria West
Introduction
Mrs <PERSON>Maria West</PERSON> (also known as <PERSON>Marie</PERSON>) died on 6th March 1995 at the age of 81. She was a
widow who usually lived alone. At the time of her death, her son had been staying with her for a
few days. He said she seemed in reasonably good health and was fairly fit for her age. In
recent weeks, she had begun to suffer pain from arthritis in her legs, hips and back, for which
she took anti-inflammatory drugs. She also had poor circulation in the legs. She had recently
started to use an inhaler for asthma. Despite these problems, she was still leading a busy
social life and was about to book a Butlins holiday. She had not complained to her son of
blurred vision or dizziness or numbness of the hand.
The Circumstances of the Death
On 27th February and 2nd March 1995, <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> had consulted <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> about her arthritis. He
had prescribed pethidine tablets but these had not agreed with her and she had asked him to
visit her on 6th March. Her good friend, <PERSON>Mrs Marion Hadfield</PERSON>, visited her on that day. Mrs West
made cups of tea and carried them into the living room. Then the two women chatted, watched
television together and waited for the doctor to arrive. <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> said that <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> was
quite well during this time. She did not have any trouble with blurred vision and she did not
complain of dizziness or numbness of the hand.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> arrived while <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> was upstairs using the bathroom. When she came down,
she could hear voices coming from the living room, so she remained in the kitchen so as not to
intrude. After a while, the voices ceased. Then <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> came into the kitchen. He seemed
surprised to see her and said he was going to see if <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON>’s son was upstairs as <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON>
had ‘ collapsed’ on him. <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> asked if he could do anything for her. He said it was too
late; she had ‘ gone’. <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> went into the living room and found <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> sitting in the
chair exactly where she had been before. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> did not attempt resuscitation. He just raised
<PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON>’s eyelid and said there was no sign of life. He told <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> that he had just
turned away to pack his bag and, when he turned round, he found that <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> had
collapsed.
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> telephoned <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON>’s son, <PERSON>Christopher</PERSON>, and told him that he had taken his mother’s
blood pressure and had gone out to put the machine in the car. When he came back in, he
found her dead. When <PERSON>Mr West</PERSON> arrived, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> told him that his mother had had a massive
stroke. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he had expected her to die as she had hardening of the arteries but
had not expected it to happen so soon. He had not tried to resuscitate her, as she would have
been a vegetable if she had been brought round. He did not suggest a post-mortem
examination.
The Defence Case
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account, the essential aspects of which the jury must have rejected, was that he had
visited Mrs West on 6th March to see how her back pain had been since she had been taking
pethidine. If the pain were not resolving, she would have to be referred to a consultant.
<PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> told <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> that the back pain had improved but she now had another problem.
She had noticed altered vision and weakness in the arm and leg, lasting for an hour or so at a
294 APPENDIX E
The Shipman Inquiry
time. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> turned away to reach into his bag for his stethoscope and blood pressure
machine. He spoke to her but there was no response. When he looked at her, she was slumped
in her chair. He examined her and found that she was dead. He did not attempt resuscitation
as in his opinion, if revived, she would have been severely disabled. He found the son’s
telephone number and went into the kitchen to look for the telephone. (In fact the telephone
was in the living room.) In the kitchen, he found <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> and told her that <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> had
collapsed and died. When <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> asked if he was sure that <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> was dead, he
tested the carotid pulse again and proved the point by showing <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> the dilated pupils.
He telephoned <PERSON>Mr West</PERSON> and told him of the death. He did not say that <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> had collapsed
while he was outside collecting his bag. When <PERSON>Mr West</PERSON> arrived, he explained to him how the
death had occurred. He mentioned the possibility of a post-mortem examination but Mr West
did not want one.
After the death, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> made an entry in the medical records in which he stated that
<PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> had complained of blurring of vision and weakness in the arm. Strangely, he did not
mention that she had died in the middle of the examination. The Crown suggested that this
record was a fabrication, designed to add plausibility to his false account of her death. As the
jury convicted, one must infer that they found that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> must have given Mrs West an
injection of diamorphine soon after his arrival.
Certification
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> completed the Medical Certificate of Cause of Death (MCCD), saying that the cause
of death was a cerebrovascular accident of only a few minutes’ duration. A contributory cause
was arteriosclerosis of 18 years’ duration.
When completing cremation Form B, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> said that he had been present at the death with
a neighbour. He said he had carried out a complete external examination. He felt no doubt as
to the cause of death and there was no reason for further examination of the body.
The Expert Evidence
Dr <PERSON>John Grenville</PERSON>, who had examined the records, said that for many years Mrs West had been
at risk of having a stroke. She had had high blood pressure on two occasions in 1990 but this
had been well controlled since then. She had also had a suspicion of carotid bruit and a
fleeting episode of blindness. The records also revealed chronic bronchitis and asthma but
these were not relevant to the death. The records for the week before her death showed that
<PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> was prescribed pethidine for hip pain. The only possible reference to a vascular
problem was an entry for 4th March which said:
‘ pethidine for pain, <ORGANIZATION>TCI</ORGANIZATION>’,
which could have meant ‘ to come in’ or, as <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> claimed in evidence, ‘ transitory cerebral
ischaemia’.
Dr <PERSON>Grenville</PERSON> said that <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account of <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON>’s sudden transition from life to death did
not ring true. Death did not occur in that way. If <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s account of the collapse were true,
he should have attempted resuscitation and called an ambulance. Anything less was not
acceptable as <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON> was not terminally ill. Further, he said that the history recorded by
APPENDIX E 295
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> of a complaint of blurred vision and weakness in the arm sounded more like the
symptoms of a mild evolving stroke than the kind of severe stroke that can cause a sudden
cerebrovascular death. The cause of death given on the MCCD could not be justified and the
case should have been reported to the coroner.
Comment
This was a cremation case with no direct evidence of morphine in the body tissues. There are
a number of significant features about this case, all of which are common to other cases:
• The death took place in Mrs West’s home and while <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> was alone with her. This is
a highly suspicious factor, as other general practitioners say that this is an extremely rare
event.
• Death was extremely sudden, so sudden that it could not have happened as <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>
claimed.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> made no attempt to resuscitate the patient and justified his decision by claiming
that she would have been severely disabled if revived.
• <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON>’s appearance in death, sitting peacefully in a chair as though asleep, was similar
to the appearance of many of <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON>’s other victims.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> gave inconsistent accounts of the circumstances to different people. However, it
must be borne in mind that the accounts of friends and relatives may not be wholly reliable
due to the passage of time and the effect of shock and distress.
• There was a dispute as to whether <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> mentioned the possibility of a post-mortem
examination. The relative said he did not. <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> made no report to the coroner in a case
in which such a report was plainly called for, as the death was sudden and unexplained.
• To explain <PERSON>Mrs West</PERSON>’s death, <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> selected a moderately plausible cause, in that there
were features of her medical history which put her at risk of a stroke.
• <PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> wrote on cremation Form B that a neighbour was present at the death. Although
she was in the house, <PERSON>Mrs Hadfield</PERSON> was not in the room at the moment of death and the
statement that she was present is misleading. This is a typical example of the way in which
<PERSON>Shipman</PERSON> would make false statements, seeking to distance himself from the death or to
make it appear he had not been alone with the patient at the time of death.
